THE RED PAGE.
Last night, that seemed a rather satisfactory bit
of sen-tiuient : this morning it looks somewhat
thin and waily. But it may go as an expression
of a moo 1, acknowledging two echoes —
One from Henley’s
Crosses and. troubles a-many havp proved me.
One or two women (God bless them !) have loved me.
I have worked and dreamed, and I’ve talked at will.
.Of art and drink I have had my fill.
I’ve comforted here, I’ve suer ured there.
I’ve faced my foes, and I’ve backed my friends.
I've blundered, and sometimes m >de amends.
I have prayed for light, and I’ve known despair.
Now I look hefore, as I look behind,
Cotne storm, come shine, whatever befall.
With a grateful heart, and a constant mind,
For the end I know is the best of all.
and the other from an uifset brilliant of Will Ogilvie’s,
‘called ‘My Lover, the Bush,’ which ends—
The winds of Dawn are moving,
The river oaks astir . . .
What heart were lorn of loving
That had no Love but her?
Till last red stars are lighted
(Ogilvie is very fond of ‘ red stars ’)
And last winds wander West,
Her fait.h and mine are plighted—
The Lover I love best!
though pro-
bably the not-very-recondite ideas would have
been suggested anyway. However, it were
ungracious to send Burns to Parnassus by
the steep and thorny way of strict originality
while dallying one’s-self along another fellow’s
primrose path.
* * * *
The mood brought itself while I was thinking
over George Black’s now historic apology for the
legendary song-Burns. .Judging by Scottish cor-
respondents’comments, the champion’s affirma-
tion of the essential originality of Burns has been
especially justified by his numerous admissions of
unoriginality ; and the slightly inconsequent affir-
mation that the average Scot knew all about the
unoriginality of this original poet has been deci-
sively corroborated by the statement that, out
of some 150 editions of Bnrus, only “at least
one-third ’’ contained notes to show where his un-
originality came in. Two Scottish commentators
in the score or so are disposed to admit that
there may be a liere-aud-there spot which the
whitewash does not cover, and these urge the
familiar plea that the spot is 4 a very little one’—
- reiterating, in fact, the national explanation that
We are ns fou, we’re nae that fou,
But just a drappie in our e’e!
‘And if Robbie did help himself to a wee
verse now and then,’ asks one, ‘is it for that
you would call him plagiarist?’ This was all
expected, for no quill oan pierce the doubled
armour of patriotism and prejudice. The profit of
paper-staining was looked for in non-Caledonian
skulls. And, lest there should remain the slightest
chance of misapprehension, it seemed desirable to
criticise the critic in as much detail as space
would permit. So I commenced —
If George Black, M.P., does not get every Caledonian
vote in Gipps electorate at the coming contest, then
there is no gratitude in Scotchmen. To shield the
national idol’s clay feet he has exposed his
own body. A beautiful 8, eoiinen of Henley’s ‘ Common
Bunisite ’ . . .
* * * *
I had written thus far when my pen flagged for
a moment, and I came out on my little mountain
verandah under the sky and fell to thinking. I
saw my commentary quite clearly. I meant to
appeal a-priori, and ask was not I, an Australian
free from the fetish-influence, more likely to
judge Burns impartially from the standpoint
of .Literature than was G. 8., biased by Scots
tradition and training. Then I meant to
quote some of G.B.’s own ponderous, uninspired
verses, and to ask whether a man who wrote
like that 'could possibly be a judge of what
was or was not poetry Then I would point out
how the Common Burnsite cult caused nausea by
its extravagant pretensions regarding its divinity,
and how eminently desirable it was that the bubble
should be'pricked and some of the patriotic gas
let out. And 1 would add that the real gold in
Burns did not need this continual gilding, and
that were Burns alive he would be first to protest.
Then I had it in mind to expand a previous hint,
and to ask what credit a man would have
to-day who vamped his work like the
s >ng-Burns did ; and what would he said
of the perfervid enthusiasts who today kept
on extolling their hero for achievements
which he did not perform and for qualities which
he did not possess. Then I intended to review
my reviewer in as much detail as possible, and to
point out how he misrepresented some things
through carelessness
As when, in defending ‘Landlady, count the lawin’,’
he parallels a vamped verse, not with the original it was
vamped from, but with another verse which it obviously
was not vamped from, and which nobody ever said or
hinted it was vamped from.
and how he misrepresented
other things through dullness;
As when he failed to see that a race may be fairly
described as unpoetical to-day without affecting the dic-
tum that it produced poets in the last century—just as
in the Byzantine period the Greek race was unpoetioal
despite Sappho and Homer.
and how, generally
speaking, his apparent deficiency in poetical sym-
pathy, and an apparent mental nearsightedness
which attaches undue importance to the letter
which killeth, and not due importance to the spirit
which giveth life, caused him to leave his pro-
tege, on the whole, in worse case than he
found him in. I had no doubt of clearing away
the larger ddbris cast on my thesis—and contro-
versy over details is interminable.
» * * *
Yet, somehow, under the sky, I did not feel so
satisfied with the programme. It was not that it
was not a good programme, yet it did not seem to
me the best. Polemics have their uses ; satire and
invective are necessary to pierce thick hides and
dull intelligences ; and warmth is not truthless
because it tends to wax untruthful. But the night
was so calm, so clear, so bright. The day, like
Agag, had walked delicately to death, and hardly
a ripple of sound flowed over the stillness. Only
far down the descent a train muttered with gnaw-
ing persistence, and across the garden-path a single
frog boomed softly.
Pity that the really poetic frog has such an unpoetic
name—even the splendidly onomatopceio p uddock of G.B.’s
vernacular fails to do justice to his chorus—much less to
his solo. One would like something long-vowelled and
slow and musicianly—Dvorak might do—sounded guttu-
rally, Dvawrahk! Dvawrahk !
And Goethe’s rest was on
all the peaks ; the tree-ragged ridges lay along
sombrely, brooding and brooding. Away above,
the half-moon tossed through a smother of cirri
which hunted her like a pack of lap-dogs, rolling
over and over to be shaken off with an almost
perceptible indignation, and continually re-leaping
upon her. In the moon’s wake one dark star
sailed steadily. And everything was so hushed
that you leant to catch with a sense finer than
hearing the murmur of something more delicate
than sound—strained to see an invisible motion
which seemed to thrill through the motionless
depths of obscurity ’: maybe—who knows?—it was
the bosom of Mother Space lifting with the
breathing of a sleeping Universe. “ In ooelo
quies,” they say—“ in coelo quies ! ”
* * » *
And I thought of George Black—of his past,
and his present, and his future, and of the zeal
with which he, like the rest of us, frets and fumes
over his little fancies, and vehemently tries to
impress upon the world as the whole face of Truth
the single facet which he sees. I thought of his
life as it had been told to me —of the stolid boy
imbibing his Burns in the fair town of Edinbro,
where all the lawyers in embryo and all the law-
yers matured swarm through the streets in the
gullies and the streets on the ridges, or sit on the
doorsteps to dangle good Scotch feet in the attic
windows of the neighbors underneath. I saw
him coming as ‘colonial-experienoer’ to Australia,
drifting through—no, not drifting fighting
through the cycle of bush life with more
pluck than pence ; taking to his pen and labo-
riously improving his prose—yea, even his verse,
as far as it lay in him ; —I saw him canvassing,
framing pictures, cleaning boots at an hotel; I
heard him lecturing at the Cyclorama, to
an audience of nine country - folk and three
hens employed on board-wages to make the
foreground realistic : and all the while he
was plodding on with his eyes open for
advancement, never quitting the engrained faith
of every Scot that the world is his oyster. Then
I remembered his press-work ; his advocacy of
Socialism —heavy indeed, but impregnated with
an honest desire to help to make a better earth
for better men ; I remembered his dull, laborious
speeches, shining with an earnest intention
to impress upon his hearers such ideals as
he saw ; I remembered instances of his
ability, of his generosity, and many ex-
pressions of his belief that he was the born and
obvious leader of N.S.W. Labor-party (“ was ”
for I doubt he believes it now). And I recalled
the episode of. the journey to Melbourne, and of
the Strange Woman ; I recalled also somebody’s
statement of his most affectionate and devoted'
sonship (construing into.his own language J. M.
Barrie’s ‘ Margaret Ogilvy ’). I thought of his
male and sturdy Scbtclmess (the dogged character
which I admire, despite its narrow egotism) ; and
gradually I conjured up such a picture of him that
I thought with Kenehn Chillingly in his fight
with Tom Bowles—‘ he has a handsome face ;
’t would be a shame to spoil it.’
Then I dreamed a little, and smoked the last
cigar, and so to bed.
And what of Burns ? I fear Burns must wait—
he was so far away from my mood —so far away.
• * * *
P.S. —This comes of living on the mountains.
Had I stayed in the city. Black would have been
blasted to a certainty. But ‘from the heights,’
as John Boyle O’Reilly sings—
P.P.S. —I wonder if an argument for reducing
the price of season tickets—effective to the Rail-
way Commissioners could be based on the
demonstration that the ideals of our nation would
be raised and its charity widened by daily moun-
taimmigration en masse? If George Black will
in his parliamentary capacity introduce a deputa-
tion to Mr. Oliver, I shall be happy to attend and
read this article.

Writes ‘ Tom Collins ’ —
Re Burns. In confining comment and discussion to the
work, more or less original, of the Scottish Minstrel, a
constructive injustice has been done to the Universal
Poet. If ‘ the poor man,’as he speaks of Burns, ‘always
holds up his head, and regards you with an elated look’
(Prof. Wilson), it is not because he believes his national
bard to be the sole author of a couple of hundred songs,
chiefly amatory or convivial ; there is no great cause for
elation here. But the Scot regards Burns not only with
pride and affection, but with reverence ; and reverence is
not to be won by the reputed authorship of ‘ We’re a’
Noddin’,’ ‘ Hey, the Dusty Miller,’ and other such homely
ditties. Scarcely even by an occasional patriotic hurst,
like ‘Scots Wha Hae,’ or a dozen heart-uiteranoes,
like ‘ Highland Mary.’ It is not because of such
work that Burns has chief honor in raising the
Lowland dialect to the dignity of a language. And
it is neither love for that dialect nor admiration for
‘The Birks of Aberfeldy ’ and ‘Landlady, Count the
Lawin’,’ which gives Burns a front place in the estima-
tion of millions who neither know nor care what the
‘ birks ’ or the ‘ lawin ’ may be. But these ohansons
are not, Burns.
The Burns whom we appreciate and honor lies beyond
the Scbl tish Minstrel. George Black claims for his hero
* strength, directness, vividness, easy certainty, passion,
wit, pathos, and lightness of touch.’ Indisputable ;
but these qualities are reinforced by another, and that
the rarest and most preoious ever bestowed upon, or cul-
tivated by, man. This is the- quality of Moral Sense
-the power w'hioh enables its possessor to see through
traditional usage, early training, and current or-
thodoxy ; perceiving and recognising the, primary
truth thereby hidden from ids contemporaiii s.
Herein—hacked by courage and iritcgriiv—lies the special
endowment of all proph ts, from Is dab to EpicL tues,
from Moses to Marx. Moral Sense paradoxically a
mental quality—is the prophet’s diploma. The clear-
sighted Greeks made ‘prophet’ and ‘poet’ synonymous.
And on this impregnable foundation rests Burns’s title 10
the name of poet. The minstrel is good in his place ; the
poet is better, and the poet’s place is coextensive with
human habitation.
The English, American, or Australian democratic orator
can coin no such pointed expression of his own protest
attaint legal injustice as lie finds furnished to his
hand, in twenty d fferent forms, by. tins century-old poet.
The swagman, looking over Ins last ‘swapping-boob,’
which happens to be Burns’s poems, untie. s several pages
dirtier than the. average (the double-meaning is yours,
not mine), and there finds his own incoherent challenge
voiced with a ole mess and ferce which give value to its
worth. Whereupon he ‘ holds up his head and regards
you with an elated look,’ though his name is Dennis
O’To le, and his late father was lagged, from Bailyhooley
for cutting a fishing rod out of a young plantation. Still,
it appears, Burns’s latest editor persists in viewing him
as‘the most exquisite artist in folk-song the world has
ever seen.’ Also, an ‘artist’ outside the Vernacular.
This is ‘Sweetest Shatespere, Fancy’s child,’ over again.
Given the opportunities afforded by Labor-movements of
the pr< se't day, Burns was the sort of artist who would
be promptly jail-d by very many of his present senti-
mental admin r«.
And thougti professors of religion have so dragged that
noble w ord in’the dirt that one doesn’t like to introduce
it without saving ‘ saving yonr presence,’ it is impossible
to close these remarks without noticing the invaluable
deodorant contributed to Christianity in such work as
‘Holy Willie’s Prayer,’ the ‘Address to the Unco Guid,”
‘ The Kirk’s Alarm,’ &c. The poet’s Moral S use shines
nowhere brighter or steadier than in his life-long battle
with the orthodoxy of his day. Now name another Eng-
lish-speaking citizen who, single-handed, has successfully
gibbeted abuses of such solidity and sanctity, or who has
indicated vital truths so far in advance of his own time ?
This is the province of the Poet proper; this was the
bard’s special commission from Coila, the muse—
Preserve the Dignity of Man
With soul erect;
And trust the Universal Plan
Will all protect.
No one charges clean-steal, vamp, plagiarism, or adapta-
tion along this line. And this is Burns.
Writeß J. A.P. :
Victor Daley, writing on ‘Girls’ Song Games,’ asks,
‘ Shall we ever have an Australian child-literature of song
and story that will supersede the old i ursery favorites,
with their northern color and northern identity? Not
for five hundred years, I think ! ’ Wrong 1 My little
girl (attending a Sydney Slate-school) and her school-
mates trill out this as a game accompaniment:
‘ Johnny and Jane and Jack and Lou,
Butler’s Stairs through Woolloomooloo;
Woolloomooloo, and ’cross the Domain,
Round the Block, and home again 1
Heigh, ho ! tipsy toe,
Give us a kiss, and away we go.’
How’s that?
Writes ‘Vargo’:
Byron (by no means squeamish) on Robert Burns:
‘ Allen has lent me a quantity Of Burns’s unpublished,
and never-to-be-published, letters. They are full of oaths
and obscene songs. What an antithetical mind I—tender-
ness, roughness—delicacy, coarseness—sentiment, sen-
suality-soaring and grovelling, dirtandDeity—all mixed
up in that one compound of inspired clay !’
I have known men, and been friends with some.
And some there have been strong friends to me*
But at times and seasons one tires.of all—
Grieving to tire - for new instincts call,
And new thoughts part us more than a sea:
When you turn for solace, their lips are dumb.
I’ve known many women, and loved a few,
And a few have been kind and have loved me well;
But there always was something unexpressed
W hich none of them knew, or glimpsed, or guessed ;
There is always something you cannot tell
However closely they strain to you.
So when weary of errors and misconcep' ions
I go where the Bush fronts grand to the sky ;
And my small, weak life is merged in tin Whole,
And the sou! of rue’s lost in the general S ul.
And bland airs blow from this hot, tired I
All its worries, and griefs, and pigmy deceptions.
And I lie at rest in the sparse short grasses,
With Space poured round me and Time annulled.
And watch through the flickering trees’ embrasures
Dim greys glide into infinite azures:
In Silence’s lap I’m cradled and lulled,
And vagroni Sound croons peace as she parses.
And the Bush bends closer and holds my hands,
And her noons or her nights yearn low to listen
When I tell her all in her inmost shrine,
And receive absolution for follies o’ mine ;
Her mouth may smile, but her grave eyesglisten
She is my Lo er, and under-lands
'Come to me for wisdom,’ said the mountain ;■
‘ln the valley and the plain
There is Kc owledge dimmed with sorrow in the gait
There is Effort, with its Hope like a fountain ;
There, the chained rebel, Passion ;
Laboring Strength and Fleet ing Fashion )
There, Ambition’s leaping flame,
And the iris-erown of Fame ;
But those gains are dear, forever
Won from loss and pain and fever.
Nature’s gospel never changes;
Every sudden force deranges!
Blind endeavour is not, wise ;
Wisdom enters through the eyes;
And the seer is the knower,
Is the doer and the sower.
‘Come to me for riches,’ said the peak;
‘ I am leafless, cold and calm ;
But the treasures of the lily and the palm—
They are mine to bestow on those who seek ;
I am gift and I am giver
To the verdured fields below,
As the motherhood of snow
Daily gives the new-born river.
As a watcher on a tower,
Listening to the evening hour,
Sees the wandering currents end
Where the moveless waters shine
On the far horizon-line—
All the storied Past is mine ;
All its strange beliefs still clinging;
All its singers and their singing;
All the paths that led away,
All the meteors onoe called Day ;
All the stars that rose to shine—
Come to me—for all are mine 1
‘Come to me for safety,’ said the heights;
‘ln the future as the past,
Road and river end at last
Like a raindrop in the ever-circling sea.
Who shall know by lessened sight
Where the gain and where the loss
In the desert they must cross ?
Guides who lead their charge from ills,
Passing soon from town to town,
Through the forest and the down,
Take direction from the hills.
Those who range a wider land,
Higher climb until they stand
Where the past and future swing
Like a far blue ocean-ring.
Those who sail from lands afar
Leap from mountain-top to star ;
Higher still, from star to God,
Have the spirit-pilots trod,
Setting lights for mind and Soul
That the ships may reach the goal.
‘ They shall safely steer who see ;
Sight is wisdom. Come to me ! ’

Various Verses.
The alleged Schenk discovery is being
taken very seriously in Austria, where many
estates are in danger for want of male heirs. One
special case in which Schenk claims by his treat
ment to have retained an enormous property in
the family is considered to be that of the Arch
duke Frederick, whose -£10,000,000 were in dangei
until, after bearing eight successive daughters,
the Archduchess, in her 41st year, produced a
son and saved the shekels.
THE MOTHER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Lo ! My little child was dead.
Sad, yet calm, the salt tears fell;
For I knew the God who smote
Loved him ; with my child ’twas well.
In the grave they laid him low;
But my eyes were raised to Heaven,
And I smiled to know him there,
With the sinless and forgiven.
Then the cruel rain came down,
Beat upon the little sod,
Did not spare my child at all— ■
That was why I turned from God.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
HIS ONLY IDYLL.
Lived a life of barren torpor, capped by common-
place success;
Died respected, and was standing at the door to
blessedness.
And he knocked, whereat the angel, who was
guardian of the gate,
Quickly came to look him over ere pronouncing on
his fate.
“ You may enter, but,” he thundered, “in your
luggage here I see
You are smuggling into Heaven contraband of
memory ! ”
Then the guilty, white-haired mortal humbly,
with a wistful face,
Pleaded for a seat in heaven, pleaded for a quiet
place:
“ I have left my goods and chattels to the poor
and lame and blind ;
Left my hope and my ambition, even left my
name behind.
What I was on yonder planet, how I fought and
won at last,
Is forgotten and has vanished in the shadows of
_ the past;
Pain and pleasure, pride and glory, none has fol-
lowed here above,
Only this, a faint remembrance of the fleeting
touch of love.
Leave me then a tiny token of a moment’s earthly
bliss,
Just the ghost of what has happened, of a May
day and a kiss ! ”
But the angel would not listen : “ Holy is the joy
within ;
Do not hope to pass the portal in the company of
sin.
She, who once in godless passion made herself
your very own,
Has repented, long repented, and is singing at the
Throne.
She is here, and clad in Virtue—you shall meet her,
good and pure ;
Come, forget and be forgiven ; enter, and the
Lord will cure.”
But the mortal.stood and pondered, then he sadly
turned away :
“ No ; rriy paradise is outside, with that memory
of May.
I am happy in my dreamland, short romance of
joy and pain,
It would only spoil the vision if I met the girl
again. '
She is doubtless sweet and holy, but my dream is
sweeter, sir,
And I meant to keep it living, or—l would have
married her ! ”
VON KOTZE.
LOVERS AND LIVERS.
The heart, through countless ages
Of love-dreams bright and youthful,
On the evidence of sages
Who were honestly untruthful,
Has been credited with forces
Always recognised as mighty
Both in marriage, and divorces,
’Twixt the virtuous, or flighty.
Edwin buys a heart-shaped token
To adorn his Angy’s bosom ;
And we say that hearts are broken
When we jilt our girls, or lose ’em ;
But, a truce to foolish chatter
Touching shafts from Cupid’s quiver—
Love is just a little matter
That depends upon the liver.
A more than common liking,
An attachment strong and chronic.
Oft is brought about by biking,
Or a dose of patent tonic.
Most courtships have been noted
For inexplicable pauses,
And couples, quite devoted,
“ Break it off ” from hidden causes,
And delight in their dejection,
Reaping sorrow where they sow it;
But the main-spring of affection
Isn’t mentioned by the poet.
Though a heart go pitter-patter,
Though a soul may seem to shiver,
Love is just a little matter
That depends upon the liver.
The golden heart-shaped locket
Is a sentimental fiction,
And its price, in Angy’s pocket,
Would impart as much conviction
That her suitor speaketh truly
When he vows to love her ever;
Yet the tongue is so unruly
One can never trust it, never 1
Edwin’s talk, at times delightful,
Is more often supercilious,
Whilst Angy’s temper’s frightful
If she happen to be bilious.
They may fight, or smile and flatter,
As they stroll beside the river—
Love is just a little matter
That depends upon the liver.
EDMUND EISHER.

PUTTING THIS AND THAT
TOGETHER.
These two items, from a Sydney morning
paper, read rather funnily
The Wesleyan Conference yesterday passed
a resolution urging all Methodist people to
observe the Sabbath, and to abstain from un-
necessary travelling on that day.”
It has been noticed during the last few weeks
that the majority of thefts from dwellings which
have been reported to the police have been perpe-
trated on Sundays. The thieves appear to have
taken advantage of the attendance of the occu-
pants of the houses visited at church to commit
their depredations.”

SATURDAY, MARCH 12, 1898.
The Industrial Endowment Delusion.
There are in Australia three offices which
make a special feature of Industrial Life As-
surance. This is the kind which is brought
round retail to the purchaser’s back door
instead of being sold wholesale at the office.
The companies which go in for it have an
immense number of clients, who pay from
Id. up to Is. or more per week for a micro-
scopic benefit payable in the very far here-
after—if nothing goes wrong meanwhile.
The size of the business may be guessed at
from the fact that one of the three—the Citi-
zens’ co. of Sydney—claims to have issued
nearly 79,000 new policies last year. The
plea always advanced on behalf of the Indus-
trial business is that it. makes assurance
possible for the poorest class of the
community, which could never save enough
to pay a quarterly or half-yearly or annual
premium, but which contrives somehow or
other to stint itself of the few coppers that
are required every week to keep the In-
dustrial racket going. This is one way of
putting it ; the other way is that the In-
dustrial business is a handy way of preying
on the poorest and most ignorant class which
doesn’t know any better.
* * * *
It is a hideously expensive process to begin
with. The Industrial Assurance concern
hires dozens of persons to tramp over the
cities and towns of Australasia all day and
every day collecting pennies and small silver
subscriptions. Naturally the salaries of all
these persons eat up an enormous propor-
tion of the income. The Citizens co., last
year, spent more than half—a good deal
more than half—of its industrial premiums
in expenses. The Australasian Temper-
ance and General Society, last year,
made a rather unusual effort to boom
its business, and got, a considerable
amount of new industrial assurance, and
spent £14,700 in expenses alone to
get in £ll,BOO in premiums. In other words,
all the premiums and a lot more besides went
in expenses. This sort of thing, of course,
doesn’t happen with the Temperance and
General as a regular thing, but the fact that
it can happen even once is sufficiently awful.
A third office in the same line of business —
the Provident and Industrial co. of Maori-
land—last year spent, so far as appears from
the rather confused accounts, all its premiums
in expenses. In fact, this truly dreadful
concern seems regularly to spend, in claims
and expenses, more than its entire premiums,
and its state looks very like one of utter in-
solvency. But, apart from any question of
insolvency, the huge expense-rate naturally
makes it necessary to give a miserably small
amount of life assurance for the money, and
makes the Industrial business something very
much akin to robbery without violence.
♦ * * *
The writer has before him the Industrial
Endowment rates of the two more respect-
able offices—the Citizens’andthe Australasian
Temperance and General. In each case a
child of five may be insured, at 6d. a week,
for £24, payable at age 21. This means pay-
ing 6d. a week, or £1 6s. per annum, for 16
years. If the same amount was deposited in
the savings bank, even at only 3 per cent,
interest, and with vastly better security, it
would amount, in the same number of years,
to about £26. The two cases are exactly
parallel, because the industrial policy is not
(like the endowment policies issued by ordi-
nary life offices) payable at age 21, or at
death, if the insured dies before 21. The in-
dustrial co. simply says that, if the 6d. a
week is paid regularly for 16 years, and the
company keeps afloat and remains solvent,
and if the person whose name appears on
the policy doesn’t die in the interval, it will
pay £24. The Commissioners’ Savings Bank
gives much better security, that, at the end
of the same period, for the same payment, it
will return about £26, no matter who dies in
the interval.
♦ * * •
Supposing, however, the insured dies
before the 16 years have expired, the insur-
ance is not payable, but these two companies
agree to return the premiums they have
received, without interest. Say the person
had been insured for 15 years at 6d. a week,
the co. would return £l9 10s. If the same
6d. a week had been deposited in the
Savings Bank for 15 years that institution
would return about £24 ; so the industrial
assurance racket gives less money and less
security as well. Also, there are other draw-
backs about the office which collects the
small coin irregularly at the back door. It
is a general condition that if the 6d. a week
is a certain number of weeks in arrear the
policy is lapsed, and the co. can repudiate
all liability and forfeit all the money already
paid. It is always liable to happen that the
payments are in arrear simply because the
collector hasn’t arrived up to time, and prob-
ably many persons are really unaware
whether their policies are in force or not at
any given date. Numbers of people prob-
ably pay in a spasmodic fashion, and
their policies are in a state of lapse, and
their claims on the society are nil, a great
part of the time. If the same individual put
his small subscription in the Savings Bank,
that institution never dreams of forfeiting
the amount already deposited because the
new deposits are not made at any regular
date.
• * * *
In either the Citizens’ or the Temperance
and General offices the endowment insurer,
if he ceases his payments after, say, years,
has no claim for the return of anything.
The office reserves the right to simply
annex his £4 11s. y whereas the Savings Bank
would hand it back with interest. If he has
been insured for four complete years, how-
ever, and is unable to pay any more, the
Industrial Assurance office, in return for his
£5 45., will give him, not his money back,
but a policy for £6, payable in 12 years—if
the assured is still alive then. If the person
assured dies in the interval, the £5 4s. is
apparently returned on death. The Savings
Bank would give about £7 10s. at the end of
12 years, or it would return the money im-
mediately if requested. Also, it gives vastly
better security for the payment of its £7los.
than any mere private institution can give
for the £6.
* * • •
The Provident and Industrial of Maoriland
goes on a slightly different plan. If a child
of five is insured under the endowment plan
at 6d. per week, this co. agrees to pay at
age 21—or 16 years from the date of the
policy—£26 ss. The Savings Bank, even if
it gave only 3 per cent, interest, would pan
out for about the same amount—the differ-
ence being that in one case the payment is
guaranteed by one of the biggest and most
solvent and most carefully supervised public
institutions in the Seven Provinces, and in
the other by about the smallest and most
utterly hopeless-looking private assurance
concern in all Australasia. If the person
assured dies before the 16 years are expired
the Provident and Industrial has the magni-
ficent effrontery to return only three-four hs of
the premiums without interest. In other words,
if the 6d. a week has been paid regularly
for 14 years, and the co. has received £lB 4s.
it will hand back £l3 13s. The Savings
Bank, if it had received the same amount by
like instalments, would hand back over £22,
including interest. And. in addition to this,
there is the further risk attached to the
insurance co. that the policy is liable to
lapse at any time and all the money paid to
be forfeited, merely through an accidental
neglect to pay one or two premiums.
If the insured, after paying for, perhaps,
three years eleven months, can’t keep up
the instalments, all the money paid is for-
feited. The co. could often, if it pleased, bring
about forfeiture when the time of payment
was nearly due, by the simple process of
ceasing to call for the money until the pay-
ments had got far enough in arrear to make
the policy invalid —it is expressly stipulated
by the Provident and Industrial that “in
no case will omission to call he allowed as an
excuse for non-payment of the premiums at
the proper time.” Also, in the case of
children’s endowments, which are a very
large part of industrial business, the Provi-
dent doesn't seem to say definitely that, if a
person has paid up £5 4s. in instalments
spread over four years, and then can’t pay
any more, it will even give its written pro-
mise to pay £6 twelve years ahead as an
equivalent.
* * ft *
This article deals with one branch only of
the Industrial Assurance business ; there
isn’t enough space in a moderate-sized paper
to show the utter unprofitableness of the
same kind of assurance in all its other forms.
In The Bulletin’s opinion the notable
features of Industrial Assurance are these :
It manages to exist because it is practised
by a poor and ignorant class who, in 999
cases out of 1000, never attempt to figure
out the real inwardness of the ghastly sham.
It provides good billets for many managers,
directors and others, and exists mainly for
their benefit. And, where it doesn't exist
wholly for the benefit of the managers and
directors, it exists to make dividends for
private shareholders, and to find work for
the great army of seedy non-producers who
collect the weary, weekly 6d. at the back-
doors of the people who don’t know any
better.
A Boom Wanted.
A recent announcement by Minister Best that
the Victorian Government intended to inaugu-
rate a “ bold, progressive public works policy ”
is evidence that the continued girding of David
Syme has at last produced some result. Some
months ago David the Stupendous came down
suddenly in the Age with his ukase that the
Turner Ministry was to “go it.” It was a
favorable time to float a big loan in London,
said David, and the country, David said, was
being starved for want of public works, and the
policy of economy, in the opinion of David,
was being carried too far. There was an
undertone of menace in the article which inti-
mated that if the Turner Ministry didn’t “ go
it” DAViDSYMEmightfeel called upon to whistle
to his menial downstairs to send up a new
Ministry which would. Ever since then the
Age has been the voice of one with a strong
Scotch accent crying in the wilderness for the
Turner Government to “ go it,” and the under-
tone of menace to the effect that David Syme
might get measured for a new Government
at any moment, was always present. And now
it would seem that the Ministry is going to try
to boom a little, anyhow, in order to keep its
billet.
• * * »
Victoria does want a “ bold, progressive
policy ” ; about that there can be no possible
doubt whatever. It is about four times as
thickly peopled as New South Wales, and
it isn’t likely to hold many more people
for a long while unless the half of the
province which is now practically inacces-
sible is opened up. It wants roads and rail-
ways to make Eastern Gippsland habitable. It
wants roads, railways, and, above all, large
irrigation works to make the western plains
fit for occupation. The only question is whether
they should be paid for with borrowed money,
or out of revenue. The habit of paying for
almost every kind of public work out of bor-
rowed money is so ingrained in the Australian
soul that almost everybody takes the former
alternative for granted. When the great Syme
organ first issued instructions to the Turner
Government to go it, the ukase was accom-
panied, as a matter of course, with the remark
that the present was a favourable time for
floating a loan in London. The idea of doing
anything without the inevitable loan curse was
so unprecedented as not to be worth mentioning.
* * * *
Australasia has already the biggest debt on
earth in proportion to population. The wheeze
which every politician has got by heart on this
subject is that the debt doesn’t matter because
it was incurred for “reproductive works.”
This is partly true, and very largely a lie.
The Seven Provinces owe, roughly speaking,
£55,000,000 for works that are not reproductive
in any sense. The entire public debt of Great
Britain, for which there are no reproductive
assets to show, runs to about £l5 per inhabi-
tant. That part of the debt of Australasia for
which there are no reproductive assets to show
equals £l3 per inhabitant all round, and by
reason of the heavier rate of interest, this £l3
is quite as heavy a burden as Britain’s £l5.
It took Britain 200 years to get to this
pass ; Australasia has got into an equally
disastrous situation in 40 years. Britain got
there because it had enormous sums going out
for war expenses, over and above its ordinary
expenditure. Australia had no such outlay,
but it had immense sums coming in by way of
land revenue over and above its ordinary in-
come. And all this is taking no account of the
borrowings for railways, tramways, water-
works, sewerage, telegraphs and telephones,
and anything else of a reproductive character ;
even though hardly any of these undertakings
pay interest on their cost, yet so long as they
pay cmythinri towards interest, they are left out
of the above calculation. But merely through
charging to loans all manner of casual, unre-
productive works wdiich should have been de-
bited to current revenue these provinces, with
their magnificent land revenues, with no wars
(save a little native trouble in Maoriland), and
with practically nq navy and very little army
to maintain, have gone almost as deep in debt
in 40 years as Britain, with all its wars, with
its huge naval and military expenditure, and
with no vast land revenues to reduce the burden
of taxation, has done in 200 years. And
Britain is steadily reducing its debt. These
provinces are increasing theirs.
* * * *
Victoria has, undoubtedly, spent far less
loan money on wholly uureproductive works
than any other of the Seven Provinces. But
by spending large sums on works which were
only slightly productive it has got there just
the same. Victoria has to raise about £600,000
a year by taxation to pay that part of the
interest on its public debt which the income
from its “ reproductive works ” fails to cover.
That is about 10s. per inhabitant per annum.
The British public debt costs about 11s. per
inhabitant per annum, so even Victoria, the
least-burdened province of the seven, has built
up almost as heavy an unproductive load in
40 years of peace as Britain did in 200 years of
war.
* * * *
Another reason assigned .ad nauseam .why
Australasia should keep on raising loans is that
the money “ develops the resources of the
country.” Sometimes it does. Bub every
civilised country is developing its resources all
the time, and will keep on doing so all the
time, and there is no earthly reason why Aus-
tralasia should be the one country on earth
which can’t develop anything without borrow-
ing. Moreover, an enormous amount of the
borrowed money doesn’t develop anything.
Another reason assigned is that when a pro-
vince suddenly stops borrowing, as Victoria
has done, the increase of population is violently
checked. Naturally it is. When the Vic-
torian boom was at its worst, some ten years
ago, about one-tenth of the population lived
on wages paid out of borrowed money. When
their borrowed bread and cheese suddenly
stopped other industries couldn’t find
immediate employment for this immense
mass of population, and many shifted to other
provinces where the gorgeous borrowing policy
was still going on. Yet another reason (ad-
vanced by the Agk) why Victoria should go
making some more debts, is that it has bor-
rowed so much less than N.S. W. that each
inhabitant pays 4s. less per annum in interest.
This is held to mean that Victoria can safely gO
further in debt till the extra interest absorbs
the said 4s. pet annum. An equally logical
view is that Victoria could stand 4s. per
annum extra taxation, in order to raise
£220,000 a year out of revenue for new public
works, and thus not go further in debt. To any
community which hasn’t got the loan complaint
in its worst form it seems quite as easy to pay
£220,000 a year towards new works as to pay
the same amount in interest on the awful dead
horse of an old debt.
* » • *
Allowing for the fact that some public
works yield no return at all, and that most
of them don’t pay full interest on the loan
money with which they are constructed,
a return of 2 per cent, is about all that
can be reasonably expected. The loan money
costs fully 3 per cent. On this basis a province
which borrows even £500,000 a year for public
works adds £5OOO every year to its taxa-
tion, and at the end of 20 years it will be pay-
ing £lOO,OOO a year in perpetuity as the price
of its little boom. The same province, if it
raised £500,000 a year extra revenue, and built
the same works therewith, would add £lO,OOO
every year to its revenue, and in 20 years would
have £200,000 a year coming in instead of
£lOO,OOO ayeargo i n g out. This is a vast difference,
and any Government which is under orders from
its Caledonian tyrant to go it may well con-
sider the point. Victoria has nearly £2,000,000
more gold on hand than N.S. Wales, which
shows that the money with which to pay more
taxes is available. And there are plenty of
devices for raising the money if the Turner
Government likes to try the experiment of
creating a Public Works Fund, and setting
aside certain special sources of revenue to keep
it supplied.
* * * •
The common plea in this matter is that the
country is poor and can’t stand more taxation.
Yet these provinces are lightly taxed on the
whole in proportion to many other countries.
They claim also to be about the wealthiest com-
munities on earth, and consequently the best
able to stand taxation. In any case, they can
stand taxation quite as well to build railways
and irrigation works as to pay interest to Bull
on the money borrowed to build railways and
irrigation works. And taxation, when the
money is profitably employed in the country,
makes it richer instead of poorer ; the money
remains in the country, though it may have
changed hands, and the works which were con-
structed with it are there also, and where there
is now £500,000 in gold there would be then
£500,000 in gold and £500,000 of valuable
public works as well. Hitherto, when Aus-
tralia has made a special effort in the way ot
taxation, it has always been to send the money
out of the country by way of paying interest to
Bull —interest on the old, weary, dead horse
which being dead is still immortal—and that is
quite another thing. The experiment which
The Bulletin advocates has never been trie
yet. The public works boom which was made
out of revenue would not be liable to ®
sudden and disastrous stoppages, and the awlu
depressions, and dreadful recoveries which
characterise the loan boom. And £500,006
year raised by taxation and spent in the cowwhy
on reproductive works would not only add
£500,000 every year to the aggregate wealtn
(because at the end of each year the money
would still be in the country and the new
works also), but it would likewise add about
another £lO,OOO every year to the State income
in the shape of receipts from these works, an<
in the meantime it would supply work and
wages to about 3000 able-bodied men represent-
ing, with their families, about 15,000 people
The Bulletin believes in having a great boom
in every province of Australasia, and this isi
idea of how to start one.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Federal Capital.
The partial settlement which has been arrived
at re the Federal Capital should give Australia
a rest from the gruesome maunderings of A. G.
Taylor about the alleged agreement —which
that long and brassy gentleman says is already
cut-and-dried—to make Melbourne the capital
of the Commonwealth, and thus leave Sydney
a mere dead provincial town, bleaching on the
shores of the river of Time. A new clause in
the Constitution, proposed by Premier Turner
of Victoria, provides that the capital is to be
Federal territory, and this agreement shuts out
Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide equally from
any chance of being the Federal city. The
place selected as the centre of the Common-
wealth will be absolutely severed from the pro-
vince to which it now belongs. If Victoria
surrendered its one big city, what remained
would be a mere second-rate province, with
700,000 people—very little more influential than
S.A. or Queensland —and with hardly enough
voting power in the Commonwealth Parliament
to make itself heard. If N.S.W. gave Sydney
over to the Commonwealth as a Federal terri-
tory, the N.S.W. that was left would be a
secondary province of far less population and
voting power than Victoria, and with a coaly
little capital at Newcastle. If S.A. handed
over Adelaide, it would reduce itself to about
the rank of Tasmania. Therefore this agree-
ment, if the Convention has sense enough to
adhere to it, ends the gory conflict among the
existing capital cities. And it is well that it
does; the idea of having the Federal city on
the sea-coast, convenient to all enemies who
may come along, and having a Federal and a Pro-
vincial Government in one city, and of having a
Central Government with no home of its own, but
lodging on sufferance in the house of one of its
subordinate States, was utterly foolish and
impracticable. The new agreement was not
arrived at, however, without another desperate
and unseemly grab on the part of the N.S.W.
delegates, five of whom, including Premier
Reid, voted for fixing the capital irrevocably
at Sydney. Tasmania, S.A., and Westralia
made no grab at all. A proposal to fix the
capital in Melbourne was only supported by
one Victorian delegate, Peacock of the Fearful
Laugh ; therefore the Southerners can at least
say that they reached out far less vociferously
for the loot than their Cornstalk neighbors.
The proposal to make the capital Federal terri-
tory, and thus disqualify all the existing capi-
tals equally, was supported by nine Victorians
and only five N.S.W. delegates—the other five
hanging on grimly to the last to their claim
that Sydney, and no other place, should be the
seat of Government. The fact that the vast
majority of the South Australians and Vic-
torians voted thus solidly, and that only one
Victorian voted for Melbourne as the seat of
Government, at least gives the lie to the alleged
“ conspiracy ” to make the Yarra-bank city the
political centre of Australia. But the fact that,
of all the five delegations, that of N.S.W. was
the only one that clawed to the very last for
•■he ownership of the Federal city, and that
N.S.W. is also the only province where a loud
and brazen provincial party has systematically
denounced Federation as a conspiracy on the
part of the other provinces to get the Federal
city, is a very curious combination.
Civil Service Employment.
Editor Bulletin.—The dignity of a calling is
the exact measure of its'' estimation in the
public mind—an estimation that rests largely,
if not altogether, on the public need of its
ministrations. Nevertheless, although the
whole machinery of life would come to an
abrupt standstill with the cessation of the
labors of the Civil Service of any country, the
Service itself (in Australasia) occupies a
lower standard of estimation in the public
mind than does the most unskilled branch of
unskilled, labor. The cause is not far to seek.
In a democracy in which government of the
people, by the people, for the people, has be-
come a petrified shibboleth of office-seekers, the
class whose position appears to be even
momentarily assured becomes the immediate
target of attack. The Civil Service is
there—and it is always there. It is
popular to attack it, to retrench it, to dis-
miss it. The civil servant is alleged to be
somewhat superiorly educated. He wears a
coat in office hours, and his hire is called
“ salary,” and not “wages.” Moreover, he is
paid once a month ; consequently the working
man hates him most cordially. The poor
wretch has no certitude of continuity of em-
ployment. A change of Government means to
him a fresh alarm. Dismissal spells to him
starvation, for no decent firm would employ a
man whose service has compelled him to be
“servile,” and oppressively “sir’’-vile—not
“civil.” Retrenchment is always popular, for
it is such an easy method of adjusting
revenue to expenditure, and often obviates
the imposition of fresh taxation. Then, too.
Democracy is a blind fool. It thinks to serve
the cause of labor by degrading what it con-
siders to be a privileged form of labor. It
seeks to drag down the status of the Govern-
ment as an employer of labor to that of the
worst type of the employer of labor in the open
market. Its true democratic course would be
to maintain the status of Government in this
connection, as a standard of possible attainment
by all outside employment of labor. There is
no immunity from continuous attack, therefore
the Civil Service is inefficient and ill-manned.
The best men in the community are frightened
of it. Democracy cannot be run on “ busi-
ness ” lines (which mean buying labor, and
other things, in the cheapest market and
selling in the dearest) ; Democracy must be
run on socialistic lines—lines that tend to
the social advancement of the whole com-
munity, with the Government as
its brightest type. What sort of a poor, cor-
rupt creature would a judge be were he amen-
able to the regulations of a Public Service
Board? Hey! Again, what kind pf rank-and-
file efficiency can yon expect from a Civil
Service, the highest prize in which is £lOOO per
annum ? The trembling wretch who enters, with
a petition for an advance in his “screw,” the
board-room of the N.S. W. triumvirate sees in his
mind’s eye the gruesome legend over the portal
“ .£lOOO per annum.” In the ordinary course
of human nature, the cheap chief of the service
could surely never admit a value for service
distantly approximating his own; the gulf
which induces respect —and servility—must be
maintained at all hazards. The English civil
servant has a dignified position in public life,
mainly because it is under the aegis of the aris-
tocracy, whose younger sons must be pro-
vided for. There, truly, patronage is a power,
but it is a patronage seldom exercised on behalf
of incompetency. There examinations of a
preliminary nature must be passed before a
candidate is admitted to nomination, and the
examinations are severer than our own. The
report of the Public Service Board of N.S.
Wales seems to indicate the utter incompetence
of the entire Civil Service. Considering the
universal incompetence, is it wonderful how
vacancies, which have from time to time
occurred on the Board itself, were so readily
filled by the discredited and worn-out chiefs
of various departments. The Board should
be placed absolutely outside Parliamen-
tary interference. The members should be
permanent, and they should be paid the
amount earned by a first-class man in any
outside liberal profession, say some £2500 or
£3OOO per annum. If the Governors of the
Service are worth to the State only £lOOO a
year what would they earn in a private capa-
city, and what are their under-strappers worth?
Emphatically, no vacancy on the Board should
be capable of being filled at a moment’s notice
on the nod of a Minister. It is the Acting-
Judge iniquity in another form.— Publico.

The New Fateh on the Old Garment.
In The Bulletin’s opinion there is no process
by which a nominee Legislative Council can be
gradually reformed and brought into demo-
cratic ways. The only thing to be done with
it is to wipe out the nominee principle utterly,
and clear out the old nominees in one lot, and
start afresh. In Maoriland Ballance began a
gradual reform by appointing all new members
for a seven-year term and leaving the old life
members to die out through old age and the
accumulation of barnacles upon them. The
scheme hasn’t panned out' any too well ; the
first batch of seven-year nominees are already
beginning to vote the Tory ticket and to talk
the Tory shibboleths. In N.S.W. ten demo-
crats were sent to the Council barely three
years ago, and already, according to the man
who sent them, they are growing unrecognis-
able. And it is quite natural that they should.
Before they went to the Upper House they, in
common with most other progressive poli-
ticians, usually called their present colleagues
old, dull-headed fossils of the most lamentable
kind. After they went there the majority of
the Upper House still consisted of the same
fossils, and the same term of contempt remained
in use. To the public an M.L.C. was still an
ancient wooden person with no brains and
much bingey. By-and-bye the old terms of
reproach begin to irritate the new democratic
M.L.C. It is one thing to stand outside and
say, quite truly, that the average M.L.C. is a
pompous ass; it is quite another thing to
stand inside and be one of the asses,
and hear the fellow outside say the same
thing. In course of time the new M.L.C. gets
a great deal madder at the democrat outside
than at the fossil inside, and joins the fossils.
The same thing always happens under the same
conditions. When the first English conquerors
settled in Ireland, they regarded the local kern
as something beneath contempt and too low for
derision. But the Englishman in England
went on calling the residents of Ireland slaves
and hogs and other things, and the Englishman
in Ireland found himself included in the ana-
thema simply because the other fellow never
took time to discriminate, and he ended by
throwing in his lot with the native and be-
coming more Irish than the real Irishman.
A dozen different waves of English settlers
poured into Ireland, and the mere handful
of real Irishmen absorbed almost all of them.
And in the same way, a reforming Govern-
ment may send up one batch after another of
new democrats to a Tory Council, and the old
original Tories may in course of time disappear
utterly, but so long as the reform begins by
adding a patch on to the old. garment, instead
of burning the old garment altogether, very
little permanent good will result. The new
men inherit the bad name of the old institution,
and that creates a gulf between them and their
fellow democrats outside. They are not paid,
in N.S.W. at all events, like the M’sL.A.,
which is practically telling them that they are
not worth payment. Their powers are strictly
limited as compared with those of the Assembly,
and when the best democrat is appointed to a
legislative billet with the intimation from his
fellow democrats that he isn’t worth any
salary and that he needs to be strictly
tied up as a suspicious character, he soon
feels a desire to kick those other demo-
crats. He is also told that he must
avoid all interference with financial matters,
because he hasn’t the necessary brains and
honesty, and he doesn’t possess the popular
confidence—in short, he is an unpaid legislative
leper, and he presently grows angry enough to
behave like one. Just by way of an experiment
The Bulletin would like to see John Norton
created an M.L.C. ; and if, after he had been
told a few dozen times by the democrats out-
side that M’s.L.C. were an ancient, stupid,
dunderheaded race, he didn’t rally up the aged
senators to deal out stoush to all and sundi",
and to chuck out everything that came in thei 1
way, he wouldn’t be the fighting John tins
paper takes him for.
Vice-Regal Chief Justices.
Australia, thus far, has had one Federal
system of Government. All the provinces have
been satisfied to sanction the immoral practice
of appointing a Chief dustiee to act as poli-
tical-social figurehead when the ordinary
vice-reeal person was not available for
duty. Governors in Australia, as we are often
reminded by newspaper writers here and at
’Ome, serve mainly to represent the Queen in
an inner social circle, and dispense hospitality
to a chosen few. His Excellency’s wife is a
more important influence in Government House
politics than her husband, we are told. The
vice-regal establishment is supported by the
community at large for the particular benefit of
Sassiety, with a capital S, whose members are
expected to court the friendship of the reign-
ing family. When a Chief Justice is sworn
in as Deputy-Governor he puts himself
on terms of intimacy with the moneyed class,
and the other Judges, who mostly rally round
their chief in his entertaining character, join
with him in a highly improper sacrifice of
the Bench’s claim to impartiality. The law
lays itself open to be “ nobbled ” by the Nicest
people, and in a great measure it is “ nobbled.”
To any furious admirer of the administration of
English justice all the world over The Bulle-
tin can only say that Judges in Great Britain
live less exposed lives than their Austra-
lian brethren. Eor indirect proof of which
fact search the London society scriptures.
Judges in Great Britain confine themselves
almost entirely within a legal, professional
circle, and when called upon to weigh criminal
evidence against a member of Parliament, or a
high-toned personage of any sort of distinction,
they can do so in a calm, judicial spirit. The
case of Lord William Nevill provides a useful
object lesson in the difference between the re-
spective environments of British and Austra-
lian Judges. The prisoner pleaded guilty
in the Court of Queen’s Bench, to a charge
of obtaining money by fraud, and he
was sentenced right away to five years’
penal servitude. The same Lord Nevill might
have performed the same crime in Mel-
bourne, where he was quartered in days
of yore, and nobody can positively affirm that
he wouldn’t have come to the same or worse
grief at the bidding of, say, Chief Justice
Madden. Nevertheless it is positively certain
that Madden, the occasional Acting-Governor,
couldn’t avoid the consciousness of a Govern-
ment House intimacy with the titled swindler.
Should a Judge be tempted into close society
contact with the ruling class which, under any
circumstances, must always be a somew’hat
favored class? Or should he live up to his pro-
fessional character by striving to be as unap-
proachable off the Bench as he is whilst up-
holding the dignity of his wig ?
Yet another more or less Suffield case. On
July 13, 1897, a young woman named Hughes
alleged that she had been assaulted and robbed
at Rhyl, North Wales. Upon complaint to the
police the latter produced photos, of men and
youths who had been previously in custody.
The woman promptly identified the photo, of
one Walter Spriggs, who had been previously
convicted of a petty offence. Spriggs was
arrested in London, and at his trial five wit-
nesses swore that they had seen Spriggs in the
neighborhood on the day of the assault. For
the defence 12 witnesses testified that on that
day the accused was with his parents at Forest
Gate, hundreds of miles distant. Justice
Grantham, who tried the case, summed up
dead against Spriggs and observed that the
alibi set up on behalf of the accused was alto-
gether too perfect and too artistic, and he went
on to remark that these witnesses had come
into court dressed in a manner above their
station in life. (Also, perhaps, they shuffled
their feet in the box.) How the five witnesses for
the prosecution were dressed did not transpire.
The Judge also abandoned all precedent by
referring to the prisoner’s previous conviction in
his summing-up, thus influencing the jury against
the prisoner. The jury found Spriggs guilty,
and he received a sentence of five years. Sub-
sequently the case was taken up by Mr. Yel-
verton, a London barrister, and Home Secre-
tary Ridley was induced to put it in the
hands of the Criminal Investigation Depart-
ment for further inquiry. The evidence of the
defence witnesses was not only confirmed but
further supported and Spriggs was released,
after serving two months of his sentence, upon
a “special license,” and has been promised a
“ free pardon.” This latter idea of giving a
pardon to a man for an offence he never com-
mitted is the only humorous incident in the
case.

Melb.’s gilded Mayor, Malcolm MacEacharn,
who is David Syme’s latest white-haired boy,
headed a deputation, the ether day, to the
authorities of the enfeebled Vic. Postal Depart-
ment, to demand that another cable connecting
with the dear old Motherland should be con-
structed. Malcolm Mac. urged as a reason
that, only a few days before, he had perforce
let a ship go uninsured for a fortnight, and had
also lost the sale of a shipment of wheat on ac-
count of cable-breaks. A dual or absolutely
reliable cable-system evidently means tons of
money for the big Melb. merchants. Why,
then, don’t millionaire Malcolm Mac. and his
club pals, to whom the deadsure connection
with ’Ome means so much, put up a few thou-
sands apiece as a subsidy to induce the accursed
Government to do this thing? It is “a bit
thick ” to expect the working taxpayer to pro-
vide speculators and wealthy business houses
with such a costly toy as a second cable. To the
common halfpenny-Australian, whose messages
to England are neither lengthy, nor frequent, the
present service is sufficient. He wouldn’t even
reap any advantage from another line in the
shape of additional world-wide news in the
dailies, for the syndicate now deals out even-
handed non-competitive slush to all alike. And,
as for the war argument, it may safely be
assumed that Bull’s enemies, on an outbreak of
hostilities, unless they perish suddenly by the
wrath of a righteous God, will cut that girdle
wherever it may lie in the sea.
When it was proposed in the Federal Conven-
tion that the railways, under Commonwealth
management, should charge uniform rates by
distance, the loudest lament from the N.S.W.
side was that the cut-rates were necessary for
“the development of the country.” Which
seems a little thin, somehow. As an instance,
Berrigan, in Riverine, can send its wheat to
Melbourne (about 180 miles) for about lbs. per
ton. It can also send it. to Sydney (+34 miles)
for 16s. per ton. N.S.W. loses several shil-
lings per ton on the transaction. It doesn’t
develop Berrigan worth a cent., for the Melb.
line had already developed it. And other
places in N.S.W. have to pay extra rates to
make up for the loss on the Berrigan transac-
tion, consequently their development is re-
tarded. So where does the development come
in ? Where the hindrance to
comes in is sufficiently obvious.
Q,. Govt, proposes — by-and-by — to bring in a
Bill to amend the “ Act for the Protection of
Aboriginals and the Prevention of the Sale of
Opium ” —spurred thereto chiefly by the death
of a Muttaburra Chinaman. The poor wretch,
robbed of his opium, went mad, and
ran wildly round the township. Being
hospitalled, he promptly died. When
post-mortemed, the medical dissector could
And no cause of death. “All the organs
were healthy, and showed no signs of any
disease likely to cause death.” Yet, after he
had become unconscious, “ his temperature rose
suddenly to 107 deg.” He had been in his
usual health on the previous morning. Local
Chows merely said— “ We see that befoh
many time ; p’leece stop him opium ; he die
mad.” Aren’t whites a lot of unsmirched
saiuts? In many cases where the Act has
missed flat murder, it has inflicted torture
worse than starvation. Whether the use of
opium is or is not desirable, or is or is not justi-
fiable, the brutal stupidity of cutting off the
opium-users’ supply at ten minutes’ notice is
simply damnable.
A daily paper’s London correspondent says
that there is an agitation among directors of
Anglo-Australian companies for improved tenure
of their mines. All right; what’ll they pay for
the improvement? The leases are for compara-
tively short terms, it is true —21 years, and so
on—and it may be owned up to at once that the
cos. might do more justice to the properties if
they could lay out their plans for a longer life.
Wherefore the term might be extended if
But, first, what’s the advantage in a short
term? Answer it with another—What’s the
good of a short Parliament? Obviously that the
people may kick out the members if they don’t
suit. So with the leases. As matters stand,
the country has the power, at the end of 21
years or whatever the term may be, to consider
a lessee’s conduct and act accordingly ; and as
the lessee knows that the lease is granted,
not solely for the benefit of the lessee, but to
secure the well-being of the people—who are the
owners and lessors—as well, the lessee also acts
accordingly. At any rate, he is restrained
from doing things that he might otherwise
attempt —the awful day of reck >ning comes
nearer and nearer. What gunrantee will J.
Bull give us that, suppose we do throw away
this safety catch, he won’t, let the cage to the
bottom of the shaft with a dull, sickening thud?
Bull may be able to contrive an effective sub-
stitute for the guarantee that the short lease
affords ; but The Bulletin can’t think of any
in time for this issue. And, moreover, it is not
its business to think of any. It’s Bull’s busi-
ness—he’s asking for a change. If a desired
further improvement is in the direction of
abolishing the labor conditions, what are they
going to give us for that ? The people who
own the mines (it’s just as well to keep
on repeating that to J. 8.) want room to
“ graft ”; and so they stipulate, when they
order the crowd off the grass, that room shall
be found for at least a couple of them per block.
It is a purely business arrangement —a conces-
sion made in lieu of rent. If, then, they under-
take that the lessee shall have the right to order
everybody off the grass, will J. Bull pay a rent
higher in proportion ? If he won’t, but still
expects us to make the change, what sort of an
idiot does he take these kawlinies to be?
At Brisbane, a little while ago, William
Eastwood, charged with failing (because he was
out of work) to support his children in the
orphanage, was given a week to find employ-
ment. Failing to get it, Pa Pinnock related to
him once more the old lying tale that any man
who really wants work can find it, and conceded
him another week. “If you haven’t got work
by that time I’ll give you three months,” said
the kind, fatherly Pinnock. It is a joyful thing
to be selected by any bull-headed official as the
one man in the great, hopeless array of the un-
employed, who must find inside a week a thing
which probably isn’t there, or else go to gaol
for three months. The Bulletin again invites
Pinnock to go penniless to some town where
he isn’t known, and call himself Smith,
labourer, and hustle round in moleskins after a
job which will keep body and soul together,
and see if there really is plenty of work for
every man who wants it. Then when he
has been warned off from 47 different places,
and has slept out in the park, and got rheuma-
tism in his old bones, and been ordered by some
other beak just like his own dreadful self that
he must find work immediately or go to gaol,
it may possibly dawn on him what a flinty ass
he has been. Anyhow, if Pinnock doesn’t try
this experiment then he doesn’t know whether
a man can get work or not, and he has no right
to say what, he doesn’t know, or to gaol any-
body for not doing what he can’t prove to be
possible.
♦
The Federal Convention is gradually coming
round to the conviction, which this paper
arrived at a year ago, when it published “The
Bulletin’s Federal Constitution,” that the
railways and the public debts should be
taken over by the Commonwealth. But
though the members are coming round,
the process is slow ; it takes the aver-
age Convention delegate such a long, long
time to see even a plain business matter like
this that there is no visible hope of doing
any good about it this Convention.
The Age wants an adjournment and a
fourth Convention, so as to give this
urgent matter another chance, but another
long delay would probably kill Federation for
good. The best tiling to be done now is to
provide the easiest possible way of making
amendments in the Federal Constitution, and
get the Commonwealth started. After all, so
long as there is abundant opportunity for
repairinu the Constitution later on, the first
defects matter comparatively little.

The Wilson Barrett season opened at Her
Majesty’s with a very large attendance, yet the
writer has seen smaller audiences in Sydney where
the enthusiasm was considerably greater. The
ntter woefulness of the “ Manxman ” was partly
responsible. It is a tragic play, bathed in unutter-
able grief, and dragged through rivers of tribula-
tion, and then left to dry on the bank, and then
dragged through some more, and hung out in-
sensible on a line to recover consciousness.
I here is a touch of cheerfulness in the first act,
but after that the woe settles down on top of
everything like a depressed encyclopaedia in 26
volumes. Apart from the big, manly character of
Fete, which sheds a halo over the tale, there is
only a moderate amount of plot, and what plot it
amounts to is of a rather familiar order. Kate
Cregeen, the daughter of the hairy, pious ghoul
who keeps the public-house, is loved by Pete, who
has no assets save his noble heart and his huge
boots, and she loves Philip, who is rich, and has
prospects of rising to be a large tin-pot in
the island. Pete goes away to make his fortune,
leaving Philip to take care of his girl, and Philip
takes care of her in the old, old way and in shady
places late at night. She marries Pete when he
comes back rich, and lives miserably, and bolts
miserably, and the home breaks up. And when
Pete and Philip have had an explanation—the
sinful Philip sitting a crushed wri-ck with his
troubled conscience and his tall hat by his side—
they all separate. Pete starts miserably back to
Africa or somewhere. Philip trails wretchedly
away to the court, where he sits and lectures the
local thief. Kate drags the wreck of her once-
beautiful self back into the old public-house,
where it is understood that she is going to pass
the rest of her life in being preached at by her
father, the ferocious old moral publican with the
spectacles and the whiskers. The publican, at
the beginning of Act IV., is found rehearsing the
church choir in his yard and beating time with an
axe, and that is the only bright spot in his char-
acter all through.
• * * m
Pete is a noble, big-hearted personage,
and Wilson Barrett plays him with reasonable
success. His principal disadvantage is that he is
a heavy man who moves rather eumbrously in
his big boots. Barrett's Roman profile makes
The Bulletin desire to see him sideways as Nero
in the “ Sign of the Cross,” or, if he is too ponder-
ous for Nero, it would like to see him sideways as
Vitellius before that bloated Ctesar reached his
most mountainous condition. Barrett is un-
doubtedly a fine actor' in many respects, though
he doesn’t break any records. There is sometimes
an impression that a good deal more could be made
of Pete, who is a character of vast possibilities, and
usually has the centre of the stage all to himself.
Pete is a trifle lugubrious all the time, even in his
cheerful moments. He is at his best when his
house of cards and his Kitty come down in a heap
on his head ; then he suggests the strong, silent
hero, and his large sea-boots are set firmly but
heavily in the right path, and the audience rather
likes Barrett, and weeps copiously.
* * * *
Three other characters practically fill up
the rest of the story. The Kate Cregeen of Miss
Jeffries is a beautiful woman, stately, like the
departed Maxine Elliott, but brighter, less cum-
brous, and more intelligent than that vast, placid,
cow-like charmer. She is a bright, clever bit of
womanhood before her fall. She falls more grace-
fully than the average, inasmuch as she doesn’t
take to wearing shapeless, brown rags as an advt. of
her yvoe; and several times she provides a streak of
tragic intensity which suggests that the soul of the
real artist is in her. Percy val s Philip Christian is
an utterly colorless character ; Percyval is
chiefly remembered so far as the man who looks
like Titheradge and acts very unlike him.
Old publican Cregeen, as represented by Ambrose
Manning, is the most wholly satisfying person in
the cast. His make-up, his tones, his whiskers,
his steely glare through the big spectacles, and
the soured and embittered trot of his two big
feet, all suggest the most ferocious and vinegary
kind of Christian. He is played cleverly and con-
sistently, and The Bulletin just loves old publican
Cregeen. Miss Lillah McCarthy, as a pleasant
old lady, and Horace Hodges, as a mean-looking
subsidiary villain, one Ross Christian, fly round
on the outskirts of the play. A willing and
rather scientific little scrap between Philip and
Ross, and some good scenery with a lot of hay and
many roosters and their harem in it, also call for
mention.
** * *
A certain smattering of prosperity has
begun to manifest itself at Sydney Opera House
(dress-circle Is. 6d.) where the Barnes-Stanford
management is now in its third week. The old
dim curtain with the three angels painted on it
(the tight angel on the left who is staggering
among the clouds, and the doubled-up angel with
the huge bare feet on the right, and the tlnn angel
in the centre who apparently didn’t have enough
to eat) now looks down on better business than
the Opera House has seen for some little time.
The latest production, “Thou Shalt not Kill,” is
quite as good a drama as could be expected
for eighteenpence. It is something about a girl
who fell through the machinations of a clergy-
man when she was very young, and became a
beautiful and desperate Tottie. Later on she
marries a good old baronet with a green cucumber
in his hand, and then she finds her sinful parson
again. She hugs him in the old way, but he
coldly refuses to be hugged, and their troubles
and complications lead to the heaving of the
baronet over a cliff. After that the drama
becomes twenty times madder than could be ex-
pected even for eighteenpenc.e, and how mad a
drama may be expected to become for eighteen-
pence it takes a lot of experience to define. The
sinful J.P. who butchered the baronet holds a
wildly-inforinal inquiry, at which the chief witness
and the policeman fight in the court-room ; also he
arrests the parson. He likewise strangles the
baronet’s widow in her bedroom, but next day she
appears in her usual health, and her butchery
isn’t again alluded to. She is laid low at last by
the bad man’s poclcet Krupp just as everything
comes to light, but she takes a lot of killing.
* * • *
One especially hilarious scene in a drama
which gives a lot of mirth for eighteenpence, is
where the assassin pays ,£2OO to the man who saw
the murder, and takes a receipt in which
said man promises not to say anything about
the crime, and gravely puts the compromising
document in his pocket. Then he shoots in
an off-hand fashion at his visitor, and goes
away. The assassin, in fact, shoots at almost
everybody on sight; generally when he is intro-
duced to a person he fires on spec. Some of the
scenery is distinctly above the eighteenpehny
standard, and one or two members of the co. are
far superior to anything that could be reasonably
anticipated at Is. 6d. Miss Therese Lindahl is
beautiful and stately as the baronet’s married
Tottie, and her version of that soiled angel is a
very clever piece of work. Miss Alice Senior
does good work all the time : also Stanford and
Phil Walsh do good work part of the time. The
t’ ing which calls loudest for strong insult or
contumely or pigs-feet, or anything which can
express ill-feeling, is the unlovely woodenness of
the parson’s best girl. Whi n one considers that
the bad J.P. wades in blood together, his con-
duct is even below the eighteen penny average.
Also, the parson’s pants do a lot to bring his holy
calling into disrepute.
** * *
“ Nancy,” at Sydney Lyceum, was to have
been succeeded last Saturday by the “ Terror of
Paris,” but business was so good that the manage-
ment decided to rcll the old drama along for
another week. Pretty Lilian Wheeler, in the
short, innocent frock of Nancy, may have had
something to do with the boom. In the first act
of the drama there is a festive gathering of boat-
men and their wives round the long deal table
with the gin on it, and Nancy’s weather-beaten
parent makes a speech, in the course of which he
states that Nancy is a good girl, well brought up,
and able to swing an ugly left if called upon.
Nancy rests a small, dimpled paw on the table by
way of unreliable corroboration, but possibly the
public kept on going in the hope that she might
bash somebody by-and-by, or chew Diver in the
midst of his wickedness, or otherwise assert her-
self. So far, however, Miss Wheeler hasn’t swung
that alleged ugly left of hers on anybody.
** * *
The three Elegelmanns are near the end
of their season at Sydney Tivoli, and the gym-
nasts who come after them will have a very large
contract on hand. Anyone else who comes to the
country and wants to pose as the most wonderful
person on earth in that line will have to do some-
thing very unusual indeed, and it is rather difficult
to imagine what the Hegelmann crowd has left
for anyone else to do.
