** * *
The Sydney rush to hear Albani was not
exactly repeated in Melbourne last Saturday, for
although a hundred or two enthusiasts stood
around the Town Hall entrances as early as six
o’clock, there were some seats available in all
three divisions of the auditorium when the tardy
Governor turned up. Perhaps the people who
stayed away had taken a packed house for granted
and were holding back their rush for subsequent
concerts. Anyway, the organ-gallery, which had
been made ready for a five-shilling overflow, re-
mained as bare as Mother Hubbard’s cupboard
likewise a few rows of seats, upstairs and down-
stairs, at the farthest end of the building. Ail the
same, a very imposing crowd welcomed Madame
Albani with a sober and reasonable show of
gladness, and the performance in general got a
promising reception. To expect thrilling results
from Albani’s autumnal voice is to be disap-
pointed, of course. It is older, for its age, than
the comely lady herself, who appears to have been
much less careful than Patti of her splendid
natural gift. A faded voice, and gone at the edge
in some places, yet, nevertheless—as The Bul-
letin remarked in Sydney—the voice that Albani
utters and makes the most of. The artist cannot
use it with the facility of former days, and her
breathing is audible afar off, but the consciousness
and confidence of a triumphant past are in her
vocal efforts whilst, the charm of her platform
manner goes for much. Albani at least shows
cause for a great reputation when she attacks an
operatic number. William Pauli, the baritone of
the co., made a big success on Saturday. The
mezzo, Miss Sarah Berry, and violinist, Miss
Clench, were encored with rather less vehemehce,
and Orlando Harley—scarcely recognisable ih any
respect as the tenor whocarne out here with Foli—
managed to fetch ’em with a sentimental song.
To-night (Thursday) and Saturday are the remain-
ing Albani occasions in Melbourne.
** * *
The joyous pantomime at the Opera
House announces the last nights of its Melbourne
career. Addie Conyers doesn’t slay the Giant any
more. She passed out of the show on Friday,
and Miss Fdie Hay tor, who is a fine, massive boy
to put in the foreground anyway, has undertaken
to lay the Giant low for the rest of the season.
As a matter of fact, the monster isn’t laid low.
Circumstances compel him to remain upright
during his death agonies, and after he has received
a mortal wound as though it were a stitch in the
side, he walks away to join the great majority.
** * *
“ Shadows of a Great City,” at Melb.
Royal, is a broad, irrational production, supported
by some deserving scenery and a respectable co.
The Firm brought “ The Shadows ” from America
long ago, when Maggie Moore, J. C. Willi am sob
and Kate Bishop were concerned ih a valorous
attempt to make it appeat a first-class article.
Time hasn’t mellowed the dialogue, or recon-
structed the plot, and there doesn’t seem to beany
reason, bar Grattan Riggs, for restoring this
memory of the past to its original haunt. Riggs
plays Biddy Roonan, an Irish faymale with a
warm har-r-r-r-t and a s(>-inch waist, in a humor-
ous, inoffensive style, but considering that
Maggie Moore could do the same thing ten times
better, it seems a pity for the male comedian to do
it at all. “The Shadows” fortunately doesn’t
give way to gloom and tears as often as might be
expected, and the co.—comprising Charles Hollo-
way, Chalmers, Temple Harrison, Willard, Miss
Leighford, and Miss Fugenie Duggan—keeps the
trouble moving at a fair pace. Business is very
good at the Royal of late, and manager Holloway
intends to knock creation, in a week or two, with
a new and gorgeously-mounted draina—“ Whteh
London Sleeps, ”

Barrett at Melb. iMncess’s, appears rather friskier
than before, and her lack of high moral tone is not
lamented. Sundry alterations have happened to
this particular breezy nothing since its previous
outbreak. A new shade of hair and a touch of
sentiment are supplied by Miss Leitch in the place

which imitation certainly softens the blow of Miss

Gilbert, Coleman, George de Lara, Howard
Vernon, and other male features appear to

Ada Reeve is doubtless back in the name-part ere
now. On Saturday the little leading lady sprained
her ankle, a calamity that enabled The Bulletin’s

invalid’s French shoes, as Susette. The under-
study sang her songs expressively, and her per-
formance at all points was most commendable,

** * m
Wilson Barrett tells an interviewer: “I
start with the view that Hamlet was not a man of
30, but a lad of 19 or 20.” There is nothing the
matter with W.B.’s view of Hamlet’s age except
that he (the actor) can’t possibly communicate it
to his audience. Barrett, for as long as the writer
can remember him, has been essentially a heavy
actor—heavy about the head and neck ; heavy in
his movements (as compared with the activity of
such a mature “boy” as Bellew, or the'late
Charles Ryley); and, above all things, heavy in
his voice and elocution. Any attempt by Wilson
Barrett to portray a youth will always be a failure.
Witness his uncanny representation of the boy-
poet Chatterton. Despite the actor’s earnestness
and skilful attention to stage-business details the
performance is painfully unreal. “ Chatterton,”
at Melb. Princess’s, the other afternoon, set the
present scribe thinking of the first shock he ever
experienced from a “leading man.” It was in a
small provincial theatre, and they were playing
“ Lady of Lyons.” Widow Melnotte appeared to
be a very young person, with a grey wig on her
head and the traditional lines of age painted on
her face. The door of her cottage was fluno-
violently open, and there came pounding ponder-
ously into the kitchen a list, rooster, who held
aloft a gun decorated with gaudy ribbons, and
crowed in tones of rumbling thunder, “I have
won the per-rize, moth-er! ” This was the
widow’s son, Claude Melnotte, starting with the
view that he was a gushing ini ant of 19, or so.
* * * .
It isn’t an actor’s reading of a Shake-
spearian part but his interpretation of his reading
that the audience has to consider. For instance
the Cassio (Wigney Percyval) in the Wilson Bar-
rett co.’s poor performance of “Othello ’ doubt-
less reads the lines of the famous “drunken
scene ” aright to himself. He wishes to represent
a dashing young soldier in a state of reckless in-
toxication. But he doesn’t succeed. His attempt
fails absurdly. It is one thing to aim at appear-
ing drunk, and quite another thing to strike your
audience to that effect. If Wilson Barrett as
Othello, were required to go on the burst,’ he
would read the fact intelligently enough, but he
wou dn’t con vey the intelligence to the house in a
convincing manner-judging from his attempts at
razzle-dazzle portraiture in “ Ben-my-Chree ” and
“Silver King.”
Cartwright has a singularly attractive voiee, a tine in-
strument capable of expressing every shade of feeling
and having more inflections to the sentence than the
writer has discovered in the tongue of any other pro. he
can call to mind. In a play of French origin this quality
means much, since the giddy little insinuation is often
quite lost for the want of the tiniest tilt of the tongue
At Poverty Point.

Has Albani ever been an amateur sprinter ? Her little
race-on and race-off the stage recall the antics of the
joyous youthful sheep or the frisky junior goat in spring-
time. Artemus Ward struck an immortal truth when he
sadly observed that “ Women and cows shouldn’t run.”

A wife, irritated by her husband’s falsity, sends for a
gallant, whom she has hitherto strenuously resisted. He

he won’t go, Then she persuades him to take a few drops

grabs the phial, drinks the lbt, and falls senseless on the

Henry Arthur Jones’s professed contempt for his share
in the manufacture of “ Silver King” again recalls that
Jones and his collaborator, Hermann, quarrelled qver
their respective claims of authorship as soon as they had
begun to divide the profits of a joint success. Hermann
devised the plot and gave the “ robust sentiment” to the
play, whilst Jones put in the character sketches and the
principal speeches, and an epic-rani here and there—it is
generally supposed. Absurd of Jones .to despise “ the
dull devil of spectacular drama,” which brought him into
notice. “The Silver King,” despite the out-of-dateness
of its opening scene- Denvers don't go t,o the dogs in
skittle-alleys nowadays—will be alive when every play
written by Jones “on his own” has passed out .of
memory.

audience. First, a little laugh will be heard in one corner
of the auditorium, and then, bit by l>it, it will ripple
round the house, to end in a universal shriek, followed
by a clatter of hand-applause. They don’t seem to grip
as quickly there as they do in a Sydney, Brisbane, or
Melb. audience. Anyhow, Winton (here with Rickards’
Biograph c. 0.) used to find himself in the middle of the
next joke before the laugh at the last had started. So did
Thornton.”

A MESSAGE FROM THE QUEEN.
That gracious message from the Queen,
It works with magic power :

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
N.S.W. revenue for February, 1898 (£671,171),
was £68,617 better than that for February, 1897, so the
income for the first eight months of the present financial
year is now only £126,839 less than that for the corre-
sponding period of last year. But the month’s expendi-
ture (£763,917) was fully £90,000 larger than the revenue,
so the yawning deficit keeps getting bigger. With eight
months of the financial year gone the shortage is now
£517,309, which is a tremendous lee-way to make up. At
this time last year the shortage was £442,614, so there is
a bigger gap to fill this year than last. And the shortage
would be still worse, only £14,300 has been charged dur-
ing the iiast month back to “1895 and previous years”
—back to the old, handy, elastic, ever-useful “ Dibbs
deficit,” in fact. It was announced many months ago
that only about £7OOO remained to be charged to these
by'gone years, but still the process of debiting items of
expenditure back, and diminishing this year’s shortage by
making the 1893 or 1894 shortage larger, goes on gaily',
and the Cash Basis never gets really started.
During the past eight months the N.S.W. loan
expenditure has been £1,123,188 against £946,303 for the
corresponding period of last y'ear, or an increase of
£176,885. So things are again as reported a month ago :
the big deficit getting bigger, and the public debt in-
creasing faster. The only present prospects seem to be :
(1) To shove along the big land auction of 110,000 acres
which is advertised for this month, the money to be paid
in June just in time for this .vear’s revenue ; (2) to make
a special effort in the way' of leaving this year’s accounts
unpaid during the four remaining months ; (3) to charge
as much ordinary expenditure as possible to loans or to
the legendary “ Dibbs deficit ” ; (4) to hustle up the
seizure of the Education Fund ; and (5) to have the
general election in June, before the deficit has to be for-
mally announced.
* * * •
When S. A. resolved to undertake the manage-
ment of its British debt through the Agent-General’s
office, instead of employing’ a bank as agent, there was a
howl among influential authorities on finance about the
ridiculous impossibility of the scheme. It has worked
well enough, however, and the cost is £lOO per annum for
each million of debt. Victoria does its business through
a bank at £250 per million. N.S.W. pays its bank in
London on an average t’425 per million— £17,000 a year
more than it would pay on the S.A. basis, and £9OOO a
year more than on the Victorian basis! And £9OOO a
year is equivalent, at present rates, to a lump sum of
£300,000 —so isn’t it time to have a change ?
» * * *
Victoria’s revenue for February (£530,409) was
£25,035 less than for Feb., ’97. The receipts for the eight
months of the financial year which are just gohe are still
£133,409 more than those for the corresponding eight
months of last year, but the improvement doesn’t seem to
keep up very well. The revenue has kept well ahead all
the way round, and now', just as it turns into the straight
for the run home, it begins to wobble badly.
* * * *
Queensland’s revenue for February (£222,459)
was over £2OOO better than that for Feb., ’97. Tasmania,
for February, had a revenue of £71,760, which is £12,742
more than the takings for February, ’97.

The ordinary life department of the Citizens’
Life Assurance Go. (Sydney) show’s the best y'ear it has
yet experienced. The premium income for 1897 w’as
£64,669, and the total income, including interest on
investments, £70,012. Out of this, no less than £44,707
w 7 as added to the accumulated funds, which now stand at
£155,218. The following is six years’ record of the
Citizens’ ordinary life department
Income. Amount added
to funds.
1892 .. £15,641 .. £11,187
1893 .. 20,488 .. 14,636
1894 .. 31,080 .. 18,208
1895 .. 44,089 .'. 26,427
1896 .. 55,483 .. 31,059
1897 . 70,012 .. 44,707
The expenses are always kept down to exactly 15 per
cent, of the income, so the ordinary branch of the Citi-
zens’ is, on paper at least, the cheapest-managed office in
Aus'ralia, the A.M.P. only excepted, and it runs the
A.M.P. very close. Also, the securities look good enough,
even though “freehold property” has increased dur-
ing the year from £5478 to £16,912, which is rather
a large increase in a kind of investment that is none too
reliable. Whether the increase arises through purchase,
or foreclosure of mortgages, or how’, doesn’t appear.
The Industrial Branch (the depressing 3d. and
6d. a week business),which is far the larger part of the Citi-
zens, doesn t look nearly’;so promising. Six years’ record
of the Industrial Department is, as follows :
Income Amount
• lncome ’ added to Funds.
1892 .. £109,686 . ... £24,460
1893 .. 112,957 .. 21,763
1894 .. 126,998 .. 10,060
1895 .. 133,404 .. 10,503
1896 .. 146,647 .. 11,655
1897 .. 156,144 .. 167
One apparent explanation is this ; The expenses of the
Ordinary Department are limited to 15 per cent. Con-
sidering the experience of very much larger offices than
the Citizens’ the chances are that the Ordinary Life De-
partment isn’t worked for 15 per cent, or even for 20.
Any’ expenses above the 15 must be charged to the In-
dustrial Dept, because there is nothing else to charge
them to, and if the Ordinary Life Department continues
to boom as at present it will probably grow so big that its
expenses, over and above 15 per cent., will be more
than the Industrial can fairly carry. The dividend of
10 per cent, to shareholders is charged wholly to the
Industrial; also the depreciation of furniture, and the
great bulk of everything. The Industrial expenses for
1897 were £83,954 or 58 per cent, of premiums, as com-
pared with £74,630 or 57J per cent, of premiums in 1896 —in
other words, the total income increased by £10,500 and
expenses swallpw’ed up £9300 of the increase. During the
last two y’ears 1 12,600 of the Industrial income has been
“ profit on realisation of securities,” and but for this wind-
fall the funds would actually have decreased. If the
Ordinary Branch keeps growing at the present rate, and
the Industrial keeps remaining about stationary at the
present rate, the chances are that the Ordinary will have
to pay a far bigger share of the expenses by-and-bye,
which will damage its glory considerably.
* * * *
The Citizens’ co. is just now pulling down its
premises in Castlereagh-street, Sydney, with a view to
building a finer building on the site. This involves the
destruction of the old building—an asset which ap-
pears to stand for a pretty large amount in the accounts.
Judging by the Industrial Department’s accounts, any-
how, this doesn’t seem a very good time to meet any
losses. Also a feature of the Citizens’ which concerns
policy-holders materially is that it is a proprietary con-
cern, and the people who supply the money' have no voice
whatever in the management. Out of £346,000 of funds
the shareholders supply £22,350, and the people who own
this £22,350 run the whole show. There isn’t even a
large body of shareholders to supervise the directors ;
the directors and a few officials of the co. are the princi-
pal shareholders, so it would appear that in the main they
supervise themselves.

The Bulletin now learns that auditor Souther-
den, of the Q.N. Bank, quitted the service of Gibbs,
Bright and Co. before he became professional overhauler
of the books of the Drury institution. Therefore, the
inference that Hart, of Gihbs, Bright and Co., who helped
direct the Q.N.8., was practically audited by his own
doesn’t hold water.
* * • *
The sale of the business of the Queensland
Mutual Insurance Co. to the Manchester Fire Co., was
duly ratified, and, so far as Tiie Bulletin’s information
goes, no explanation of the mysterious circumstances of
the sale was offered. The case, as it is persistently told
by the man in the street with much circumstantial detail,
is this : The shares of the Queensland Mutual were worth
about 3s. 3d. or 3s. 6d. The management, or some of it,
received an offer from the Manchester co. to buy the
business and assets at 10s. a share. Thereupon a share-
broking firm proceeded to circularise the shareholders
wholesale, offeVing to buy shares on behalf of somebody at
something’ like 45., and many holders sold before the Man-
chester co.’s offer was made public, and thereby lost 6s. a
share. The person or persons who bought apparently
knew about the offer to purchase, and also had access to
the share-register in the office. Also, the fact that there
was an offer to buy’ the business seems to have remained
very' dark while these deals were going on. All of which
has led to much language on the part of people who
sold for 4s. shares worth 10s.
• * • *
That very mild little institution, the Austra-
lasian Mutual Live Stock Insurance Co., reports a profit
for the past year of £ls9—equal to a very little over 3 per
cent, on the modest capital of £4780. In 1893 the profit
was £2 ; 1894 showed a loss of £1; 1895 produced a loss of
£3 13s. 9d., but since then things have looked up a little,
and the co. has now £335 of accumulated profits. The
total liabilities to shareholders and the public are £5585,
and the visible assets are put down at £1335. The very
wide gap is filled in by setting down “ goodwill ” as
worth £4250. The Live Stock Insurance Co. paid
its last dividend in the year God knows when, and The
Bulletin forgets how it arrived at its present painful
state. Anyhow, “ Goodwill, £4250 ” is a very beautiful
item.

Baxter and Saddler, who claimed ,£20, 000, re
the Gladstone (Q.) railway', were awarded £7700 by the
arbitration court. Arbitrators’ expenses alone ran to
£l4OO for each side, and with all the other expenses it
probably cost £5OOO to arrive at that £7700 verdict.
* « * *
The insolvency of Cyprian Stanton, late of the
City Bank of Sydney, is the old, old story of the manager
who was applied to by' himself for an overdraft, and hav-
ing been acquainted with the applicant for many years,
and having the highest opinion of him, granted it imme-
diately without security. Still, it is sometimes hard to
draw the line about the manager’s overdraft. In the case
of Drury, of the Q.N.8., for instance: If Drury, while
manager of the Q.N.8., had kept his account at some
other bank it would have created an impression that he
couldn’t risk the coin in his own institution. Yet Drury
knew his bank too well to trust it with a credit balance,
and the bank ought to have known its Drury a great deal
better than to trust him with an overdraft—but it didn’t.
So there they were.

Industrial and Provident Permanent Benefit
Building and Investment Society (Sydney), with a capital
of only £80,040, contrived to earn £5154 profit for 1897.
The visible reason is that the concern with the name that
is so long that the other end can only be seen on a clear
day' doesn’t speculate in property of its own, has no big
mortgage on top of it, no debentures, and only owes
£1044 on deposits altogether.
• • * •
The Metropolitan Mutual Permanent Building
and Investment Association (Sydney) shows a loss of
£2945 for 1897. This co. has £140,000 capital and proper-
ties to an alleged value of £283,720, which only yield
£6708 net for the year—equal to £2 Bs. per cent, per
annum. Said properties are tottering under liabilities
to the extent of £142,000, which is probably not far
from their present sale value, and as the interest runs to
£8325 for the year, or £l6OO more than the entire net
yield of the properties, there is very little prospect of
dividends this century anyhow. The reserve-fund is now
swallowed up all except 1 14, so next .year’s loss will begin
to chew large holes in the capital. If the Metropolitan
had been content in the boom days to own £150,000 worth
of property unencumbered, instead of grabbing after
£300,000 worth with a mountainous mortgage on it,
things might have been different.
* * * *
The announcement that one of the big Ameri-
can assessment life assurance companies proposes starting
business in Australia is another roundabout reminder
of the fact that this country, through the absolute want
of any legal standard of solvency, or any State audit, or
any decent precautionary legislation of any kind, is a grand
field for the foreign and local life-assurance swindle. The
assessment system is .mostly swindle. The ordinary sol-
vent life office always keeps on hand a fund, accumulated
from premiums, equal to the full present value of its lia-
bilifes, and if three-.fourths or five-sixths of its policy-
holders took their surrender values and left, it would be
quite as solvent as ever with regard to those who re-
mained. The assessment co. bursts when a lot of its
policy-holders quit. Its principle is that every one pays
a small sum regularly to cover his share of working ex-
penses. Death claims are met by calls on members —that
is to say, they’ are met if enough calls can be got in.
The older a" member gets the heavier are the
calls, and when a member lives to a great
age and becomes poor and past work the calls gener-
ally get so heavy that he is frozen out after paying regu-
larly for many years, and dies uninsured after all. In
the ordinary life-office the old, impoverished member wlm
has been paying for 30 to 40 years can generally let his
policy slide for the last seven or eig’ht or ten .vears of his;
life—the bonuses keep it in force. Ordinary life assurance
gives the biggest profits to the old member ; the assess-
ment business breaks the old member by striking hi
with the biggest calls. In the ordinary life-office th
premiums get smaller with years because the bonuses can
be applied in reduction of premiums ; in the assessment
system they grow bigger.
In the U.S., the home of this unlucky system,
the assessment co.’s go bankrupt by dozens. They mostly
collapse from the same cause—inability to collect enough
calls from live members to meet the claims in regard to
dead members. The assessment co. is simply a life
assurance office which gives credit to its customers with-
out security, and its promise to pay £lOOO at death is
really a promise that it will pay that amount if it can
collect it from the other fellows. The assessment assur-
ance curse has grown so bad in the U.S. that in several
states there is a movement to prohibit it altogether. If
this alluring game is allowed to start in Australia the need
for some vigorous life assurance legislation will be more
urgent than ever.

* • • *
Melbourne just now shelters an organised gang
of long-finners, who are working the exchange-cheque
swindle to a very large amount daily. All the men con-
cerned are legitimately named similarly to well-known
wealthy citizens, and unfortunately for the changers it is
only after the cheques are returned that the difference
between the wealthy and the worthless parties is dis-
covered. The law unfortunately does not allow of prose-
cution on a “ worthless ” cheque when the drawer has
any'thing at all to his credit in the bank on which it is
drawn. Hence the conspirators avoid arrest by having a
few shillings at the banks on which cheques are drawn, at
times, for scores of pounds.
* * * •
Onlooker : “ I express no opinion on the merits
or demerits of the remarks of the chairman of the White
Cliffs Opal Mines, Ltd.— vide Bulletin, Feb. 26—but sub-
mit the following reliable information :—When the co.
took over the property, there were at work, bringing in.
the vast returns mentioned, about 300 tributors, nearly all
at 75 per cent, tribute and ‘ nothing found,’ and working
on Block 1, admittedly the best on the field. These were
replaced, there and then, by some 35 or 40 wages-men, who
continued to work throughout the 3'ear in question—tri-
butors were not employed till the last four months of the
financial year, the percentage being 50 per cent, and
tools, etc., found, no inconsiderable item- These were not
allowed to tribute on Block 1 either. The system of ‘ open
cut ’ adopted entailed a terrible amount of development,
and having to pass through a lot of worked ground. The
heart of the good opal country has only now been reached,
so that the returns in question are not disproportionate to
those of previous 3'ears. It seems to me that the directors
of the co. have an idea, as you say, that their property, as
the name would suggest, embraces the whole of the field.
They do not seem to realise that a lot of new opal country
has been opened up since they bought t;heir property,
said country being responsible for a lot of saleable opal
during the past year. While not admitting that opal-
stealing is common, it is pretty clear that some of the
gem goes astray, and supervision is indeed difficult. If a
law were passed requiring all opal-buyers to be licensed,
and a record kept of all their transactions in opal (sqch
record to be open to official inspection) it would give the
legitimate buyer a locus standi, and do away with the
illicit opal-buyer. Such would be better for both bona-fide
buyer and seller.”
* • • *
Contributing shareholders in the Mt. David
mine (N.S.W.) are having their little funeral, and are in-
dignant—no wonder—at the manner in which they have
been fooled. “In conclusion (say’s the chairman in his ex-
planation), I wish to point out that no prospectus was
ever issued, and the public were never asked to come in.”
But they' got there all the same. Men went in under the
impression that the money they' put up went to work the
mine, instead of to pay for it. The present position is
embarrassing, for if the co. called up all the capital it
“ would still be £5OOO in debt.” The syndicate subscribed
for the contributing shares in the first instance. What
it did with them afterwards is clear. The right thing
to do under the would be to sell them at
any price ; failing that, to give them away. Right-think-
ing persons don’t object to parting with a liability. The
chairman’s explanation evades the issue : that the co. was
formed with a capital of £120,000, that five-sixths of this
was issued as paid-up, that the remaining sixth had to pay
for the mine and work it, and has still to face the music.
In fact, five-sixths of the mine represents very little more
than the option held to purchase it ! It is a new way of
fooling N.S.W. people, though it has been practised
in two or three other instances. Had the Mt.. David mine
turned out remarkably well —which would mean striking
something richer than the oWner ever saw—no trouble
would have occurred, but, as usual, it didn’t. By dint of
cautious selection the owner managed to get out some
200 tons of rich ore and then got £ll,OOO cash for the
mine. While the plant was going up two or three small
lots were sent to Sydney—“which went well.” Assays,-
too, were sent all the way to Maoriland. These, too,
“ went well.” An oz. per ton, at least, was expected
from the bulk —which has only gone a few dwts. A new
manager finds the machinery has been put in the
wrong place, and that other serious mistakes have been
made ; and the statements before made, and as often
denied, that the reef is erratic in quality and that the ore
must be gouged or otherwise picked, are now admitted.
And there is the devil to pay. And yet how those shares
have been tipped and boomed, in and out of print!
* * * •
N.S.W. has to wait on London papers for official
news of most of the Bull-owned mines working in the pro-
vince. Surely 7 the country’ owning the mines is entitled
to know what is being done with them by their lessees?
In gold-mining, the biggest work is being done by’ the
Angio-Australian co. Its operations are controlled by
Eissler, who has displayed good judgment in buying, has
done good work in opening up, and, better still, has had
big luck. According to the last reports published in Lon-
don anew body of ore has been opened in the Gallymont
mine, driven on for 90ft., and bulk assay samples give an
average of some 9oz. per ton for an average width of 3ft.
The Gibraltar, on the other hand, does not seem to be
looking as well as usual, but it is difficult to get at the
bottom of things unless yields are known within a reason-
able time of their being made.
* * * *
One N S.W. field has struck a little scheme.
The chief shareholder in a boss mine secures a piece of
ground not even on the line of reef, though adjoining the
boss mine in one remote corner. Meeting of boss mine
shareholders then called and a proposal to amalgamate
with the duffer carried, whereby small shareholders are
relieved of a big portion of their interest, and forced to
become interested in the duffer. But trouble is brewing.

B.H. Junction is ready for a revival, after just
twelve months’ battling with the consequences of the
creep and the rip-it-out policy which led to that disaster
The new concentrating-mill is ready, and recent develop
lnents underground have been quite satisfactory.
* * • *
Occidental (the N.S.W. low-grade mine) report
for half-year shows the ore milled just under 3dwts., 01
more than a dwt. less than the average of the last seven
years. During these years 50,000 tons have been milled
for an average of a fraction over 4dwts., leaving nearly as
much in the tailings easily treated by cyanide. Last half-
year showed a working profit of over ,1000. The
manager attributes the fall in the average return to the
quantity of surface debris put through the battery as a
cheaper means of getting rid of it than carting it away,
and better returns are anticipated.
* * * *
The curse of the Sabbath tipster—or something
nearly as bad —has fallen on the Girilambone mine,
N.S.W., the shares of which mine have nearly come back
to where they started from—los. When they reached,
for a brief stay, 355., people were strongly advised to buy
them, as they were “ sure to see £5.” It would be inter-
esting to know who sold them at 355. Now proposed to
reconstruct by issuing 50,000 new shares paid up to 7s.
6d., calling up 2s. at once and the balance as required—
and it all will be required, if half the statements made of
the financial position of this concern are true. That it is
carrying a camel-load of debt is obvious. It is impossible
to ascertain from the balance-sheet to what purposes the
£12,500 debentures have been applied ; then there is
another item of £5263 exclusive of debentures. The only
uncalled capital is £3500. To the writer, the whole thing
is mysterious. Wanted by the public, a detailed statement
from the directors.

Dear Bulletin. — The transparent perversion of
“ Shareholder” (?) (Lake George mines), in your issue of
26th Feb., savours too much of the proverbial herring
drawn across the track. Let him once more read the par.
in yours of 12th Feb. Tonnage and percentage only are
referred to. Assay value has been introduced bv “ Share-
holder,” with a clumsy attempt to discount the intelli-
gence of the “public generally,” who are quite able to.
discriminate ’tween tonnage and assay value. He might
now enlighten the “ public generally ” as to the centesimal
loss of copper, silver and gold which prevails in putting
coke and a blast furnace against an ore like that of Lake
George. This would be interesting to the “public
generally',” who have been invited to assist with a cool
£50,000. Just allow me to remind you that the returns
published only give the percentage of extraction, and are
ominously silent as to copper charged to furnaces. Iq
short, what is the loss? —Sulphur.

T. P. Lyons, writing to Kalgoorlie Miner, re-
marks that the Maritana management “ has apparently
endeavored from the first to spend money on the i ine
without producing satisfactory results. They have over-
looked the fact that depth is required to bring it on a level
with the Boulder, or even the Mt. Charlotte. Instead of
s’nking to, say, 500 ft., they are spendingthe shareholders’
money foolishly in driving on what is already known to
be practically the cap of the lode. To perform that
reco nised useless work, the mining - engineer,
manager, the Melb. office, and four men are em-
ployed, and are taking in sa’aries, etc., the monthly
calls made on shareholders. In order to stop such ex-
pensive and fruitless proceedings, I ask m3' fellow
shareholders to join with me in getting six months’
exemption, and to take the management from the present
directors, putting it into the hands of practical men who
have the knowledge and the will to give us something in
return for the money we have expended. The expenses
at present are at the rate of £8709 per 3'ear. By allowing
£3500 to keep a mining engineer or expert, a manager,
and four men, including mining material and the Melb.
office, there would still remain 5200 to be accounted for,
regarding which probably the management can give an
explanation.”

Ed. Bulletin.— Coal Creek Proprie'ary (Vic.)
shares are perpetually being quoted as “ buyers,” 3d. or
6d. I have a standing order for any number of them at
Is. to Is. 3d., yet have never seen any quotation as to
“ sellers ” for the last six weeks. Other stocks well held
are similarly treated by outside brokers, and rigging
appears to be the order of the day'. In fact, the jobbers
appear to do as they darned well please as to getting
quotes in the two daily Melb. papers. I will buy all the
Coal Creeks available at Is. —R.
* * * •
The Tasmania g.m. is having a bad time with
the water. The engineers’ strike delayed new pumping
machinery six months over contract time, and an inrush
happened which taxes present appliances up to the last
pound of pumping power. Constant stoppages are neces-
sary to clean condenser pipes, the water being so highly
mineralised. Not enough mineral in the water to pay a
dividend, so the usual 18d. solatium will be deferred until
the regions below run dry.
* * * *
A Klondyke correspondent of S.F. Examiner
says he is in partnership with Frank Slavin, than whom
“a better fellow never lived.” The life, however, he
describes as awful; 94deg. of frost at time of writing, and
daylight only from 9.15 to 3.30. Flour, £2O per 501 b.
(over 1 800 per ton !) ; whisky, #2 per pint; candles, 4s.
each ; restaurant-meals, 14s. ; drinks and cigars, 2s. each.
An easy fortune, the writer adds, could be made out of
diamond jewellery. He saw a ring worth £BO sold for £l5
to a watchmaker who had an order for it at £l2O.
« * * *
A. C. Aubrey, the Maori lander, who is lecturing
(in Melb.) on his personal experiences of Klondyke,
declares that the new gold discoveries are only in their
infancy, and he advises all strong men to raise £l5O and
set out for the frozen North in seven months’ time.
Aubrey, presumably, will be glad to lead the excursion
party on Cook lines.

WATSON’S WHISKY.
Society
The Brassey Curse is cosmopolitan, and
quite ready to supplement a local mishap with an
imported casualty. Much about the time that
the Sunbeam was making yet another attempt at
perdition on the Victorian coast the other day, the
wife of Gov. Brassey’s nephew, H. L. Brassey,
was rolled over by her horse in the hunting-field,
in England, and had a leg smashed. Why is it,
by the way, that there is never any Marine Board
inquiry into these Sunbeam misadventures ? Even
if alleged master-mariner Brassey isn’t in personal
charge of the hooker on these occasions, some
qualified person is supposed to be in command,
and has no more right to escape inquiry than any
other shipmaster.
It evidently runs in the family—this
disposition to fall off something and get
hurt. The Bulletin begs herewith to ques-
tion the fairness of permitting Gov. Brassey to go
’Ome on a steamer unless he be, to some extent,
chained up. Although destined to suffer disaster
of one sort or other, he could have his destiny
placed under reasonable restraint.

m m * •
The tearful girl who doesn’t know why she
did it appeared the other day before Judge Hodges
in Melbourne, and Hodges, who evidently didn’t
know what on earth to do with her, finally gave
the beautiful, damp, interesting vision one month
in gaol. She had been a servant. She said she
was quite happy, and everybody treated her well,
and she had no animosity against anybody. All
the same, she admitted having set the house on
fire in nine different places at once, and made a
beautiful conflagration ; she couldn’t tell why she
did it —she simply did it. Five or six centuries
ago, when a girl was in this unaccountable
state, the law would have had her dislocated
by the proper official, by way of extracting a
clearer explanation, and on the general idea that a
witness was always more reliable when she was
dislocated. That process is out of date now, and
when the girl in the dock is a frantic and unac'
countable person, the law doesn’t even allow water
to be poured over her to make her more cool and
collected. So the girl who set the house on fire in
nine places and nearly charred the family got off
with a month, mainly because the law was so be-
wildered and driven off its head that it didn’t
know what else to do.
A Gippsland farmer, who was burnt out
recently on a large scale, was certain that
his grass had been feloniously ignited, and
offered .£25 reward for the discovery of the perpe-
trator. He also rioted round, talking gory war
and sudden death for the edification of the sus-
pected person. Likewise he sent for the black
trackers, and the niggers were forwarded from
Sale at great expense. After a cursory examina-
tion their verdict was : “ No man ; woman feller
tracks.” They unearthed the boot that had made
the tracks ; it was worn by the farmer’s 17-year-old
daughter, who admitted, under pressure, that she
had started the fire “ because she loved to see the
people excited, and the water-carts hurrying
about.” The farmer is now called upon to pay the
£25 reward, and another =625 expenses in connec-
tion with the trackers, his daughter is in disgrace,
and his heart is desolate.

General Boom : “ One of the absurdest
spectacles in Sydney is that of the Permanent
Artillery band, about 40 strong, playing a whole
half-dozen warriors up Oxford-street to church
every Sunday morning. ‘ Four-and-twenty men
and five-and-thirty pipers.’ Last time I saw them
the contingent numbered one officer, five men,
aiul a beltless prisoner, temporarily out of the
cells for the good of his soul. Comic opera was a
fool to it! ”
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent alleges that in
a country town, the other day. a stranger went
into the pound-keeper’s stable and stole his saddle.
He was arrested, tried by the pound-keeper him-
self and the local store-keeper— only other J.P.—
and fined 405., or a month. Then they sold his
horse for <£3, and told him to dear out. Well, it
was pretty good justice, but a bit irregular and
Jaypee-like.
* * * *
He couldn’t resist the temptation of help-
ing himself to half-a-dozen of the local J.P.’s
peaches, but the eye of the beak was down upon
him and a summons duly followed. The culprit—
a Government employe—was afraid he’d lose his
billet, so he got the local schoolmaster to intercede
for him, with the result that the beak consented
to cancel the summons on condition of receiving
A 5. Of course the man paid up. Thus a man
who is supposed to see the law administered
actually sucks blood-money from a working man
whom he has in his power.
* • * •
During a recent hot spell, a northern
N.S.W. town was much scandalised by the con-
duct of a J.P., who administered justice in his
bare feet and with very brief linen pants. In
fact, all that end of his washup which could be
seen underneath the desk was naked, and, for all
the litigants knew to the contrary, he had nothing
at all on except his coat. To the fellow who gets
three months, it doesn’t matter much whether his
washup is naked or not, but the legal profession
objected greatly to the J.P.’s feet, which were two
inartistic-looking masses of bare humanity hang-
ing underneath the desk.
* * * •
Col. Gordon, S.A. military commandant,
lately returned from a year’s trip to England, and
the citizens breathe again. S.A. has an army of
only 200 men and two Maxim guns, so that Gor-
don and his huge moustache really constitute
S.A’s first line of defence. S.A. also has some
heavy guns, bought for several thousands during
a war-scare, which have long been rusting on
Glenelg beach. One of Gordon’s chief duties
when in England was to try and dispose of this
artillery, but, although S.A. Govt, was willing to
throw in Gordon and his moustache and the
Maxims to conclude the deal, nothing came of his
mission.

The numerousness of S. Australia’s J’sP.
may be accounted for by the manychanges of Minis-
try prior to Kingston’s accession. The only gift in
the power of an Australian Minister is a J.P.-ship,
which explains the fact that the average Adelaide
citizen dispenses justice in the court in the morn-
ing and runs an illicit totalizator in a barrel
down the alley in the afternoon.

Awhile ago an Australian Governor —who
is pious—visited an aboriginal mission station and
stayed there a few days. He rose at five o’clock
one morning to make a trip round the place, and
hearing a loud clatter in a certain building inno-
cently entered. It was the female dormitory, and
the native girls—as is their invariable habit —were
dancing and playing high jinks in a state of
nature. Whether lI.E. shut his eyes or put his
hands up to his face—the latter for obvious reasons
is preferable in such cases—is not recorded.

* * *
The constable in charge
of a Melb. suburban police-
station complains of being
wakened up at all hours by
drunks, asking their way
home—inquiring if the place
is a pub. wanting a match—
and so on ; the cause of the
sorrow being the lamp and
clock over the door. Yet,
why he should complain is
beyond understanding. Every
policeman wants promotion,
and his activity and intelli-
gence and fitness for promo-
tion are mostly judged by the
number of drunks and other
people that he arrests. And
here is a cop who hasn’t to go
out into the highways and
fight with his drunks and run
them in. The drunk flows
gently to his door all night
ready to be locked up—and
still he isn’t satisfied.

FROM SYDNEY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She turns her head
To the sunset red,
In the thresh of the heaving bar,
In the outer sea,
Exultantly,
She bows to the evening star.
She fights her way
In the gathering grey
Through the strong, intolerant sea,
From its clutching claws,
And grim, great jaws,
She springs triumphantly.
While far and bright,
As the South Head light
Dips down in the distant sea,
Where the city sleeps,
A woman weeps—
And the ship goes North with me.
FRANCIS KENNA.
NOT
“a VETERAN.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas in the “ Hunters’ Contest” Mickey had a colt to ride,
Who’d jumped so well at home, you couldn’t stop him if you tried
’Twas not the fence that stopped him now, hut on the rails there leaned,
And all about the jump there stood, some photographic fiend.
But Micky held the brown colt straight, though in his heart he knew
’Twould need some luck to get him past that photographic crew;
Mick’s seat was firm and hands were low, as he came swooping down
With iron nerve and hard-set face upon the swerving brown.
Now, when they neared the water-jump, and heard the cameras “ click,”
Despite of coaxing—whip or spur—the colt would stop and kick;
When, notwithstanding Mickey’s skill, the colt had th us baulked twice,
A photo.-fiend stepped up to Mick, and offered ms advice.
And yet another one arose, and thus l heard him chide:
“ That moke can't jump! and I don’t think that you knowhow to ride!”
Said Mick, “ The ’orse can jump; and I’ve done all a man may do,
But—my ’orse wasn't foaled upon the field of- Waterloo! ’’
BREAKER.
A HEAD-NURSE'S CAP.
(From the Probationer's Point of View.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
O star-far snowy crown !
White summit of renown !
Mont Blanc ! whence to look down
On Alps of straining-!
We give our luscious years
God meant for love, not fears,
Our koh-i-noors of tears
To thy attaining.
Spiritual body ! lo !
Of three years’ life of throe 1
Now saint-serene you show
In calm, high places.
And we are shriven there,
Sins that as scarlet were
Are changed without demur
To angel-graces.
O consummated bliss !
The straining chrysalis
May guess our ecstasies,
May guage them truly ;
When, shell discarded quite,
Wings thrilling to the light,
Poised ready all for flight,
It fronts fate newly.
Mount spirit, butterfly,
Nought is too deep or high,
Heart-deep or Heaven-nigh
For thy comparing ;
For O, our luscious years
God meant for love, not fears,
Our koh-i-noors of tears
Go with thy wearing !
M. A. ROBERTSON.

Dear Bulletin.— Re, the question of sex-
regulation - there would seem to be some connec-
tion between kangaroo-tail soup and fertility. In
Corowa, N.S.W., there lived, a few years back, a
widowed squatteress, whose pet aversion was
babies, and who would only engage childless mar-
ried couples. But, alas, her favorite family-dish
was kangaroo-tail soup, and, after about a year’s
residence at Corowa, the couples would be child-
less no longer. At length, however, the lady be-
thought her of an old couple, who had been
married in England 30 years before, and who had
no offspring. They were imported as soon as
. possible for service at Corowa ; but, alas ! in 15
months, the kangaroo-tail soup got in its fine
■ work, and the inevitable bantling arrived —with
the result to its parents of the ignominious sack.
Their place was taken by a still more antique
couple, whose ages aggregated 117 years, but
two years of the magic tail-soup regimen pro-
duced the usual effect in the shape of a lusty boy.
This was the last straw. The cherished luxury
was thenceforth sternly tabooed, and nobody was
ever allowed to bring a kangaroo within 10 miles
of the homestead, on pain of instant dismissal.
V 'th the soup went the continuous baby-produc-
tion, and the old lady was happy once more.—
Xob.
* * * *
An Oriental gentleman informs The Bul-
letin that the sex-by-diet theory has long existed
in India. The intending parents keep apart for a
month, during which, if a boy is the desideratum,
the man half-starves himself, while the woman
fares of the best; while, if a girl is wanted, it is
the woman who fasts and the man who feasts.
Once more— Ex Oriente, lux !

A Q. police-sergeant Mas invented
an outrage on Sunday drinking. He
leaves the publican severely alone, but*
hauls up the drinker. Moreover, th©
local Bench backs him by fining thd
Sunday thirst-man £2—rather a top
price to pay for, say, a threepenny
drink. The cross-counter to this should
be to engage a hotel bed from Saturday
night till Monday morning, when the
engager becomes a “lodger,” entitled to
drench his inner reticulations to the full
extent of his desires.

N. : “ The Melb. Oddfellows (es-
tablished 1848) are holding their jubilee.
Ten years ago the friendly societies
were happy enough, but now there is
altogether too big a crowd of defaulters
walking about, men struck off, after
perhaps a lifetime of payments, because
they- have not been able to squeeze a
shilling a week out of nothing a day.
Wherefore the genial, brotherly element
is gone, and the shut-out regard their
whilom brethren as a lot of wolves.”

The frequenters of a certain Syd-
ney hall—stronghold of a particularly
holy sect—are so collectively—or collec-
tionly—mean that the plate had to be
superseded by the levy of a silver coin
at entrance, Result: nothing over a 3d.-
pieoe, while expenses were relatively
enormous. Thereupon a notice was
posted, to the effect that the authorities
valued admission at not less than fid. per
head, with the effect that attendance
has so diminished that the threepenny-
piece will have to be fallen back upon.
Beggars can’t be choosers !
A pretty piece of police tactics was ex-
posed and denounced by Vic. County Court Judge
Gaunt, the other day. It came out in an appeal
against a Petty Sessions sentence of 12 months
for indecency. Accused, five months after
the alleged offence, had been identified by
three girls, but the evidence of two other girls
who witnessed the offence but failed to identify
was not brought forward. This was made the
ground of appeal. Judge Gaunt enquired, “ Why
were these girls not called in the lower court ?’
To which Crown-Prosecutor Walsh candidly re-
plied, “ Because they were unable to identify the
accused.” “Then,” said the Judge, “it was the
duty of the police to have called the girls.” Other
strong evidence was given in favor of appellant,
and the conviction was quashed.
* * * *
N. : “I dropped into the Melb. Wesleyan
Conference to-day, just as they ballotted for
president. There are no nominations, but every
member votes for whom he likes, and it is regarded
as a test of popularity. Pastor Fitchett received
only one vote, doubtless from a sense of apprecia-
tion of the news that he has received <£soo for the
English edition of ‘Deeds that Won the Empire.’
The new president, one Grove, is a mild edition
of General Booth, and walked into the Secularisa-
tion of the Ministry, as far as he dared. But
Mr. Eitchett, with his ever-breadth of smile, is
an impossible eel to grasp. Why does he stand
so immeasurably head and shoulders above the lot
of them, able to dictate his own terms, and defy
all rule, even the itinerancy ? Not because he is
so great, but because they are so small.”
* * * «
Melb. : “In the Wesleyan Conference, to-
day, a pastor expressed the hope that his craft of
this denomination might never fall to the starva-
tion level of the C. of E. clergy. What does this
mean ? Archbishop Goe draws <£2ooo a year, in
addition to a large private income ; Dean Vance
about £lOOO, other benefices £BOO, £7OO, £6OO.
A number in Victoria run up to £5OO. Nay, all
the Vic. Anglican clerical wages clubbed together
give an average of £5 a week. Wherever can the
poverty come in ? It is well known that the rich
ministers are only too eager to dash to the relief
of the poor parsoness, whose little boy thrusts his
knee through his breeches when he kneels down to
pray. Therefore, Pastor Torrance’s proposal to
pool the incomes and divide up must be super-
fluous.”

Apropos the great bath controversy, Cairns
Post valorously maintains that 75 p.c. of the
everyday run of North Queenslanders do not in-
dulge in shower or plunge bath every morning.
Also, that 40 p.c. don’t even wash hands and face
before breakfast. Statistics, however, gleaned in
the neighborhood of Northern Mongrelia ought-
not to be taken as a basis for striking a Europea'
average.

From Maitland (N.S.W.) Mercury’s
solemn report of speech by one Rev. Bice, at last
meeting of Maitland Benevolent Asylum :
That this meeting- is quite in accord with the objects of
the Benevolent Society. . . . and trusts the Divine bless-
ing will continue to accompany its labors. When he
first came to town that institution was the first object
that caught his eye. . . . Not only that it was a valuable
one and well cared for, but from a distance it was a proud
and most beautiful object in the sight. . . . and inside,
the first thing that struck him was the matron herself.
. . . He had not in all his travels seen anything similar
to compare with the Asylum, and West Maitland ought
to be proud of that institution. ... He thought it would
be well if clergymen had something of the kind to look
forward to. . . . The Asylum itself showed it had come
to stay. . . . The balance-sheet made their hearts good
(!!!), and it was a great contrast to an institution he had
just left—the Australian Board of Missions—because it
could not pay his salary as secretary.
Two workmen lately fell from a hoist at
-t. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, and the one who
wasn’t killed was taken to the hospital in a bad
state. As he wasn’t expected to live the in-
memoriam fiend got on the job promptly, and
presently a figure in seedy black called on the
poor, distracted wife, and remarked something to
the effect that the “ Lord gave and the Lord had
taken away.” Then he sniffed resignedly with a
damp, red nose, and asked if she would like to
order, say, three dozen of their beautiful memorial
card with a bit of poetry on it, and the name and
date of death. Presumably somebody buys that
black and leprous card with its slab of hymn
about the vacant chair, else the ghoul couldn’t
raise his bread and cheese, and his black suit
with the snail-tracks on it, and his spirituous
refreshment. But why anybody should buy it
and find comfort in it passes comprehension ; pro-
bably it is another branch of the emotion which
makes newly-married people go to the cemetery
on wet days and look at the epitaphs. And, some-
how, when the spectral card-seller comes round to
the house of mourning no one ever hears of the
dog being sooled on him. Perhaps no one has
sufficient energy, or possibly the animal is con-
fined in his kennel with a crape bow on his tail,
and isn’t available.

Fifteen men at one swoop is probably the
record for a sea breaking inboard of a vessel.
More correctly, ‘‘'seas”; for in the case of the
French four-masted barque President Faure, it
was the simultaneous tumbling in of seas to star-
board and port that robbed her in an instant of
three-fourths of her whole crew ! She was run-
ning 12 knots at the time, before a gale that made
it impossible to round to or lower a boat, even if
the loss had been known in time ; but the vessel’s
height of bulwarks kept the deck so long a lake
that by the time the state of the case was dis-
covered she had left the poor castaways miles
behind. Of the 15 unfortunates, the oldest,
melancholy to state, was only 34, and the youngest
16, and it is notable that in this case—as in that
of the s.s. Fiado, now overdue at Sydney—the
crew was exclusively French--mostly Bretons.
The “Dutchman” doesn’t get the footing any-
where else that he has made good in English
vessels.
# * * *
“One who knows” writes to The Bulletin
from Perth, emphatically contradicting, from per-
sonal experience, the rumor that there is plenty of
employment to be had there. Boarding-house-
keepers, it is stated, are the only people who are
doing well, and the invariable answer to appli-
cants for work is that hands are being turned off for
the want of it. There are numbers there already
for whom there is no employment. The shipping
and boarding-house people, it is further alleged,
make a point of concealing, in their own interests,
the real state of the case, and a ruinous state of
things, it is predicted, must result from a continua-
tion of the population-flow westwards.
* * * »
Anyone who would get an idea of the
jolly-and-fat conditions prevalent in Sydney,
should walk through the main streets at any time
between midnight and 4 a.m. City shopkeepers
place boxes of rubbish on the-kerb last thing at
night, ready for the morning scavenger, but said
scavenger is anticipated by the horde of homeless
and hungry wretches who slink out of the dark-
ness to ransack the boxes for anything edible (?)
they may contain. The writer has seen a mangy
dog and one of Sydney’s night-haunting goats
driven away from a noisome George-st. rubbish-
box by a reputed man for the sake of a crust the
cur had previously nosed and contemptuously
rejected. And though he has walked also the
nocturnal streets of Melbourne and other cities,
the writer has never (perhaps times were then
better) observed the ghostly rubbish-fossicker out-
side the “ jolly-and-fat ” metropolis of N.S. W.

The British Realm, a journal devoted to
military jingoism
If you shoot in the rains in India, or shortly after them,
say up to the end of December, you should have a glass
of sherry with five grains of quinine in it daily ; your ser-
vants also should have the same, without the sherry, but
dissolved in some water to which has been added ten or
fifteen drops of dilute sulphuric acid.
Nature, then, has made a severe distinction be-
tween Colonel John Bull’s internal economy and
that of his colored beater !

Once more the question—“ How much
fine equals how much gaol?” Brisbane (Q.)
Central Police-court tally, taken in order of daily
paper’s report, for last day of Feb. ult. : By
Papa Pinnock, P.M. —Male chronic drunk, £2 or
14 days ; ditto, ditto ; female ditto, £2 or 7 days;
male ditto, £2 or 48 hours (!); drunk while in
charge of a horse and cab, £2 or 7 days; cruelty
to a horse, £2 or 7 days ; petty larceny, ill or 1
month (! !); obscene language, £2 or 48 hours (!).
Same day, G. P. M. Murray, P.M., dismissed
a complaint with £2 12s. costs or 21 days. Same
day,-by W. Yaldwyn, P.M.—Obscene language,
£2 or 15 days. Therefore—ill fine equals 1
month’s imprisonment; £2 fine equals 48 hours,
equals 7 days, equals 14 days, equals 15 days : £2
12s. costs equals 15 days. And this illogical, bile-
varying mix-up occurs practically every day,
everywhere ! ” '

Sydney Evening News makes fewer mis-
takes than most Australian papers in the ampli-
fication of cables. For instance, the Sydney morning
papers the other day received a message which
read “Royal attendedLedeum Cathedral, Athens,”
and both Herald and Telegraph translated to
the effect that the Royal Family of Greece had
attended the Ledeum Cathedral, at Athens. The
Evening News man, however, rendered it as an
attendance by the Royal Family at a Te Deuifi
at the Cathedral, at Athens, which was, of course,
correct. Again : Some months ago a cable curtly
stated “Obituary Edwards.” There were six
possible Edwardses in the the reference books.
The News cable editor looked up their biographies,
and selected the one with an Australian connec-
tion, and so “killed” the right man. • S.M.
Herald, D. Telegraph, Melb. Argus,, Mell>.
Age and S.A. Register, each slew a wrong
Edwards, and so between these six papers five live
Edwardes were gathered to their fathers.

King O’Malley’s arguments at a meeting
of the Vic. Woman’s Franchise League—“ The
first man brought before the first woman J.P. in
Wyoming was her own husband, charged with
drunkenness. She gave him 30 years' hard.”
Also, “On one occasion I was on a jury in a
murder case, and two of the jurors were women.
The male jurors agreed to a verdict of not guilty.
But the women would not hear of it, and it Was
no use arguing with them, because they simply
went into a corner and prayed fervently fbr ten
obstinate men. So in the end the man was con-
victed.” ; ;

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oft-times a man by Beauty’s power is stricken,
And marriage culminates what “looks” began ;
Post marriage -when of Beauty he doth sicken
She’s struck by him—but on'another plan.
She yields her hand to what is thought—devotion ;
The parson ties the matrimonial twist;
The glamour.passing soon dispels this notion,
for in exchange she often gets - his fist.
Orange.
A.S.L.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, March 8, 1898.
mt Hear Myek—
The fluttering'coat-tails of a flock of clergy—as-
sembled to greet the arriving Ormuz, with their
head-man Smith and family on board—blackened
the Quay as if a crow convention had settled there.
The Primate’s chastened greeting included a re-
mark on his flock’s goodness in being there “ so
early,” and elevated the meeting to the rank of a
sacrifice ; each solemn sin-eraser felt that he had
scored with the Recording Angel by giving up his
early morning sleep to greet his chief betimes.
Among the passengers were a couple, to meet whom,
Btrangely enough, no one attended from Govt.
Hmise, which omission was distinctly undiplo-
Fashion is becoming freakish about skirts. The
decree has gone forth that the new jupes are to be
cut altogether on a different plan. In Melbourne,
I believe, they have arrived; the Cartwright co.
has Lady Clarke 11., and other dressy
persons, but it will be months ere the new
draggly folds will flop from the hips of Sydney
womankind. A dress-expert told me the other
day that her frocks “ to arrive” will all be petti-
coated thus. Her dresses are not made on the
wearer, but imported ready-built from ’Oine. A
rough model of this latest skirt is a tablecloth with
a hole out in the middle for the waist, and four
corners snipped off; and if the wearer looks as if
she were shut up inside a vast umbrella, she will
have the comforting reflection of knowing that she
is the Right thing.
The tall form of Speaker Abbott was seen in
town last week as he came up from the Convention
in the South to his daughter’s wedding. Miss
Abbott was married on Wednesday, 2nd, to Mr.
Norman Simpson, of Ban-aba. At first the
church (St. Thomas’s, North Sydney) was going
to be unflowered, as the bridal fell in Lent, when
it is considered sinful to jubilate florally in high
church circles; but, after a spasm or two, the
greenery was allowed. (I wonder if ritualistic
persons really think that the Almighty sits aloft
on the watch and dots a black mark against the
same of every sinner who carries a bit of colored
vegetable into church at the wrong time?) Miss
Abbott’s gown was notable for some high-priced
lace and also for much chiffon which veiled all.
There were four bridesmaids in white and yellow,
» favorite wedding-color combination since the
vice-regal followers wore it last year.
A feature of the recent Josephson-Grimshaw
wedding was a song while the register was being
signed. Miss Pedley sang to an organ accompani-
ment, and as this is the first time she has sung
since her recent long and dangerous illness, every-
body was glad to hear her.
At a swagger wedding lately, the families were
doing their best to keep the ceremony select, and
none but the Naicest were invited. And every-
body connected with the ceremony was so much
annoyed when alarge, irrepressible lady who writes
came and took it all down in her little note-book
and made copy of it.
All the old-fashioneu notions about divorce
being a painful process, about as abrupt and jerky
as having a double-tooth drawn, are quite obsolete.
The new pjtinless method applies the anaesthetio
of good feeling to the raw wound of severance, and
divorcer, divorcee and TertiumQuid are all on the
best of terms. The latest sign of good manners
was exhibited at a recent re-marriage of a lady
whose husband had successfully applied to a kindly
judge and jury to cut the matrimonial knot. She
married the other man, and, just to show that
there was no animosity, husband the first gave a
glittering wedding-present. And all three were
quite content, though it did look as if the deceived
No. 1 were giving his wife away '*r 4 -s % bonus to
the first customer that offered.
The committee ladies who chooe the prizes gi ven
to the Alfred Hospital nurses at the recent swagger
annual meeting, didn’t allow their literary tastes
to riot in unbeaten tracks in selecting the gift-
books. Out of a baker’s dozen of volumes, six
were Tennyson—always a safe card to play—and
the other seven included the bald abruptness of a
dictionary, the religious eroticism of Franees R.
Havergal, and the soaring poetic flight of Shelley.
It must be a very catholic taste that marks with
equal eye Havergal and Shelley.
At the great door-struggle of tne first Albani
concert in Sydney, the fighters trying to force
their way to the pay-hole were supposed to be
superior persons of cultured musical taste, who
would snort disdainfully at being compared to a
first-night crowd at a sixpenny gallery door. All
the same, they pushed and struggled and grabbed
hair and clothing off each other, and were as
aggressive as any li push ” could be. One girl
snatched at a man’s neck in her excitement, and,
in her absorption, didn’t discover for some time
that she was holding in her hand the mans collar,
which she had wrenched off the astonished vic-
tim. On the day after the final sing-song the
hall-keeper ranged up in a corner a whole array of
wrecked umbrellas. Probably the gamps were
taken out to bang the floor in applause, for a sun-
shade at night, if there’s no sign of rain, is a use-
less appendage. But there’s a type of woman who
never sets foot out of doors minus a brolly.
So many toney persons are announcing their
approaching departure for ’Ome that soon there
will be no Nice people left in the city of the beau-
tiful ’arbor, except the absentees, as Paddy would
say. And the woman of to-day has her own send-
off, and speechifies thereat, and the papers report
her words just like a man. Woollahra House will
shortly be empty, when the Paston-Cooper family
depart, after two years’ sojourn here. At Pad-
dington Barracks Lady Cooper, who has played
patroness to the wives of the men who play
soldiers at that millingtary centre, was farewelled
by the Soldiers’ Wives’ League, and, in present-
ing Mrs. Tommy Atkins collectively with the
highly practical parting-gift of a sewing-machine,
she made a neat, gracefully-worded speech with-
out notes, also without hesitation. Another
recent send-off was given by the same local
“ League ’’—when two or three are gathered
together now it’s always a “ League ” when it
isn’t a “Guild”—at Moss Vale, to Mrs. Chris.
Bennett, of the Evkxoose, who, with her family,
trips ’Ome for two years. Local magnates
gathered in force, and donated an album of views
to the lady.
Electric search-lights at fancy-fairs are some-
times as disastrous as flash-light photos, of first-
night crowds in their revelations of hidden cir-
cumstances. There was one thrown on a festive
function in a southern city a little while ago. The
flash.lighted up an obscure garden-seat, and the
features of a frisky, young and highly-placed
matron sitting thereon and partially obscuring the
waistcoat of a friend who happened to bo there
also. The lady and her husband have since bidden
a farewell to Australia, and now some scandal-
mongers are shaking their heads because along
with their names in the listof passengers permail-
steamer the name of the flash-light friend happens
again.
As far as the house was eonosrned, Monday
night, at Her Majesty’s, was an overflowing
meeting. Stalls were full-dressed even to the
ninth or tenth row, and the d.c. was par-
ticularly gorgeous. Glasses were eagerly focussed
on Maud Jeffries on her entrance, and there was
no disappointment in the verdict on the new
beauty. She recalls Maxine Elliott, but, being
more vivacious than that rather stolid Venus,
ought to have more drawing power. Also, she
has soul pieieing eyes and a dot-and-carry-one
walk. Her best blue gown was a symphony in
tucks and side-skirt pleatings. It took the
audience some time to become used to the new-
comers’ voices. It’s in hearing a complete Eng-
lish co. after an Australian one that the difference
between the accents is felt by the ear. English
voices wrecked by wear-and-tear in fog and east
winds soon become husky and worn, but the accent
remains refined and rather affected in the ears of
the folk who talk Australian and glory in their
indolent way of using their strong, well-formed
vocal organs. Music effects in the “Manxman”
are artistically manage i by the orchestra, headed
by conductor Caron ; the incidental tunes are
high-toned selections from Wagner and Schubert,
and old-world unhackneyed Irish songs.
When a N.S. W. Woman’s Mutual Help Society
was recently preparing for a dance in aid of the
funds, they had a large, enamelled bucket filled
with materials for a mighty fruit salad—about -£1
worth of grapes, bananas, pine-apples, peaches,
and cream—when in came a member who sports
B.A. after her name. She was asked to kindly
clear away the fruit-skins, &c., while the others
proceeded with the salad, whereupon she grabbed
the bucket and promptly upset the whole caboodle
into the street.
One of the Government creditors of even date is
a bewildered undertaker with a long bill for an
interment, which the department interested won’t
foot because the burial hasn’t yet come off. An
aged patient in one of the charity-barracks was
sinking fast, and a kindly attendant rang up the
sick man’s only known relative to come out that
afternoon, as the old man was dying. The wires
by chance were crossed and a city undertaker re-
ceived the message, and that afternoon the coffin-
seller arrived with all the panoply of a pompous
funeral. Meanwhile the patient rallied amazingly,
and the undertaker claims compensation for time,
labor, and vehicles supplied, while the department
naturally objects to pay for a funeral which never
happened.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ At times the Austral Salon could tear its hair
over the scarcity of would-be hostesses. But
when the Club throws open its doors to dis-
tinguished guests, competition is keen for the
honor of feeding the lions. No less than 22
‘ hospitallers ’ went snacks in paying for the tea
and cake provided t’other afternoon for the Wilson
Barrett reception. And the co-operative bread
cast upon the waters yielded prompt profits arid
quick returns. For one thing, the hostesses- of
course with their sisters, cousins, aunts, and
chums—get the run of the Holy of Holies inside
the ribbons, which barred off the mere mass of
adherents from the immediate presence of the
Idols of the Hour.
“ First of the guests to arrive was W.B. himself,
wearing, amongst other things, a turned-down
collar, while his bright brown curls haloed the
rim of a bell-topper. Up to now, none of his own
chicks had appeared, and there was hardly another
man on the premises. So the sensitive creature
took fright. May-be it was the bristling phalanx
of brilliant hats (many of them bought for his
express benefit) that took his breath away. Any-
how, he renounced the soft delights of cushioned
ctiairs, flowers, and suchlike accumulated on his
behalf, and went and sat behind the curtain on a
packing-case. Later on, when he had lots of com-
pany in distress, he came out, crisped up, and
made an elegant speech, in the course of which he
made so bold as to tell his hearers they looked
most charming and beautiful ‘ in the mass.’
“ Maude Jeffries came late, having strayed away
en route in lifts and trams. fcUie looked as nice a
girl as ever lived, but that day one sympathised
with critics who accuse her of ‘untidiness.’ Her
black accordeon-pleated skirt and blue chiffon
sleeves bad seen their best days, and her waist and
hip tactics were purely hygienic. A top-heavy
black hat, clustered with pink roses, could not
hide the sad fact that the winsome little curls and
spontaneous tress tendrils of stage coiffures tend
to straggling locks for street wear. Cheerful,
flaxen-haired Daisy Belmore came along in an un-
pretending white frock and sailor hat; while
spectacled ‘Spider’ Percyval looked darker arid
more academic than on the stage. Manning, voted
‘ the beauty of the bunch,’ fell with a crash from
feminine favor owing to the appearance of Cecilia
Wilman in her unsuspected role of ‘Mrs. Manning.’
When the Clarendon people had done their time
at the revels, they straggled off the premises as
they had come in ones and twos, and drifted down
street on shanks’ pony, while Harry Rickards anil
daughter Noni departed in a brougham manned
by green liveries.
“The Salon programmers a whole, wasn’t a
howling success. Probably performers were ner-
vous. One member eloeuted a ‘Wild West’
sketch, with menagerie imitations, but yelling
Indians and chirping crickets expressed themselves
unconvincingly through the mouthpiece of a pal-
pitating amateur.
“ Lady Brassey’s ‘at home,’ to bid her lively
step-daughter and meek stepson-in-law farewell,
was just a gathering of the very Nicest with a
slight leaven of political bigwiguery. Evidently
the idea was to send off the F.T.’s with flying
colors, and a splash of bawbees w-as intended to
blot out some previous records of cheese paring.
P.p.c. cards swelled into booklets tied up with
gold cord, and emblazoned with the family
initials and the years of arrival and departure.
Inside were v ignettes of the departing pair and of
the son and heir, while bathos was reached with
Byron's lines, ‘ Fare thee well: and, if for ever ’
&o. The fixings of the shivoo itself turned out to
be quite as smart as the invitation-cards. One-
half the ballroom was given up to the dancers, the
other was furnished, and there Uncle Tom and
hostess wandered about receiving their dear
friends, who, for this occasion only were all ad-
mitted at the main entrance, and directed by a
posse of servants through the drawing-room into
the dancing chamber. Light and casual refresh-
ment paved the way for a sumptuous sit-down
supper. In the ballroom the dancing half was
void of decoration, beyond palms and small tables
devoted to display of silverware, signed por-
traits of ‘ duchesses,’bowls of flowers, &c., which
were pretty often on the verge of destruction hv
soineunwary Augustus orother who had tripped hi-
generous feet over the Turkish rugs. The garden
was much patronised, but the colored electric
lights made things more picturesque than pleasant
for several couples. Interesting tableaux were
plainly visible, although he and she had taken
themselves to what they reckoned was a dark
corner. In at least one instance the lady was not
free, and hubby, amongst the guests, might have
taken note —several notes, in fact—of how a mas-
culine coat-sleeve shows up against a light-satin
dress. Diamonds were as plentiful as blackberries.
Lady Brassey, looking very thin and pale, sported
black with a white bodice having a black top
secured to the waist by straps of jet. A brush-
like aigrette waved above a diamond ornament
in the hair, and plenty more of the same stones
flashed from neck and corsage. Mrs. F. T. faced
the light in a very up-to-date garment of pale-
grey satin, with crossing bands of cream lace on
the bodice fastened down by carbons; diamond
tiara and ornaments ad lib.
The lately-returned wife of Surgeon Ryan also
sported pale grey, but fixed things up becomingly
by covering the corsage with pale-green fly-net
streaked with silver spangles. A lately - re-
turned globe-trotter, Mrs. George Chirnside, was
another devotee tqgrey, while Mrs.-Percy* hirnside
of that ilk sported black very much embroidered.
The point about Mrs. Percy C. was the fashion in
which her golden ’air was twisted upon top and la
Ch inee, and surmounted by a structure of dia-
monds. A prospective bride cut a tip-top figure
in pale-blue with many sequins ; and Miss Clara
Manning held her owe in white with dressings of
pale-green. Baronet Clarke’s spouse, who has
shed her mourning to some purpose, seemed to
have come in the character of an animated cab-
bage besprinkled with dew-drops on the outside
leaves. Her waving skirts in the dance revealed
green supports, or stems, amid clouds of pink
mysteries. Mrs. Edward Miller was imposing in
yellow satin and diamonds ; and Mrs. Albert
Miller, as usual, was resplendent in pickle-cabbage
pink hung with black lace. Hon. Rupert Caring-
ton’s wife failed to ‘ fetch ’em ’ in a nondescript
white-and-black garb with a splash of red. Pro-
ceedings wound up with a cotillion, in the course
of which the performers were seen gyrating round
a maypole at the loose ends of colored ribbons led
by Captain and Mrs. F.T., or being driven team-
fashion down the room in a polka by the same
energetic couple, the Captain driving ‘ faymales ’
while his lady acted as whip to the baser sex. A
doll-baby figured prominently another time. It
was somewhat pitiable to behold the sedate
johnny whirling round with this effigy as his only
partner, and equally sad to witness some curled
darlings of society with jockey-caps and whips
sprinting down the room on hobby horses to
secure partners. The last figure, from the dancers’
point of view, was the ‘ Souvenir,’ in which they
received gifts decorated with portraits of the
departing pair.
“ The allurement of association with Lcrd
Brassey only drew one Federal delegate to the
ceremony of opening the new Melb. Salvation
Army maternity home. The official programme
provided fora ‘call upon one of the Premiers or
the leader of the Convention to speak for five
minutes.’ But there was no Premier or no leader
present, and although the Army had hired four
big four-horse drags to convey the delegates, Dr.
Cockburn was the only one who turned up ! It
was probably this disconcerting circumstance that
led Commandant Booth to forget the Governor’s
name and call him ‘Lord—er—inn—Aberdeen,’
much to the annoyance of the Old Man of the Sea,
who looked daggers.
“The first Albani concert at Melb. Town Hall
was rather overburdened with ‘ Gorsave.’ When
His Ex. and party arrived, late as u-ual, the audi-
ence found itself expected to stand and look re-
ligious, whilst Benito Scherek pounded a whole
verse of Hanthem from the awful organ. And
again, at about 10.50 p.m., the panting Diva and
her three assistant vocalists tightened the Silken
Bonds with a full-length rendering of the same
1 ideous chestnut. The Hanthem was not being
given ‘by general request,’ as far as one could
judge from the general countenance. Even Gov.
Brassey wore a troubled expression, and the de-
jected haviour of Sec. Lord Neville’s wan visage
might very well have moved the singers to cut it a
little shorter. Lord Neville is too sad for words,
these times, and he makes matters worse by affect-
ing yellow coat-facings about two shades brighter
than an attack of jaundice.
“ All doubt as to the identity of the floral offer-
ing that had the vice regal pasteboard attached
was set at rest when Albani came bounding back
to the platform to sing her first encore number.
She had evidently picked out the Brassey bouquet,
which she brandished in the direction of her dear
hostess, as though it were a precious compliment
indeed. Now, considering the many thousands of
bunches of garden produce sacrificed at the altar
of an Albani during, say, 2o years, this warm
display of enthusiasm over the Brassey posey must
be set down as the most artistic item in Saturday’s
performance.
“ The retirement of the well-beloved Mr. F. W.
Haddon, after 31 years’ continuous occupation of
the Ano US editorial chair was signalised by a big
and enthusiastic Argus shivoo, at Melb.'Town
Hall, last Saturday. The antiquity of the con-
servative organ was shown by the dates of some
of the people associated with it, publisher
Ebbsworth harking back to 1300, and the still-
youthful looking accountant to 1851. Howard
Willoughby (the new editor), Mr. Haddon him-
self, and Mr. E. S. Cunningham (the new ‘ news’
editor were the only speakers, apart from proprie-
tors Maokinnon and Spowers, who conveyed the
cheerful intimation that at no time of its career
had the Argus been in a more prosperous condi-
tion.
“On Steeplechase day the vice-regal pen looked
pretty dull without the Governor’s crisp married
daughter, who left for ’Ome last Tuesday with
hubby and small bov. Quantities of farewell
flowers were sent to her on the China, and they
kept on arriving for so long that at last the steamer
