The engaged Miss Madden trod her native
heath once more in blue-striped silk and hat
crested with cerise geraniums. She and pretty
sister Ruby have come back with their tresses
trained sheer to the top, where the ultimate
arrangement is shrouded in the crown of a small
round hat. *1 he combed-up hair at the back and
sides is very little waved. Soon, very likely, we
will all be Japanese as to coiffure, as of yore. The
younger wanderer had her frock cut rather low in
the neck, then a narrow strip of lace was fastened
innocently at the throat with a small brooch. A
waist-buckle as broad as the wearer enlivened the
back view of her bodice.
“ Local Shakespearean enthusiasts still bark
and bite at the memory of Wilson Barrett’s
Hamlet and Othello. Nevertheless, in the latter
role, he made a fairly fascinating fire-eater, just
tawny enough to indulge in snowy robes, crimson
mantles, gold helmets, and Oriental gems, with
pleasing spectacular results. For all his good
looks, Wilson Barrett, with his face washed, has
no eyes worth mentioning. Of course, his peep-
holes perform their principal purpose in life all
r 'ght, ‘ good enough to see with, my dear! ’ But
that s all we can say for them. However, Othello’s
complexion—capable even of throwing John Cole-
man s narrow orbs into prominence—completely
counteracts W. B.’s optical short-comings, thereby
adding an extra string to his lady-killing bow.
“ So far as looks go, Maud Jeffries was never
more delightful than as Desdemona. As the
Commandant’s lady pleading for alcoholic
Cassio, she wears her low-necked white chiffon
robe, a green - and - gold bolero and gorgeous
Damascene girdle. Long strings of coral beads
encircle her neck ; red, pink and creamy roses
nestle in her dark flowing curls ; and she herself
glows like some tropical blossom. Later, with
the honeymoon over, and her ‘old man’ con-
victed of chronic bad-temper, she sits wrapped in
pale-blue pelisse over an underdress of white
muslin awaiting with well-founded misgivings the
home-coming of her Ethiopian. ‘ Forewarned is
forearmed, and with such clearsighted forebodings
it made one angry to see the victim sit still and
be murdered. Sympathetic Emilia under the bed,
with instructions to scream if the worst came to
the worst, and a few lap-dogs and alarm-bells at
hand to raise a riot if necessary, might have saved
the situation.
“But, perhaps, Shakespeare knew best. It’s a
difficult matter to decide on the exact moment to
let appearances go hang. Emily Mather (Deem-
ing’s victim), for instance, as shown by the evi-
dence at her murderer’s trial, had been seen hi the
yard of their villa wringing her hands, a picture
of abject terror, probably on the very day of her
death. Yet when a passer-by asked what was the
matter, the fugitive smiled and went mdoors
again to dree her weird.
“Desdemona was too pretty a woman not to
meet with a good deal of sympathy from the
audience. An Insolvency Court Judge had
brought his flock of small girls as usual, and
didn t those poor kiddies soak their little ’ker-
chiefs ! Their grief recalled a former night when
they nearly washed their sleepy eyes out be-
moaning the wrongs of the ‘Sign of the Cross’
martyrs. An advertising doctor and his wife
also came with a small boy. A string of weirdly-
dressed infants would bob at the heels of some
parents, I do believe, if they got tickets f or an
execution to-morrow morning.
“ Daisy Belmore, with violets all over her hat,
saw her colleagues’ farewell performance from the
back of the cl.c. Rose Mtisgrove was somewhere
about, too, in a pale-grey frock befrilled with
briglit-blue chiffon. That dashing society belle,
Miss May Wilson, in white brocade and green
velvet, with her fiance, Mr. Ernest Raleigh, and
others, looked her last on Virginius from the
Governor’s box. Maud Jeffries got heaps of
flowers. W.B., in his white muslin Roman robes,
made a speech, was mobbed at the stage-door by
women, and hunted to Ins hotel by a waggonette
of girls. These gay Dianas, so they say, were
rewarded by a parting address all to themselves
from their quarry as he stood on an upper landing
at Mcnzies’ with a bedroom candlestick clasped in
his trembling hand.

At Mt. Gam bier, Vic., the other day a
blacksmith suicided in his shop by hanging him-
self to a rafter, having first lit a large fire on the
dirt floor directly under the spot. When he was
found, the greater part of his body was burnt to a
cinder, and the upper half still hung from the
rafter. A religious crank, surely, trying to break*
sheol gently.
A Melbourne policeman lately captured
a small, husky, eute-looking boy, named David
Solomon, on a charge of larceny, and started in-
quiring into his antecedents. David proved to
have a Scotch mother and a Hebrew father, and
his religion, he said, was “’arf and-’arf,” pre-
sumably half Presbyterian and half synagogue.
This shows a new danger ahead. The man who
finally owns the earth will be named Donald
McMoses, or Alexawilder Cohen, or something
like that. When the Jew and the Scotchman be-
come one composite race, with the large, glossy
beak of Cohen above the whisky breath and the
kilt of Donald, and that new race charges
humanity in general with its claymore in one hand
and its p.n. in the other, then humanity in general
will be routed with great slaughter.

In N.Q., not very long ago,
the D.C. Judge on circuit dined
heavdy over-night, and afterwards
taught some real lydies how to
dance “ Ta-ra-ra boom-de-ay ”
then prevalent. Next morning,
when the hour for opening court
arrived, his Honor was not out of
bed, and sent word to the old
bailiff who acted as clerk, to pub
the clock back 10 minutes. VV hen
the timepiece recovered her lost
ground, the bailiff came out on
the court-house verandah, where
he had a good view of the Judge’s
pub., and, seeing no appearance,
he mounted a chair and shoved
the big hand back another lOmin.
He went through this performance
thrice ere the Judge turned up
and opened court at “ ten o’clock
sharp.” The feelings and remarks
of 60 jurymen, many of whom n'ad ridden 20
miles on horseback that morning to attend the
court, can be dimly guessed at.

P. Durack, who pegged out his claim in
eternity at Perth (W.A.), some weeks ago, was
the pioneer who opened up the Cooper country
round Wmdorah (Q.). In the sixties he over-
Janded thither from Goulburn (N.S.W.) and
settled on dhylungra station. In recent years,
somewhat cramped in fortune, Durack took up
large areas in Westralia, and once more accumu-
lated wealth.
John Norton should bulk largely in the
history of Sydney City Council during the next
few years. The Question is—Will he transform
the Council’ or will it transform him? One can
hardly imagine the pugnacious and purposeful
political pigmy (how easily alliteration associates
itself with the subject) whittled down to the calm
kerb-and-gutter level, and heaving in front of
him the orthodox aldermanio stomach.

Dennistoun Wood has received a send-
off from the Victorian legal profession on his
pel nnnent departure for Tasmania. Thirty-
ive years ago, or more, he was a commanding
figure at the Vic. bar, on equal terms with Michie,
Ireland, Dawson, and almost with Fellows, being
also a mighty obstreperous man in reactionary
polities; by the side of the lawyers of that day,
ere it was fashionable for the robe to he Liberal.
Then he went to London, and proved him elf well
in the second line. His return to M-lb. was un-
fortunate, like the trip of Sergeant S.eight From a
dashing, ardent man, Wood has inev it: bly become
a knotty, gnarly trunk. It goes without saying
that he is an unsurpassed talker—off the boards.”

nals of scribbling and pushing the sale of his book,
dabbles a little in such “ sciences ”as p dmistry
Recently he visited the Isis, a canefield district
where the red volcanic soil makes contrast with

Saumarez Smith has come back an Arch-
bishop, and says he doesn’t personally care a
brass button for the title as a merely personal
ornament. Unto which the ungodly say “ Rats ! ”
for S.S. is just the sort of daisy to 'bloom the
better for such titular manure. And his growth
and blossom, and odor, won’t be in the least
affected by the reflection that this is just the sort
of thing that is killing the Anglican Church in
Australia. The democratic spirit resents the im-
portation of such worn-out trivialities of worn-out
lands. The only good point about the matter is
bmith s intention to add Cd. to his cab-fares in
order to keep up the arc hi episcopal dignity. *

LA6ER
Aboriginalities .
“ In answer to Alex. M., re the lizard, com-
monly known as the iguana, or goanna, the Aus-
tralian Museum natural history department has
adopted the popular name ‘ goanna,’and has, I
think, so labelled some specimens on view there at
present. Goanna is certainly not aboriginal; it is
only a corruption of iguana. But, as Alex. M.
says, the lizard in question is not an iguana at all,
so the name goanna, being distinctive, is to be
taken as original and adopted, although for some
time those who use it may expect to be accused of
illiteracy. The correct name, I believe, is ‘lace-
lizard.’ E.F.

“ JBilly the Joyful ” :
A big Q. cattle-station is the property of a bank, [and
during a recent protracted drought the manager writes
for permission to shift stock while cattle-routes are yet
open. After months of waiting the permission arrives,
whereupon the manager wires—“ Five nionths too late.
Affairs now desperate here. If no rain immediately,
frightful losses.” A few days later the long-expected
deluge occurs, and another wire goes through “ Splendid
rain ; creeks over the plains ; expect now to save half the
herd.” Message from head-quarters in reply—“ Muster
immediately all fat stock for Adelaide market * Bank
policy of speedy realisation in a nutshell.

N. Queensland advfc. :
T OST,—BAY HORSE ; age, 13; used to'ecclesiastical
G work f or the last six years; of no value to any-
body but the owner. Apply Vicarage.
Thirteen years old—and for six of them plodding
sadly in front of the poor parson and his family ;
scanning the horizon for fine Sundays in hopes
that a good collection might give him a feed of
real corn ; dutifully wearing his shoes longer than
any other horse in the neighborhood, and accentuat-
ing with a prayer every fresh paten in the harness;
broken-kneed, spavined, wind-galled, strained—
and (what surplusage !) “ of no value to anybody
but the owner.” For of such is the Kingdom of

Dear Bulletin, —There was another case
of Lynch law in N.Q., before Normanton was the
port of the Norman River, and the camp was at
Burketown. A negro cook at the only hotel was
charged with stealing nuggets, dust, photos, &c.
A suspended camp-oven-was banged with the
back of an axe to collect the boys. The boys
rolled up. The negro had a camphor-wood chest
—a .rare article then up that way, but he hhd
come round by steamer, and had been a sea-cook.
He refused to give up his keys. “Daddy ” ordered
the chest to be split open. The lost goods were
found. As in the Cloncurry case, a jury was em-
pannelled, the negro being allowed right of chal-
lenge. Found guilty. Also found that he had no
monev. Subscription. A4O got. _ Negro ordered
to leave the camp at sparrow-chirp. Court ad-
journed. . . . Sparrow-chirp : Negro found lying
drunk with two empty bottles (rum) beside him.
Refused to shift. Said six blanky white men
could not shift him. Stockwhips to the rescue.
Flick! Nigger howled. Flick! Flick! Flick.
Nigger arose and ran ; followed and flicked for
three or four miles. —Dipso.

J.M.L. : Last summer, padding the
hoof along the Southern line with a fox-terrier
slut and two pups, which latter I carried in a
billycan, I camped one snaky day by a creek, and
I and the slut went down for a swim, leaving the
pups on the ground with the swag. When igo
back a big diamond snake had swallowed one pup
and had started on t’other. I killed the reptile
and chopped the puppy out, much scared but
otherwise O.K. That pup I sold to a cocky later
on as a prize pup, being, I solemnly swore, out of
“Reptile ”by Tomahawk. I vebeen by his place
again this year and he told me this : The pup
grew up all right, but had always a slippery,
wriggly way with him and would swallow steaks
and 8 chops without chewing them. They missed
him at the beginning of the winter and never laid
eyes on him till spring, when one day the cocky
saw a strange-looking thing creeping out of a
hollow tog. It was the pup. He had slept the
winter through like a snake.

Dear Bulletin — .Be fly-bitea. So far,
none of your correspondents have come within
miles of the fortune I maintain awaits the man
who finds an effectual cure for bunged-eye. The
tobacco “ cure ”is an old and effectless one. I’ve
tried it in small doses as “ A.R.8.D.” suggests;
and I’ve also jammed a half-a-plug into the eye
immediately I felt the itching, and nearly burnt
JOHN NORTON.
Photo., Beavis Broa.
it out. But in vain; the bung closed it
just the same. “ Tweed-Riyer-J’s ” hot water
fomentation, as well as cold water linseed,- bread-
poultice, mashed potatoes, dough, raw steak, and
all manner of eye-waters also failed. Being a
particular favorite of the mysterious insect, I ve
tried nearly every so-called remedy without suc-
cess. Doctors I have consulted have coolly ad-
vised me “to wait till the eye went down again
on its own,” as they knew of no other remedy^
Be the flv itself, / have seen and felt A.R. B.D s
fly bite my hands and arms. The bite resembled
the quick prick of a needle. But the place never
swelled, nor itched. You never see the bunging-
insect settle on or leave your eye, and never feel
the actual bite. Sometimes I doubt whether
the whole biz is caused by a fly or other insect at
all. In cultivation districts the pest is far worse
than elsewhere. Harvest fields are the places to
look for bimged-eyes. Out back on the Darling,
Paroo, Warrego, and N.W. border of Queensland,
where’ ordinary flies swarm, there is very little
bung-eye.-R.lt., Narandera.

Ed. B. —Re “ Never Never.” The phrase
was not used by men who worked over W.Q. in my
time (a score of years ago), ft was always “ Out-
side.” Seventeen years ago I met a stockman who
told me he was going “ inside ” for a spree. His
idea of “inside” was Stony Point (now' Wm-
dorah) on the Cooper.— R. Fairly, Gladstone.
* * * *
Who says the days of “lambing-down”
are done? T’other day at Bunclaberg (Q.) the
cook of a labor-vessel took up his quarters at a
certain hotel and handed his cash (£4B) to the
publican for safe keeping. At the end of a riotous
six days the joyous Bacchanalian was informed
that his money was “done.” According to evi-
dence for defence the plaintiff (for naturally afore-
said cook kicked up a rumpus and appealed to the
law) had shouted expensive liquors for all and
sundry ; had given the barmaid the price of a new
dress—to say nothing of a beautiful ring and
other trifles. Also, at the close of every day. (de-
fendant admitted with truly charming simplicity)
the price of champagne shouted was duly de-
ducted from plaintiff’s little deposit—and all
around was lush, fresh additions to the till, and
general joy in the House of Bung ! Wherefore
it will be easily seen that that .£4B must have
dwindled down very low in tie course of six days.
An unsympathetic Bench, however, gave a verdict
for some 12 guineas in favor of plaintiff—to say
nothing of multitudinous costs —and a shout of
virtuous triumph rent the boozy welkin.
* * * *
Way back in the “ sixties, ” around a station
near Breeza, the plains for miles were covered
with what are known as “ rolly-poleys,” and the
crows were in swarms. When shearing was over,
two young fellows—lrishman “Big Mick” and
Englishman Harry—set out on foot for Breeza
station. They had two water-bags, but both were
emptied by the time they got within 20 miles of
Breeza, and “Big Mick” was utterly “done.”
His mate induced him to pull off his boots and
make one more effort, but he dropped again at 15
miles. Harry then left him, and made all haste
to the nearest house he knew to get a quart
of water ; found the place deserted, and, crawling
on hands and knees, made for the well. The
bucket and rope were gone, so he tore off his shirt,
made a rope, tied the quart to it, and drank till
ho could drink no more. Then he started back
for his mate with a single quart, got two miles,
stumbled with exhaustion, and spilt the water.
Back he went again—refilled, made another start,
and this time reached Mick, but found him dead,
with Ins eyes already picked out. —F.C.K.

well. Van with two lady passengers

deaf. Heavy teamster gets small one

Teamster’s faithful dog gets teeth into

guage, heat and gore. Caretaker of

The farewell supper given to artist Percy
Spence— whose portrait is printed herewith—by his
friends and brother artists was presided over by
the versatile ex-Premier Dibbs. In the course of
the speech in which he proposed the health ot the
guest of the evening, the burly ex-politician
denied any pretensions to the dignity ot art-
critic. He was simply an art-lover, and as such
he admired Mr. Spence both as a man and as an
artist, and deeply regretted the lack of pubho
encouragement which made it necessary tor so
cultured an exponent of Australian art to leave
them for the old country, where, however, he
hoped Mr. Spence would stay till he topped
the tree. Incidentally, the chairman remarked
that the victim of the valedictory effusion
had recently painted a portrait of Mrs. Ibbbs,
which, in his (the speaker’s) opinion, could literally
speak. The chairman concluded his speech with
all sorts of complimentary references to Mr.
Spence, and every variety of good wishes for his
future. There were present most of Sydney
artists, and many of them joined in basting e
liar ting guest in what appeared to them the most
agreeable manner. It may be a matter of mere

“SUBPLUS.”
The term ‘‘surplus ” is applied, in life insurance
affairs, to the balance, ascertained periodically,
between the assets and the liabilities. Out of this
balance or surplus, distributions of profits are
made to the policy-holders entitled to participate.
The natural tendency of distribution is to diminish
the surplus for the moment; but in the case of a
prosperous company, the decrease is speedily more
than covered by accessions of profit from various
sources. It is clear, however, that the more
liberal the periodical distributions of profits, the
more gradual will be the growth of the surplus.
Conversely, if a life insurance company withho ds
from its policy-holders the full benefit of the pro-
portion of profits immediately available, and
prefers to accumulate in the interest of future
participants, it follows that the surplus will in-
crease more rapidly in proportion. Of course it
is easy to keep in hand more than the future will
require, under any possible circumstances; and,
when that course is followed, injustice to present
participants is inevitable.
Now, The Mutual Life of New Yofikj while
careful to make abundant reserves for future
bonuses, lias always been equally careful to dis-
tribute to its policy-holders the full immediate
benefits w'hich they are entitled to claim. The
Company has never sacrificed the interests of
policy-holders entitled to immediate participation,
to the formation of an imposing but empirical
surplus, never likely to be required even in the
remotest future. The surplus shown by The
Mutual Life at the end of 1896 was =£6,105,444.
It is a steadily increasing surplus, which, need-
less to say, is more than ample for all the
demands for which it has been created.
But, supposing the Company had, throughout
its history, acted on the principle which
has been adopted in, at least, one prominent
instance—of reserving a large proportion of the
surplus at every distribution, and, in addition,
reducing the surrender-values allowed for discon-
tinued policies, what does the reader suppose
would have been the amount of The Mutual’s
surplus in 1896 ? Not six millions, but upwards of
24 millions sterliny! This is not guesswork. The
figures are based upon the official report issued by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, which gives annually the exact record of
every company doing business in that State.
Since itsorganisatiori, Tiie Mutual Life has paid
in bonuses and surrender values to its policy-holders
.£44,445,244, which is more than twice the amount
paid for similar items by the next largest company.
Observe, then, what a magnificent surplus The
Mutual could have exhibited to-day, had an
illiberal policy been pursued in relation to the
assured —a surplus exceeding that of any other
office by more than 150 per cent. But, fortunately,
the Company preferred to sacrifice a mere useless
and vainglorious display to the solid interests of
the policy-holders. Who shall say that, in taking
thiscourse, the Mutual Life of New York has
not chosen the better part? and that, in so doing,
it has not well earned the high reputation which
it has attained in every part of the world as “ the
Policy-holders’ C 0.,” “ the Co. which does the most
good ? ” The intelligent reader will not allow him-
self to be misled by the parading of sums of no
use to the present generation, and in excess of
any possible needs of the generations to come,*


WOMEN IN COURT.
During the present sittings of the Central Criminal Court, his
Honor was obliged to ask one of the witnesses, an elderly woman, a certain
question, and he remarked that he experienced some delicacy in doing so in
the presence of so many women, old and young. He added that he could
not understand respectable women sitting and listening to evidence of the
kind.
Elderly Witness (hearing rather imperfectly) : “ Ah ! my son, you’ll have to
ask someone older than me ! ”

The Christian Endeavorers object to the
Sunday opening of Sydney Zoo. “ Two of
a trade,’’ Ac.
The officials of the Sydney Zoo are to wear specially-designed
uniforms for Sundays. The design will be a cross between a
bobby’s uniform and a parson’s garb.
“ Once more the detectives have attacked
the famous Collingwood (Melb.) tote-shop.
They had to batter down successive iron-
studded doors and run the gauntlet of
fierce dogs specially trained to deal with
policemen.”
An armor-plated, police-proof tote-shop is
now in course of construction.

IN STRANGE.
clothes under NEW CONDITIONS.
Some of the bundles of clothes sent
by charitable people in the city to the
distressed districts in Gippsland after the
bush fires were not -acceptable (says.
Melbourne Argus). Theyvwere sadly wanting in several essen-.
tials. In the first place, they were not clean, and, in the" secondt]
place, th.ev were of a character unknown in the bush. " ' "
At Thorpdale a, bundle of left-foot boots, mostly women’s,
was sent up for distribution* on the assumption, doubtless, that the
community was largely composed of people with only one leg.
Several persons who follow the occupation of rail-splitting have sud-
denly come forth in all the glory of frock-coats and bell-toppers, and
one or two have made an appearance in uniforms formerly worn
either by the Salvation Army or by letter-carriers. In another dis-
trict a person answering to the sobriquet “ Tiger Ned ” may be seen
abroad in the garb of the church ;• but an Irishman who has been
driven to wear a kilt sent up by some benevolent Highlander may
be recorded as an object of pity.

Theatre Royal, 28th July, 1883.
I congratulate you on your successful introduction of
the HAPSBURG iron-framed Pianofortes. I have used
several of them in different towns, and have invariably
found them all that could be desired in a first-class Piano-
forte. Being extremely powerful, sweet, and clear in
tone, the touch is elastic and sympalhetic ; and, although
tuned above concert-pitch (a test which few Pianos will
stand), I can always depend upon the HAPSBURG

Sir WILLIAM C. ROBINSON, Governor of South Aus-
tralia, has given his unqualified approval of the HAPS-
BURG PIANOS, after careful examination. This being
duly reported in the two Adelaide papers, a storm of cor-
respondence ensued from jealous competitors. The good
opinion of the talented composer of “Unfurl the Flag”
may well be highly esteemed.

From M. HENRY KOWALSKI, the talented composer
of the well-known “ Marche Hongroise ” and others, a
member of the Jury of Class 13 (musical instruments),
Centennial Exhibition, Melbourne
Sydney, 18th May, 1890.
In answer to your inquiry concerning the qualities of
the HAPSBURG PIANOS, I will say that they possess a
good inusical tone, easy touch, and can occupy a superior
stand amongst the great number of instruments imported
into Australia. I consider that they are durable, their
mechanism showing every element which constitutes a
careful work and conscientious making.
I remain, Sir, yours truly,
HENRI KOWALSKI.

Dear B.—Hearing that Sydney T. and 0.
Journal, of 26th Feb., had got a spirited illustra-
tion of the boats leaving the foundering Atacama,
I was tickled to find that what the block really
represents is a German ship, in good order and
condition, hove-to, and sending a boat to rescue
castaways from (apparently) Spanish vessel, El
Paso, of Corunna. The fakeinan forgot to take
the lettering off the boat’s stern. Thus does
“ great” journalism rise to the occasion.— Alex.
M.
On a nearly-played-out subject :
Editor Bulletin, — Your correspondent “ Maori ” adds
his little perjury to the old gag about the discovery of a
decayed corpse in the bath of a London hotel. He fears
that the “untravelled Australian” may “miss the
point ” of that ancient chestnut, so he wades in to make
it quite clear how all the screed you have lately published
about the personal uncleanliness of the hated Britisher is
mere spiteful tommy-rot, and no one knows it better than
the travelled Bulletin, but part of the mission of the
'* Bushman’s Bible”is to further swell the noddle of the
“ native ”by leading him to believe he is civilised. Evi-
dently “ Maori ” was not a social success in London, and
so when he reads the legend of the corpse in the London
bath (it used to be a French bath) he uses it as a peg on
which to hang his grudge against the Britisher at his
hotel w'ho glared at him stolidly and said “ Haw 1”
just that and nothing more —when “ Maori ” had
chipped into the conversation with a friendly “By
cripes, any o’ yous coves goter match ? ” It’s a
way the Britisher has of snubbing outsiders, but
it doesn’t prove that he objects to wash himself.
As to the 4x2 dungeon bathroom “Maori” mentions.
What a “ri-a-rally” with the screw-jacks it must have
taken to get George Reid into his bath at the Hotel
Cecil 1 Perhaps “ Maori ” has got his “ princely caravan-
serai ” mixed up with his fourpenny cubicle at one of the
huge model lodging-houses. The mistake is excusable,
but if he wished_ to gratify his yearning for a matutinal
or any other “ dip ” he could have done so in any part of
London —not “ 2s. to 6s. a go,” but for the modest 2d.
that would have procured him use of a clean bathroom,
two clean towels, new cake of soap, brush and comb,
boot-jack, flesh-brush and mirror with hot or cold water
as desired ; or for the same money he could have had a
swim in a carefully-warmed swimming-bath in any parish
in London. Can you get that in any part of any city in
Australasia? In view of the extreme cleanliness of the
native is it not remarkable that so many Australian hash-
shops proudly include a bath in their trinity of luxuries —
“ bath, piano, and latch-key.” No need to advertise such
an item in London, as the blawsted Britisher takes it for
granted that the “tub” is included. Again, if the Lon-
doners, as your fatuous correspondent asserts, have such
a horror of water, how does he explain the fact that hun-
dreds of men and boys go in the early morning to bathe
at the Serpentine lake, the Highgate and Hampstead
ponds, &c., all through the winter when they have to
break the ice to get in ? The dirtiest dog will bathe out
here in the summer, not for cleanliness but for coolth.
You have given the native plenty of space on the subject;
don’t you think it’s time you gave the no-account
Britisher a show' ?—C.C.

been a martyr to acute rheumatism for many

Cure. The second bottle added considerably

QUITE TAME.—A CRICKET ITEM.
Even the children can play with him now!
The Convention’s latest compromise on
the railway question is a painful case of trying to
wobble in two places at once. It boils down to
these six propositions :
(1) Each State may make any railway-rates it likes;
(2) at the same time, the Federal Parliament may make
laws to prevent any State charging rates which are
injurious to the trade of another State ; (3) all the same,
if the rates are necessary to make the railways of that
particular State pay, they mav not. be interfered with ;
(4) but, if they prevent the railways of some other State
paying, the matter may be further reconsidered : (ft) a
Certain amount of latitude, in addition to any lutitude
above provided, shall be allowed, if th.e special' rates are
necessary to develop the territory of the State, provided
they don t hinder the development of any other State ;
(fi) a commission shall be appointed to do all these
things, and it shall have such powers of doing them as
Parliament may decide.

The Federal delegates, last week, solemnly
msnlved to “recognise the Supreme Being in the
Constitution.” Which was, doubtless, very kind
of them. At the same time, if the pious person
really understood the immense gulf between the
Omnipotent and a transient delegate named
Henry Briggs, he would leave the matter alone.
1 lie idea of a Deity being recognised by Henry
Biggs has some elements of the grotesque.

The N.S.W. Law Dept, having decided to
proceed no further with the Carruthers-Norton
libel-suit, the report that Minister Carruthers will
resign is again in the air. If Carruthers had let
things alone, on the ground that his opponent was
too small an insect for notice, all might have been
well. But he asked a jury to give him a clear
certificate of character and didn’t get it, and now
the abandonment of the case may be taken to
imply that there is no visible chance of getting it.

The sending by the Australian Premiers
of that direct message of sympathy over the
Maine disaster has been a shock to the right-
thinking persons who shrink in such a superiah
mannah from direct cable communication with
America which they fear might contaminate
Australia, but which Australia is bound to have
ere long. These are the people who affect to be
only officially aware of the Great Republic’s exist-
ence, and object to turn a large dull eye upon any
American intelligence that hasn’t first been Bull-
dozed.
Newcastle (N.S.W.) Chamber of Com-
merce has resolved (and informs the Federal Con-
vention of the fact) that it would be detrimental
to the interests of the Commonwealth if the
appeal to the English Privy Council were
abolished, and litigants could get a final decision
m Australia instead of having to stagger after it
to the far end of the globe. The Bulletin hears
that there were six people present at the meeting
and they were equally divided on tin- subject, the
noble sentiment about the best interests of our
country being carried only by the casting-vote of
the cnau man.
At a recent “ purification of the rolls ” in
Melbourne, the usual police-official solemnly at-
tested that certain electors had left. “ I didn’t
visit Catherwood’s house myself,” he admitted ;
“ I got a man to go round for me.” Man found
and questioned. “ No, I didn’t call at Cather-
wood’s house myself,” he also admitted ; “ I asked
a man if Catherwood had left, and he said ‘Yes.’
I don’t know the name of the man.” So the Vic.
elector is deprived of his vote now because an
alleged man, name unknown, is said to have told
another man that he has quitted the district, and
the other man related the tale to a policeman.■
Eight years ago the Australian Natives’
Association held in Melb. a deadly-dull inter-
provincial convention ”to promote Federation ;
and a pretty bad kind of curse seems to have
alighted upon it. The then newly-knighted Bray
was president ; his brain weakened, and he died.
Cither S. A. delegates were the unfortunate Paris
Nesbit; J. C. I. Johnson, who has disappeared
from politics ; Barrett, about whom the less said
the better ; and Tucker, who has gone into the
Adelaide Hospital chairmanship, which is worse
than gaol Of N.S.W. men were the lately-
deceased Ninian Melville and B. 15. Nichol and
M. S. Dowel; all three have lost their seats in
Parliament. Two others ha ve failed to get seats.
Of the Victorians, though most of the delegates
were in Parliament or were political aspirants, the
only one now in the House is Peacock.
“ A word for Australia.” In one of the
angry, wordy provincial battles which were of
such frequent oecnrr. nee between the big guns of
N. and Victoria in the Convention struggle
re warring rail way-rates, it was only the presence
of the amused and sometimes piously-pained non-
combatants from the land of S!eep-a-Lot and S. A.
that prevented Reid, Turner and 00. from getting
down to the first principles erstwhile acted on by
Crick, Samson Haynes and Ginger Clarke in the
homely N.S.W. Assembly. After the row had
cooled off the wily fat men of the parties jumped
in to take back a lot they had said. “I know,”
drawled Surplus Reid. “ that the hon. Vic. dele-
gates, in their determined measures and vigorous
antagonism to my learned friend’s amendment,
were actuated, and I don’t hesitate to say it, by
what they believe to be and what probably are
absolutely the best interests of their own pro-
vince.’ And so far have the Vic. tpn removed
themselves from the arena of true Federal ambi-
tion that there was not one man amongst them to
throw the compliment back at Reid’s head, and
reply that it was the best interests of Australia
they were considering.

The horse-flies of politics get on best by
continuing to blister their prey. Look at Q.
Home -Secretary -cum- Railways Dickson. He
went into Pari t. as an “ Independent ” (having
downed the Ministerial candidate for Bulimba),
sat on the cross - benches, and mosquitoed the
Ministry with dreary, woe-begone, polysyllabic
speeches, sermons cautions, and denunciations.
Dickson’s strong point in oratory is that he won’t
use one sufficient word when he can sausage-string
six unnecessary ones upon it, and that he will
never use a one-syllable word when he can think
of a two-er ; or a two-r-r, if he has got a three-er ;
and so on. After getting fly-bitten with Dickson,
Nelson offered him a portfolio, which D. grabbed
with both hands. And now he is Home-Secre-
tary, the most important Minister next to the
Premier himself !
The Deity has at last received local habita-
tion and home in the Australian Commonwealth
Bill. When Gov. Phillip founded the settlement
of Botany Bay, he rejected overtures made by the
Fleet parson to have the name of God associated
with the establishment of the province. The chap-
lain of the day, writing about it, complains that
he was officially ignored. The soldiers were
drawn out, the flags run up, the proclamation
read, and cheers and volleys of musketry followed,
“ but all the time,” wrote the parson to the Secre-
tary of State, “I was left to stand under the shade
of a tree, and was made to feel that neither God
nor I was wanted at the foundation of the new
nation.” One hundred and ten years later the
parson, it seems, has been invited to come from
under that tree.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Five days before the last test-match ended,
Stoddart expressed his determination to speak his mind
about the Australian barracker. And when the last test-
match ended, and England came out of it severely
whipped, Stoddart raised a Ions? and piteous howl about
the beastly barracker. Cricketers, as Tub Bulletin has
often remarked, are mere overgrown schoolboys. “To be
a cricketer” (says Phil Sheridan, and he ought to know),
“ one requires very little brains, and that little must be
entirely devoted to cricket.” Hence cricketers, when
beaten by the other side, and, in addition, barracked and
laughed at by the crowd, are inclined to become demon-
strative. No schoolboy can stand being “ barracked ” or
" laughed at.’ There is always a “ nasty ” section in every
crowd, and Stoddart, like Ranji and the rest, was too great
a schoolboy to be able to treat the “ nasty section ” with
the contempt it deserves. Mr. Stoddart 'must really not
get angry because, when he misses a catch, the crowd
laughs at him. And Ranji, who is the “flashest”
cricketer this paperever saw, must not take on so theatri-
cally because the crowd laughs and chuckles when he
misses a catch and says such words as “butter-fingers”
and “gum.” As for the Tom-Garrett-agony side of the
question, that ancient, plucky war-horse should have
been “chivvied” out of cricket long ago. Cricket is a
game for boys ; not for elderly civil-servants in receipt of
large salaries.
Onesuperstition
received severe punish-
ment in the closing test-
match is the idea that the
Britisher dies hard. Noth-
ing could have been much
softer than the English
acceptance of approaching
defeat. Bad temper, bad
discipline, and hail mo-ale
were all conspicuous.
During the last 20min.
they pulled, up a littie,
anil went to a certain
death with something like
decency, hut up to that
point, their fielding was
in wretched contrast to
their own former efforts.
The failure was ventral
and comprehensive. M‘-
Laren and Briggs «ere
bowled for ducks. Ranji
failed each time. Hearne
kept a very poor length,
for him, and only got two
wickets. Richardson lost
his length, his temper,
and his head. Druce and
the two Yorkshiremen
collapsed in the s>cond
innings, Mason in botii.
Catches were missel ail
round, even by Briggsaml
Ranji. Hayward and
Storer alone played up
gamely, stopping the rot
in each innings, and doing
fairly reliable work in the
field. They, too, each
missed critical chances
once, and they were the
best of a bad bunch.
The complacent N.S.W. theory about the re-
versal of form by the Englishmen is that it was “ the new
bowling” (/.e,, Noble and Howell) that paralysed them
in the later test-matches after their easy win in the first.
Facts don’t bear it out. In the Britishers’ initial effort
five men made nearly 500 of the total scored. Of the
twelve players who have taken part in the “tests” none
of the other seven have ever made a serious appearance,
save for the temporary emergence into efficiency of Druce
and Wainwright for one innings each in the last en-
counter. The five men who did score then have scored,
more or less freely, ever since off the “ new ” bowling as
readily as off the old. They have never happened to all
five come off together, and the remaining seven (who
failed when Noble and Howell were not howling) have
failed with sorrowful consistency ever since.
The real secret of the failure of the team is the
reduction in the number of its reliable bats, after the
style of Gideon's army, and the expectation of five men to
play an Australian eleven and win “on their own.” On
the one occasion on which they all five came off, they did
win. In every other match, two or three of them have
struck snags, and with them the team. In the last
match, the new bowlers didn't take an important wicket.
But three of the five men failed in the second innings,
and the rest was silence. Wanted, a travelling team of
(say) 16 players, from which eight or nine men in foun
can always be picked for test-matches.
In the test-match, just over, four “crack”
amateur bats made 138 runs in the first innings. The
four professional bats made 184. Seeing that all of the
latter have some additional recommendation besides
hatting (one keeps wicket and all howl), the comparison
in their favor is sufficiently striking to he discomforting
to “gentlemen” players. In the second innings, the
difference in performance was still more marked. The
amateurs made 41, the pros. 87. Hayward was top scorer,
Storer second, Briggs (another pro.) third. Apart from
M’Laren’s contributions, the professionals have all through
supplied what element of reliability there has been in
the team. Hayward has now, iu four successive innings,
had to check the panic which always seems to have set in
on M’Laren's downfall, and has done it to the tune of 50
and 60 eaoli time. Wainwright has proved a sudden suc-
cess as first man after a series of failures by an amateur.
Storer and Hirst are two of the three “ aggressive ” bats
of the combination. What price a team of all profes-
sionals next time? Or pros. “ assisted by Mr. M'Laren ” ?
Abel could replace Ranji, and Brown and Ward the two
young amateurs. With a specialist hat like Gunn in
place of “all-round ” Hirst or Wainwright (whose howling
is of no value in Australia), and a specialist bowler like
Meades or Mold in place of Briggs, it would be a far
harder crowd to beat than this one.
* * * *
The fifth test-match was “anybody’s game”
until one man—the only man of the whole crowd—began
to hit out vigorously. Then it was h.is game.
• * * *
It is as ridiculous for England’s harrackers to
deny the all-round superiority of Australian cricket in
the test-matches as for Australian harrackers to ussert
that the Englishmen batted (or bowled for that matter)
up to average All-England “form.” The daily press
hereabouts shows its bias by attributing all the failure of
the visitors’hatting to the excellence of the Australian
bowling and fielding, and all the success of the Australian
batting to the excellence of the men who made the
runs. There is not a more boyishly-biassed barracker
than Melb. Age, which fed the mob outside its
office, last week, with special announcements that
M’Laren and other Englishmen had been “missed”
in the field whilst making their little lot but did not
record the occasional “ let offs” of Darling and co. Next
year an Australian eleven will be going' to England, and
will, probably, get badly beaten from time to time.
The Age and other harrackers will refer to those defeats
as “ unaccountable.” Well, in the light of the last Aus-
tralian eleven performances at Lords’ and Kensington
Oval, the recent crushing defeats of Stoddart and co. are
likewise “ unaccountable.” After the first day’s play in
the final test, 15 English wickets fell for about 210 runs,
and only two of them made more than 30.
* * * *
Combo : In view of recent utterances of English
cricketers I suggest that the following new rules for the
conduct of the game of cricket be got ready in time for
next season :
1. The members of the public who are not English bar-
raekers must preserve the strictest silence during the
progress of the game.
2. Any person, not an English barracker, wishing to
speak must first obtain a written permit signed by all the
members of the English team.
3. The band will play “ God Save the Queen ” when-
ever (a) An Australian gets out ; (6) An Englishman
makes a run; (■•) An Englishman stops a fourer. The
public will thereupon rise and take off its hat, standing
reverently until the band stops. It can-then sit down.
4. At the conclusion of the day’s play, if it has been in
favor of the Englishmen, the public will take off its hat
and sing “ God Save the Queen ” and “ Rule Britannia.”
5. If it has not been in favor of the Englishmen the
band will play “The Dead March in Saul,” and the public
will proceed at slow march (and in silence) out of the
ground.
Special Notice.—No flies admitted : all flif.s found
on the ground will be shot, and their owners prose-
cuted.

A few facts from the last test-match : The
Englishmen scored units in 10 out of 22 batting attempts,
whereas the Australians had only 4 units among 17 indi-
vidual scores. Fourof the visitors—Ranji, Mason, Hearne,
and Richardson - averaged 51 runs per head for 8 innings.
Hayward and Druce between them scored a third of Eng-
land’s grand total, but their joint contribution of 172 was
2 runs less than Darling gave to the Australian side. No
other batsman on either side averaged more than 45.
Three Austialians—Darling, Jones, and Trumble-won
the match, whilst R'chardson was the only brilliant per-
former on the other side. When Darling began to thrash
Richardson he was practically licking the English team.
S.A. cricketers are arranging a big testimonial
to Clem. Hill, the boy batting prodigy. It is to be pre-
sented to him on the first day of the match, S.A. v. Eng-
land, which happens, appropriately enough, to be his
21st birthday—March 18.
» * * *
The English team’s light went out with the old
year. Up to the end of December they had nine indivi-
dual scores of a “century” and upwards in first-class
matches, and since then they have made only two.

would remind him of the many friends he had made in

* • * *
Centuries galore in Sydney electorate cricket on
Saturday. Alee. Mackenzie ran up 137 ; young Trumper,
191, not out; Conningham, 151; Youll, 102, not out; and
Noonan, 126.
* * * •
A man who has seen all the big matches of the
Stoddart tour puts Briggs and Richardson first in point
of uniform popularity with the crowds-anyhow, with
the crowd that barracks—with Druce and Storer at the
other end. The last is natural enough, no doubt, because,
as wicketkeepers, their duty has been to appeal oftenest
against the Australians.
• ♦ * *
Ayrshire’s preparation for Australian Cup was
decidedly unorthodox, the horse never having been sent
for any gallop beyond a mile. His success, however, will
not stop future Cup candidates being galloped to death
against the watch.
• * • *
Aurum did very well in running third for the
Australian Cup under the record weight of 9st. 31b. for a
three-year-old, but the performance was not as brilliant
as some people made it out to be. Old Battalion gave the
colt (ill)., and a beating equal to at least another 61b., on
weight calculations ; nor would Aurum have got a place
if Wait-a-Bit hadn’t shut up when pinched. One thing
seems certain. The St. Albans’ crack has not gone on
improving since the Spring.
* » * *
Aleck Taylor’s followers must have had about
enough of the handsome deceiver, Wait-a-Bit.
• * • *
Resolute’s easy capture of the Bourke Handicap
(7 furlongs) was gall and wormwood for any punters who
had backed him this time. At the finish of the more
important flutter, Resolute was in great trouble, whilst
Amiable was winning comfortably, and appearances cer-
tainly didn’t suggest that W. R. Wilson’s horse could
have overtaken the leaders if the course had been a
furlong longer. But when Amiable, Resolute, Reaper,
and Sailor Boy came to meet again in the Bourke Handi-
cap, it was Resolute’s turn to run away from the three
beaten sprinters who had previously seemed too fast for
him. The glorious uncertainty of the Turf gets very
monotonous.
* * * »
Fielder was serenely confident as to Amiable’s
ability to win the Newmarket. Just before the harrier
rose he said: “She’ll be in front from the jump, and
they’ll never sight her afterwards.” Amiable should do
well in England—a splendid mare, and one of the easy-
going sort. Very little femininity about her. As she
walked down the straight she looked like a stallion—in
fact, she’s the equine equivalent of the New Woman.
* * * *
Amiable seems to be the exception to the rule
that a horse—a mare, in this case—capable of staying six
furlongs down Flemington straight, at Newmarket
Handicap pace, may always he hacked to go a quarter of
a mile further. Judging from her performances m the
Bourke Handicap (7 furlongs) and again in the mile All-
Aged Stakes, the Sydney mare is a sprinter, pure and
simple.
• * * •
Whernside’s performance in the Steeplechase last
Saturday was a double record for the race, which dates
back to ’7O. Mr. Sep Miller’s big ’un carried 91b. heavier
weight than any previous winner, and cut out the three-
miles in nearly. 9seos. faster time, ah hough he had 30
lengths to spare at the finish.
* * * *
J. J. Miller’s Australian Racing Chronicle,
from Aug., 97, to Jan. 31, ’9B, was issued last week, in
time for “students of form” to take the hand}’ volume
to Flemington. Miller’s Chronicle is beginning to get
the patronage it deserves, and, with increased fervor,
The Bulletin herewith bestows another half-yearly
blessing on the publication.
• * * »
“Ranji” Barden went back to England per
Cuzco. British Barden was about the most extensively-
advertised and worst “ big” cyclist who ever struck Aus-
tralia. Here Barden has distinguished himself for nothing
but his copious talk, and, from the man’s style of riding,
it seems impossible to believe that he was’, only a few
months back, the best man in Europe. The only explana-
tion possible is that Barden talked at the other men all
the while he was riding, and made them feel as tired as
Australia did, and his method of plugging along with an
open mouth would appear to support this theory.
» * * *
The trot at Brighton (Sydney) on Monday last
was a terrible show. Lord Beldon trotted well for a
mile, and then came down to a gait about as fast as you
could kick your hat along. Silver Cloud, who was trot-
ting in good form, passed him and then broke in a most
unaccountable style ; Beldon again taking the lead. All
this time Viking Junior was galloping “on his own,”and,
turning into the straight the last time, the three were
all galloping and together. Silver Cloud passed the post
in front of Lord Beldon, but a protest was entered and
upheld, and the race awarded to Lord Beldon. Trotting
men seem to forget that Brighton is giving them the only
chance they are likely to get about Sydney for some time
to come.
* * * *
I he 22-footer Effie scored another win, on Satur-
day, from scratch, and it looks as if Joe Donnelly has set
himself a big task in undertaking to build one to heat
her. Even Mr. Sam Hordern’s money may not he able to
bring forth anything better, at home or abroad, hut,
champion or otherwise, it is to be hoped Mr. H. will be
able to show sufficient originality to drop across another
name than Bronzewing.
* • • ♦
Whernside’s victories are always popular. The
giant takes his weight, whatever it may he, to the
front as soon as possible, and gives his backers the sooth-
ing sensation of seeing him win all the way. The Miller
stable, by the bye, is said to have an even bigger rod than
Whernside in pickle.
* • * *
Old Dondi last week joined the illustrious com-
pany of broken-necked hurdlers and steeplechasers. In his
best days, Dondi had a habit of jumping “on the bias,”
and was a tempestuous beast, hut he matured into a
fairly straight and steady goer. Lately, after three years
of failures, he made such a miraculous recovery of lost
speed that he went to his doom top-weighted.
» • • *
“ The weights for the principal spring handi-
caps,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
“ show considerable respect for Nevvhaven 11. and Mer-
man, small regard for Daimio, and an utter disbelief that
Norton can jump four miles and a-half in the Grand
National. For the Lincolnshire straight mile Merman has
8.11; 41b. less for the extra quarter in the City and Sub-
urban, in which Newhaven 11. gives him a lb., and the
6-year-old Form ha- 6st. 121 b. In Chester Cup, 2) miles,
Newhaven 11. has 51b. below the top weight, Count
Schomberg, 9st. 51b. This is 101 b. more than Merman,
who is alone in the Jubilee, £3OOO, with Bst. 71b., and
should win it. If Daimio cannot come to the front with
lost. 101 b. in the £2500 steeple, he may be considered as
a fonr-stone worse horse than he was in Australia at his
best—though the English handicapper thinks him 101 h.
better than Norton. Whatever chance the antipodean
cracks have of scooping the prize pools of the Hats and
fences will, anyhow, be fairly seen in the season now
about to open.”

Arthur Cavill beat anything in Adelaide over
100yds. (fresh water) with his double overarm stroke, blit
in 500 and 1000yds., where d.o.s. would be too tiring, and
he had to drop down to the single overarm, he couldn’t
live with the Wares (Vic.), or even with some of the locals.
The Wares are wood-eutters, powerfully-built, but not
above medium height. The S.A. champion, M'Millan, is
unluckily out of swimming for a year with lung in-
flammation. He cut out lOOO.vds., in ’97, in 15.55, same
time as G. VVare won with this year. The Adclaideans
are disappointed with Cavill who, when he gave up half-
way in the 1000yds., was vigorously hooted.
At recent S. A. swimming-races a one-legged
man won a race with double-over-arm stroke, and aone-
armed man swam well also, but unplaced. The one-levger
is named Woodman ; only shrivelled stump of right thigh
remains to him, but he gels through the water at a great
pace. The one-armed youth, who looks like a West Indian
half-negro, swims side-stroke, with armless side up. The
3-ineh stump of arm moves like a fish’s fin, and the man
swims as if born in the water.
* • * *
The “ladies’ swimming race ” at Adelaide
Carnival was greeted bv hoodlumd >in present with a roar
of delight. Four nice girls, set. about 18, clad in
close-fitting singlets and knickers, reluctantly peeled off
overcoats in front of the crowd, and then all landed most
awful “ wallops ’ on the surface. Glossy black hair
became uyiy rats tails; the maidens swam a flurried, short,
jerky breast-stroke, which made their heads hob lip and
down like bi“ sea we jdy corks, and the unjLrallant crowd
just chortled with happiness. The damp darling’s were
met at the other end by total strangers, who put willing
arms round their clinging forms to lift them out. Adelaide
isn’t having any more “ ladies’ races.”
* * * * ,
Women mustered strongly at the Adelaide
Swimming Carnival ladies’ night. Men-swimmers wore
the scantiest costume —a little less than even cyclists use
and the sex watched their every movement lovingly.
Graceful, fair-haired N.S.W. Cavill fluttered many a
heart. “You can admire a man so much better, you
know, when you see a lot of him ! ”
* * * *
This year’s Champion Stakes race, with only
two starters, was a genuine sporting event. Aniberite
won by 20 lengths from Bundook in smin. 27Isec., which
was only 4sec. slower than record time. The Derby
winner’s gallop was evidently intended to suggest that
the scratching of Auruin had been a fortunate thing for
plunging punters.
* * * *
An Auckland (M.L.) hunter, Laddie, frightened
by an approaching train, cleared a concrete wall, ditch
and railway-line-total width, 41ft. Tall, but authenti-
cated.
In the case of the man and woman Wil-
liams, convicted of larceny by Sydney S.M.
Addison on Feb. 26, The Bulletin learns with
satisfaction that the decision had more to justify
it than originally appeared. The police record
shows that the female accused bears no good
character, and three convictions for drunkenness
stand against her. This, in the dubious circum-
stances, might be held sufficient to turn the
scale. The peculiar distinction between the
sentences awarded Williams and his wife seems
still unexplained, however ; and it is curious why,
if the police testimony to character was given in
open court, the Sydney Evening News reporter
did not note it.
The 1898 volume of the Year Book of
Australia is a trifle bigger than its predecessor,
and the stock of valuable information contained
between its two boards keeps growing larger. The
only thing Australian which the Year Book
doesn’t throw light upon, so far as the writer can
discover, is the question whether the laughing-
jackass eats snakes. Every other subject inside
this continent seems to be gathered into the com-
prehensive tome, which is a source of full, accu-
rate, and painstaking information on politics,
public and private finance, commerce, manufac-
tures, agriculture, building societies, banks, cricket,
football, horse and other varieties of sport, art,
science, geography, tariffs, and everything else.
And if the editor could see his way to state de-
finitely whether the laughing-jackass snakes
or not it would be complete.
Like a bombshell into many N.S.W.
homes will fall Judge (I. B. Simpson’s expressed
judicial uncertainty as to whether, when a clergy-
man is obtainable, marriage by a registrar is valid
unless the intending spouses sign a declaration
that they conscientiously object to be married by
a minister of religion. The prevailing notion has
been that the State, since it couldn’t undertake to
say which is the true faith, had provided a means
of civil contract which should be independent of
conflicting creeds. It is also commonly understood
that the Marriage Act provides that any marriage
shall be binding which both parties, at the time
of contraction, honestly believe to be valid. If
the law is as Judge Simpson asserts, how is it that
in so many cases the necessary declaration has not
been called for by the officiating registrar ?
Paris Ne.sbifc is not mad on law points.
His first act on reaching Adelaide Asylum for the
second time (whence he was transferred from gaol)
was to point out to medical-officer Cleland that, as
a certificate was not forwarded with him from the
gaol, his further detention was a common-law
offence under the Lunatics Act. “ Keep me here
another hour,” sidd Nesbit, “ and you are liabla
to two years’ gaol! ” Cleland’s hair commenced
to stand on end. “ I won’t prosecute you,” said
Nesbit; “in fact, I shall do no more than wish
you good evening,” and he walked out of the
building. The proceedings have been a farce
all through, and seem to again demonstrate
how Kingston’s colleagues can make asses
of themselves when the S.A. Premier is away.
Mr. Nesbit gave as his reason for his <£20,000
libel-action against S.A. Register, set down for
hearing early in March, that their par., headed
“Vagaries of a Q.C.,” spoilt his chance of the
Chief Justiceship. “For,” said he, “Way is
going to resign shortly ; neither Kingston nor Josh
Syinon will take the post, and who else is there
but myself? ” But the action will probably lapse.
Dr. Cleland certifies that Nesbit’s trouble mani-
fests itself in a tremendous excess of energy. Last
time he worked it off in the excitement of a
general election. But this time there’s no such
safety-valve, and apparently somebody has sug-
gested hard physical exercise, for Mr. Nesbit took
part the other day in a hunters’ competition, and
cleared his leaps gallantly—some of them with a
pipe in his mouth !
“SO LONG. RANJI!”
“Prince Ranjitsinhji, during his Australian visit, has
received some hundreds of admiring letters from impres-
sionable young ladies. A diamond pin fell out of one
recently received by him, which he bestowed upon the
waiter at his hotel.”
“ Prince Ranjit.sinhji’s early dismissal caused great dis-
appointment to t he large concourse whose interest seemed
to he principally centred upon the Prince making a large
score.”
“ Prince Ranjisinhji said that the team had been hooted
on every ground in Australia.”
Hoot! mon, ye ken the critic's howl
Is aye the lot of every hitter,
And why compare us to the owl,
Would not the cooing dove he fitter?
Ungrateful Prince ! and cold to boot,
Whose heart no billets doux have heated,
Let others tell the world we hoot—
You might more truly say we bleated !

Interesting Illustrated Catu/ogiie Posted Free upon application.
dbufrialist, to The Bulletin :
, “Tommy” Wells, doyen of Melbourne’s active press-
men, dropped dead in harness on Saturday niirht week.
He was a tood-natured old fellow who, at 67, was as
gentle and fresh-minded as a child, and there is not a man
on the Melb. press who does not sincerely regret his
demise. Tommy was a Sheffield man, who reached Melb.
in the early fifties, todrift through mining and everything
else to journalism. For 27 years he had been law-reporter
on the Argus. He was my mentor, reportorially. He
told me he never read over his copy. I heard the sub.
rating him for leaving out bits of words. Says Tommy,
in reply : “What’s a sub-editor for?” Weils’s serious
advice to me was, “never look at your stuff in the paper.
You get worried, make complaints, and get a bad charac-
ter in the office, where they set you down for a nuisance.
Tommy hardly ever wrote a line of comment on anything
throughout his long career. Facts were his stock-in-
trade.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
ft. Newtnah, the well-khowfi jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to tneir advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
The result of the Wolfe’s Schnapps £5O
test-match competition appears elsewhere in this
issue. The ingenuity of the people who gathered
many coupons together aud guessed “English, 1
wicket, 1 run,” “Australian, 1 wicket, 1 run,”
and so on seriatim, was duly rewarded; for in
course of coupons “Australians, 6 wickets, 2 runs ”
was reached, and that guess won. Unluckily,
there were several hundred ingenious or lucky
persons, who share the <£bo in terms of 2s. 9|d.
apiece or thereabouts. If the match had happened
to be won by an irregular number of runs, say 279,
probably nobody would have guessed right,*

Dear Bulletin, —Mr. E. R. Waite now
“ bosses ” the reptilian branch of the Australian
Museum, Sydney. Mr. Waite’s casts of Australian
snakes are marvellously life-like, and are emin-
ently creditable to him and his assistants. But
Mr. Waite will keep re-naming snakes for only
Joss knows what reason. Bor example, Krebt’s
common brown snake, (diemcnia supereiliosa )
becomes diemenia textilis. The tiger snake
{hoplocephalus curtus —Krefft) is Waitised into
notec,his scutatus ! Gould’s snake (hoplocephalus
(/ouldii-KreEt) is tortured into denisonia tjouldii.
The diamond snake (morelia spilotes —Krefft >
becomes python spilotes. And so on. What’s
the need or use or sense of it ?—H. J. M'Oooev.

An obviously verdant Sydney newspaper
correspondent asserts that the extent of Sydney
hotel Sunday-trading must be greatly exaggerated,
because when he wanted liquor on a Sunday for a
sick friend he couldn't strike it till the eighth try.
The Bulletin should smile ! Bung understands
well enough the man who wants to wet his own
ungodly whistle, but the “sick friend” racket—■
he’s been there before !
Mrs. Ayer, recently dead in Paris, liad
long been pestered by needy notabilities with
offers of marriage—of course for the sake of the
colossal pile which her husband made out of
sarsaparilla and Pot. iodide. The bottle of stuff
which cost him about a dime, he used to vend at a
dollar—whacking that amount with the news-
paper-man for advertising. As in the N.S.W.
tea-business, that’s where the newspaper-man
comes in.

A Logan (South Q.) journal has struck a
patch of limitless possibilities. Inasmuch as
some of the gentlemen present at a local hop
complained that their names were not mentioned
in the local paper, the editor on the next oppor-
tunity compensated thusly : “ Mr. B. Bray, M.C.,
cream - colored pants, black vest, white shirt,
sleeves tucked up, black tie, and no coat; Mr. H.
Jones, sleeves tuckedup.no coat,” &c., &c. Of
course, there are many bush localities where
“ shirt and pants ” would have sufficed for a
costume-description of nine male dancers out of
ten. Clean shirt would have pourtrayed a dude
of the very first water.
A drunken old woman, out of gaol on the
previous Saturday, turned up at Brisbane (Q.)
police-court on the Thursday. Pa Pinnock felt
merciful: “ .£1 or 48 hours.” The old lady turned
to go to her 48 hours, but, looking over her
shoulder, and favoring Pinnock with a wonderful
wink, she said, “Ah, well, you’re not so bad this
morning, old man,” and wandered out. Pinnock’s
lower jaw dropped, his eyes looked into vacancy,
and the court became concerned. Suddenly, his
teeth came together with a snap, and he roared,
“Bring her back.’’ She was brought. “Your
penalty is altered from 48 hours to one month’s
imprisonment. Take her away.” Pinnock has
lost the chivalry of his youth. The wretched old
body only intended to pay him a compliment.
Seen on a day as hot as a furnace, Bernard’s
Swiss Studios, in King - street, Sydney, look
attractively cool and picturesque outside, and
within prove even more of a pleasant refuge than
they had promised. The Studios are the embodi-
ment of Mr. Bernard’s own ideas, and Sydney
ought to be thankful for the delightful in-
novation. The handsome reception - room is
reached by the easiest of stairs ; the ladies’ and
gentlemen’s dressing-rooms are perfectly lighted
and appointed ; but the gallery itself, with its
ladies’ dressing-rooms immediately alongside, is
very praiseworthy. It combines perfect lighting
with remarkable coolness. For this reason alone,
the Studios should become most popular with
ladies. Mr. Bernard intends to keep the price for
12 cabinets (two positions) down to 10s. 6d. ; and
visitors will not be “canvassed,” as they are in
some studios, but will receive every courtesy
instead. Nothing more artistic than the photo-
graphic work at these Studios is to be seen in
Sydney, and this fact can be very easily verified
by inspecting the handsome collection in the vesti-
bule. As the Studios are situated in the very
heart of Sydney, their business should boom.*
A Brisbane (Q.) artisan was out on his
own bail as a drunk. When his name was called
next morning, a pale but determined woman, hug-
ging to her breast a baby, pressed forward—“ Yer
Worsh’p, my husband’s gone to his work so as not
to lose his job, an’ I’ve come to take his place.
He pleads guilty, yer Worsh’p.” “Discharged,”
laconically said the P.M.

A Popular Sailing Enthusiast is Cured

PATENT MEDICINES UTTERLY FAIL.
BILE BEANS EFFECT A THOROUGH CURE.
One of the most popular and prominent enthusiasts in yachting is Mr. Joe Thiering, of Darling-
street, Balmain. He is one of the oldest members of the Johnstone’s Bay Sailing Club and the Flying
Squadron, in both of which he has taken an active part for a number of years. He has been sailing
in Sydney for 35 years, and his opinion on this grand sport is
regarded with great esteem. Although Mr. Thiering would be
taken for a strong, healthy man, it was learnt a few days ago
that he had suffered from severe Bilious Attacks, Headaches,
and Indigestion for a number of years. We give below a
letter received from Mr. Thiering stating that he has been com-
pletely cured of these ailments by Bile Beans. Mr. Thiering’s
letter reads as follows :
“ 189 Darling-st., Balmain.
“The Bile Bean Manufacturing Co.,
“ 19 Macquarie Place, Sydney.
“ Dear Sir, —Seeing your Bile Beans advertised, and being
a sufferer from Biliousness, Indigestion, and Constipation oc-
casionally, I decided to give them a trial, and I am pleased to
tell you that they cured me.
“ I was mostly troubled with Bilious Headaches and Indi-
gestion, which compelled me at times to leave off work. A
full feeling and pains across my chest often after my meals were
ailments that troubled me also. Since taking your Bile Beans all these complaints have disappeared,
and I. can now enjoy a good meal without feeling any bad after-effects. I have tried a good many
medicines advertised, but none seemed to give permanent re lief. My family say they are the best
medicine they, have ever taken. I am a blacksmith by trade, and am well known in Balmain. You
can publish this letter ; it may be the means of helping other sufferers.
“ Yours truly,
(Signed) “JOE THIERING.”
4.
Women’s Weakness.
While many of the delicate organs of a woman’s constitution need great attention, there is no
reason why a woman should suffer days of misery every month, when a little care would greatly
minimise, if not absolutely do away with, all pain. Many a woman makes herself a burden to her
husband and friends by her nervous irritability, caused through her own ignorance or neglect. It
stands to reason that a woman who suffers from disorder or weakness of one of the female organs
cannot make a happy, amiable helpmeet to an ambitious man. It is quite easy to keep all the organs
of the body in a healthy, vigorous state, with a little care and attention. Many remedies act in such
a violent manner that their use is more harmful than beneficial. Bile Beans act in a gentle, soothing
manner, and for any woman suffering from weakness of the female organs they are highly recom-
mended. They will probably prove efficient in cases where other remedies have failed, and a course
of them cannot prove otherwise than beneficial.

Working People-
“ With fingers weary and worn, with eyelids heavy and red,”
many a woman is pegging away at a machine or needle, struggling
to make two ends meet. The very nature of the work is apt to un-
dermine the health, and one of the persistent and almost inevitable
results is liver and stomach troubles. Bile Beans are a simple,
safe, and certain remedy that has stood the test of time, and when
used by women who lead a sedentary life, they will be found of
great service. These little Beans will cure all ills that come from
bad digestion and sluggish liver action.
