What Bile Beans Are.
w
FOS\
ILIOUSNESS
Bile Beans owe their existence to the discovery by an
American medical man of a certain vegetable substance
that has the effect on the digestive organs of Nature’s own
animal bile. This discovery is revolutionising all the present
methods of treatment for digestive ailments. The old theory
of pepsin, bismuth, starving, purging, &c., &c., is to be done
away with. This great vegetable substance is compounded
with other ingredients to facilitate the working, and to-day
this remedy can be purchased the world over and in Australia
under the name of Bile Beans for Biliousness. This great
specific possesses the peculiar properties of acting on the
bowels without purging or otherwise weakening the patient,
being so compounded that the substance that relaxes the
bowels has about the same action as the natural bile. Bile
Beans are manufactured in one of the largest and best-
equipped laboratories in the world at Detroit, Michigan,
TJ.S.A. They are sold throughout the world, and are an un-
doubted specific for Biliousness, Headache; Constipation, In-
digestion, Debility, Piles, Sallow Complexion, Female Weak-
ness, Dyspepsia, and All Liver Troubles. For sale at all chemists. Australian Depot, 19 Macquarie
Place, Sydney. Victorian Agents, Rocke, Tompsitb and Co., Flinders-street, Melbourne.
How M‘Dougal Topped the Score.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A peaceful spot is Piper’s Flat. The folk that live
around—
They keep themselves by keeping sheep and turn-
ing up the ground.
But the climate is erratic; and the consequences
are
The struggle with the elements is everlasting war.
We plough, and sow, and harrow —then sit down
and pray for rain ;
And then we all get flooded out and have to start
auain.
But the folk are now rejoicing as they ne’er re-
joiced before,
For we’ve played Molongo cricket, and M’Dougal
topped the score !
Molongo had a head on it, and challenged us to
play
A single-innings match for lunch—the losing team
to pay.
We were not great guns at cricket, but we couldn’t
well say No,
So we all began to practise, and we let the reaping
_ go ’
We scoured the flat for ten miles round to muster
up our men,
But when the list was totalled we could only
number ten.
Then up spoke big Tim Brady: he was always
slow to speak,
And he said —“ What price M‘Dougal, who lives
down at Cooper’s Creek ?
So we sent for old M‘Dougal, and he stated in
reply
That “ he’d never played at cricket, but he’d half
a mind to try.
He couldn’t come to practice—he was getting in
his hay,
But he guessed he’d show the beggars from
,Molongo how to play.”
Now, M'Dougal was a Scotchman, and a canny
-one at that,
So he started in to practise with a paling for a
bat.
He got Mrs. Mac. to bowl him, but she couldn’t
. run at all,
So he trained his sheep-dog, Pincher, how to
scout and fetch the ball.
Now, Pincher was no puppy; he was old, and
worn, and grey;
But lie understood M'Dougal, and —accustomed
to obey—
When M'Dougal cried out “ Fetch it! ”he would
fetch it, in a trice,
But,'until the word was “Drop it!” he would
gi ip it like a vyce.
And each succeeding night they played until the
lit: lit grew dim ;
Sometimes M'Dougal struck the ball —sometimes
the ball struck him !
Eaeh time he struck, the ball would plough a
furrow in the ground,
And when lie missed the impetus would turn him
three times round.
The fatal day at length arrived—the day that
was to see
Molongo bite the dust, or Piper’s Flat knocked up
a tree !
Molongo’s captain won the toss, and sent his men
to bat,
Did they gave some leather-hunting to the men
of I’iper’s Flat.
When the ball sped where M'Dougal stood, firm
planted in its track,
He shut his eyes, and turned him round, and
stopped it—with his back!
The highest score was twenty-two, the total sixty-
six,
When Brady sent a yorker down that scattered
Johnson’s sticks.
Then Piper’s Flat went in to bat, for glory and
renown,
But, like the grass before the scythe, our wickets
tumbled down.
“Nine wickets down for seventeen, with fifty
more to win ! ”
Our captain heaved a heavy sigh, and sent
M'Dougal in.
“Ten pounds to one you lose it! ” cried a barracker
from town ;
But M‘Dougal said “ I’ll tak’ it, mon ! ” and
planked the money down.
Then he girded up his moleskins in a self-reliant
style,
Threw off his hat and boots, and faced the bowler
with a smile.
He held the bat the wrong side out, and Johnson
with a grin
Stepped lightly to the bowling crease, and sent a
“wobbler” in;
M‘Dougal spooned it softly back, and Johnson
waited there,
But M’Dougal, crying “ Fetch it!” started run-
ning like a hare.
Molongo shouted ‘ ‘ Victory ! He’s out as sure as
eggs,”
When Pincher started through the crowd, and
ran through Johnson’s legs.
He seized the ball like lightning; then he ran
behind a log,
And M'Dougal kept on running, while Molongo
chased the dog.
They chased him up, they chased him down, they
chased him round, and then
He darted through a slip-rail as the scorer shouted
“ Ten! ”
M‘Dougal puffed; Molongo swore; excitement
was intense;
As the scorer marked down twenty,. Pincher
Cleared a barbed-wire fence.
“ Let us head him ! ” shrieked Molongo. “ Brain
the mongrel with a bat! ”
“Run it out! Good old McDougal!” yelled
the men of Piper’s Flat.
And McDougal kept on jogging, and then Pincher
doubled back,
And the scorer counted “ Forty ” as they raced
across the track.
McDougal’s legs were going fast, Molongo’s breath
was gone—
But still Molongo chased the dog—McDougal
struggled on.
When the scorer shouted “ Fifty ” then they knew
the chase could cease ;
And McDougal gasped out “Drop it!” as he
dropped within his crease.
Then Pincher dropped the ball, and as instinc-
tively he knew
Discretion was the wiser plan, he disappeared
from view.
And as Molongo’s beaten men exhausted lay
around
We raised McDougal shoulder-high, and bore him
from the ground.
We bore him to M‘Ginniss’s, where lunch was
ready laid,
And filled him up with whisky-punch, for which
Molongo paid.
We drank liis health in bumpers, and we cheered
him three times three,
And when Molongo got its breath, Molongo joined
the spree.
And the critics say they never saw a cricket-match
like that,
When M‘Dougal broke the record in the game at
Piper’s Flat.
And the folk are jubilating as they never were
before;
For we played Molongo cricket, and M‘Dougal
topped the score !
THOS E. SPENCER.
In a lecture at Brisbane, Q. Meteorologist
Wragge “ expressed his particular objection to
being called a ‘weather prophet,’ and explained
that his forecasts are formulated on strictly scien-
tific data, there being no trace of charlatanism
about his work.” Wragge has surrendered him-
self to the gibes of Philistia. Now that people
know that “weather prophet” hurts, “weather
prophet” it is certain to be, by all the gall in
human nature.
Thus “ Sirius,” somewhat condensed :
Ed. Bulletin. —Why abuse N.S.W. Governor Phillip as
a “hanging” official greedy for blood? Why overlook
the conditions by which he was surrounded, and ignore
his good points? Why can’t you be more generous?
That sneering, flippant style of yours just spoils what I
consider otherwise —[jam left to imagination..—Ed. B.]
We all like smartness and snap, bored to death as we are
with the monotony of the dailies. But then, my dear
fellow, there’s the other extreme, and, by George, you’ve
got there. Read Phillip’s life in N.S.W. and you’ll see he
was a man to be rather proud of, if you make proper
allowances for his training in the severe navy school, and
his position as the administrator of a code of Draconian
severity. But please recollect he didn’t make the code.
And why drop also on to Rev. Fish, who probably knows
nothing of the Phillip memorial lately erected at Sydney ?
It isn’t fair, Bulletin, and you know it as well as I do.
[The Bulletin doesn’t go out of its way to
“abuse” Phillip as a “hanging Governor”; it
simply calls attention to the fact that as “ the
administrator of a code of Draconian severity,”
Phillip is not a cheerful or desirable historical cir-
cumstance to recall. Phillip may have had good
points about him—very little is known of the
man one way or the other—but The Bulletin’s
position in the matter is due to a natural disgust
at the flunkey-souled people who can’t exist with-
out having always on hand a cheap hero of one
kind or another, and who are always shrieking to
have the public money expended on a statue to
somebody, for no better reason than that he never
did anything particularly disgraceful; or that
some politician or other wants to whack a com-
mission on the job. And The Bulletin, while
quite willing, for want of evidence to the contrary,
to admit that Phillip was a decent man of his
sort and time—a bad sort and a very horrible
time —finds nothing in all this to justify the
expenditure of (say) £14,000, not of individually-
subscribed, but of Treasury money, upon a monu-
ment to his memory which is neither a credit to
the community nor an ornament to one of the
champion sites of the world.]
“ There is, or was, a S.A. railway-ganger
named Finnigan, very fond of Bending long reports
of trivial occurrences. When he came to town,
the boss remonstrated. ‘Your reports are too
long, Finnigan. For heaven’s sake cut ’em short.’
The next report from Finnigan read': ‘Truck
All 6 off again, on again, gone again.— Finnigan.
P.S.—How’ll that do?’” [P.P.S.—An old Yankee
yarn ; but it will bear transplanting.]
A Wandering Australian : “ Whatever
strength lies behind the Carlist movement in
Spain the Madrid authorities do not attempt to
cloak their preparations for emergency. In the
main streets and outside the public buildings are
posted artillery commanding all the vantage
points. Carlist sympathisers say the gunners are
less trustworthy than the guns, and meanwhile
things float on in the restful Spanish way.
Parisian modes prevail here amongst the women,
who are superior in beauty to the Parisiennes;
the mantilla is only worn at church or at bull
fights, where the cow-killing is nearly as risky to
the killers as tree-felling in the mallee scrub.”
The suburban press is a fearsome institu-
tion, and sometimes achieves notable results. The
suburban press of Melbourne all but destroyed
the Government and bankrupted Victoria at the
beginning of the sittings of tlie third session of
the Federal Convention. It was a Sydney scribe
who made the discovery. He wandered non-
chalantly into the refreshment-room at Melb.
House of Assembly and called for whisky-and-
soda, tendering a shilling. The coin was refused,
and it was painfully explained to him that the
Govt, had resolved to treat the press engaged
during the sittings of the Convention purely as
guests. Everything was to be free. The indis-
creet journalist mentioned the fact to himself in
the arctic privacy of his own room, and the news
went forth. Browning’s rats were nothing to it.
The pressmen swarmed up from cellar and down
from attic. Forgotten scribes of past decades
emerged from their Caves of Adullam ; dipso-
maniacal scribblers gulped buckets of chloride of
gold and hurried to the scene of conflict; the very
churchyards yawned, and dead journalists gathered
their bones together and rushed to represent at
the Convention papers published in Hades. And
the Government acted right royally—for one
week. There was no .stint’ in eating.. or
drinking, or smoking. The tables absolutely
murmured at the wealth of Ormuz and of Ind
piled thereon. There were oysters from Britain,
lampreys from the fish-pools of Lucullus, larks’-
tongues from the aviaries of Claudius Csesar, tur-
bot from the larder of Brillat-Savarin’s friend, the
Italian Cardinal, sturgeon from Norway, frogs
from M&con, and caviare from the General. The
rarest vintages of France were at the disposal of
ragged paragraphists ; cigars of the most fragrant,
appallingly Cuban brands were provided, and
gold-tipped cigarettes, at five shillings a dozen,
were pocketed by the handful. But when some 70
scribes scrambled for fish costing three guineas a
plate, and arrogantly refused to ! be denied ; when
they poured champagne into the finger-glasses,
and lit big aromatic cigars with gilt-edged menus,
and carried home roast birds-of-paradise for the
regalement of the landlady, the Government
sadly, but firmly, put its foot down, and an-
nounced, more in sorrow than in anger, that the
orgy was over and the Convention’s free counter-
lunch closed down. As it was expressly explained
to one complaining journalistic dead-beat by the
official in charge: “No Government could stand
it. Why, if we went on like this Victoria would
be up the spout within a fortnight. As it is ! ’

Cripples All!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There's a jaunty generation that goes limping
thro’ the land,
And it calls itself a nation, and it bids its chest
" expand ;
It grows fat with satisfaction on a pile of borrowed
gold,
And it droops in dull inaction, tho’ its tongue is
triple-bold ;
It’s a race that’s fond of wailing of the yoke it has
to bear ;
It’s a race that’s always failing when it has to do
and dare ;
It’s a race of bending backbones tho’ its talk is
stiff and tall;
It’s a race of men on crutches, and we dub them
Cripples All!
There’s a crew of cringing creatures that go always
cap in hand,
And their meek, submissive features bear the milky
craven brand ;
They’ve a pulse that’s always steady when the
good man meets a fall;
They’ve a right arm ever ready when the Lords of
Money call;
They’ve a God above—they fear Him and they love
Him (so they say),
They’ve a devil and they jeer him, for the Devil’s
had his day ;
Never front they give a foeman—never breast they
give a brawl —
They’re a crowd that go on crutches —and we dub
them Cripples All!
There’s a tribe all bent and pallid, who by lying
lip and pen,
Daily prove the Deed invalid that asserted they
were men :
Tribe whose tongue is ever supple, and whose ink
is never dry,
Who’ve a front of brass quadruple and an ever-
shifty eye;
And ’tis theirs to sing the paean when Sir Midas
sweeps along,
Theirs to hail the gilded scion of a harlot with a
song,
Theirs to fight beneath the table with the dogs of
Dives' hall,
Till they totter home on crutches —like a band of
Cripples All !
There’s a troop of vot’ries fearful all a-crouch be-
neath a creed,
There are Women-faces tearful (are there men in
such a breed ?),
And a tale of driv’lling Ages by a People’s dotage
bred
All the puny mind encages till the puny soul ia
dead,
Till a priestly incantation is the People’s chiefest
trust,
Till they kneel, a nidd’ring nation, to a Deity of
Dust,
Till a symbol, void of virtue, holds the bending
throng in thrall—
Now —what are they with their crutches but a
band of Cripples All?
And the knaves who flag and falter when they
bear the battle-drum,
And the puling gang who palter when the clarion
bids them “ Come ! ”
And the fatted fools who barter on the highways
day by day
All the freeman’s glorious charter for a bauble
cheap and gay,
And the timid ones who tremble at the things upon
the throne,
And the dastards who dissemble till the blast be
overblown,
And the Little Men of Little Soul whom Little
Things appal—
Now, what are they with their crutches but a band
of Cripples All ?
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.
A Woman’s Reason.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Yes,” said the doctor, thoughtfully, “I
have had cases of some psychological,, interest,
though not exactly the kind given as doctors’
experiences in well-bred English magazines. Here
is one. It starts off like an old story, but—;
however, you will see for yourself. As I was say-
ing a moment ago, women are . Never mind,
you can pick out the moral later on.”
* * * *
I was leaving my consulting-room one
cold afternoon when, anticipating the servant’s
announcement, my old school friend, James
Hutchison, came hurriedly into the apartment. I
gave him a hearty welcome. I had just returned
from a holiday tour ; consequently had not seen
him for several months. That period, so full of
pleasure for me, had brought him bitter experi-
ence. However, that was now a thing of the past.
Some new trouble ? He flung down his hat, and
threw himself on a chair. “Ned, old fellow, I’ve
come to you about my wife.”
His voice was hoarse and strained. I could not
help noticing how old and grey he looked ; but
that was natural, very natural, after what he had
undergone.
“Come, Jim,” reassuringly; “ your wife is in
good hands. Why, I heard, coming back in the
train, how well she was looking; and, even if
there is a slight relapse ”
He interrupted me with some impatience.
“ She’s quite well—bodily. You heard all about
it then ? ”
“ Of her terrible sufferings—lost in the bush —
and her narrow escape from death by starvation ?
Why, the newspapers were full of it, Jim ! And
didn’t I wire my congratulations when I heard
that they had found her at last ? ”
“All my fault,” he muttered, unheeding. “I
left her too much alone. You know how it is
with us men, Ned. It’s not that we love our
wives less; it’s only that we forget to speak of
it; and they—they don’t understand.”
I poured him out a glass of wine. “ Maybe I’m
prejudiced, old fellow, but it always struck me
you made a capital husband.”
“ Do I ? ” with a sudden, unsteady laugh. He
drank the liquor at one draught. “ Not half
such a bad sort, am I ? Eh ? Look at me ? Not
much deteriorated since I married Nellie, three
years ago ? If I went courting again, courting a
young, delicate creature like Nellie, I’d have a
chance, eh? . . . No, I’m not mad, Ned, or
drunk. I’ve had to go courting again these last
two months, courting Nellie, my wife; because—
she’s forgotten me, forgotten everything that
happened before her awful experience, forgotten
me, her husband.”
After a little he was able to explain the matter
at length. Mrs. Hutchison was in no sense in-
sane. Simply a sponge had been passed over the
whole life anterior to the illness that followed her
wanderings in the bush. She had had to learn
everything over again. Hutchison grew calm and
even interested as he mentioned the things she
could do, as it were instinctively, and the other
things, reading and writing for instance, which
she was painfully acquiring anew. He had hoped
that this mental affection would prove only tem-
porary, but that now seemed vain. She had con-
sented, after much persuasion, to avoid scandal
by remaining in her husband’s house. He was
full of gratitude to her for her courage.
“ But can’t I see how dreadful it is for her, my
poor darling,” he muttered brokenly, “it’s like
living in the house alone with a stranger. She
must address as her husband and be, at least
before others, on terms of intimacy with a man,
who is, as far as she can realise, a mere two-
months’ acquaintance. I’m as kind to her as I
can be, but it’s like a knife to see her shrink away
if I only touch her hand ; and I’ve got to be a
gentleman to her,” with a hysterical laugh, “when
I want to be a husband.”
He pulled himself together with an effort. Such
emotion was unusual to his strong nature.
A slight pause, and he looked towards me again.
“ You will come ? ” he questioned ; “ you will tell
me if you see any chance of—of ”
“ Of memory returning ? Frankly ”
“ No, no ! ’’—impatiently. He raised his hand
as if to hide the slow flush on his rugged face—
“ any chance of her loving me again.”
Ten minutes later we had left the house, and
half an hour found us ushered into the pretty
drawing-room of young Mrs. Hutchison. Never
had the red firelight shone on a more charming
face and figure as she rose languidly from her low
chair to welcome us. One of Nature’s butterflies—
I had already classified her brainless and beautiful
—with soft, appealing eyes, doubly appealing now.
Though I had met her so often before, a new
introduction was necessary. She greeted me with
a little natural restraint; her husband with a
pathetic mingling of timidity and dawning affec-
tion. I thought Jim’s chances particularly good.
I stayed an hour chatting to both. She-was
nervous and low-spirited at first, feeling her
affliction keenly, but presently brightened up.
Once, when the rain began to fall, I went to the
window to look out. When I turned, Jim had
taken the seat next his wife, and both heads were
bent over a book. Mrs. Hutchison had been look-
ing at the illustrations, and now Jim was helping
her to spell out the letterpress. Presently I saw
him gently put his arm round her waist. She
drew back at first, then yielded, and suffered him
to press her head against his shoulder. Jim
glanced at me with a smile of tremulous happi-
ness. She followed his glance and blushed deeply.
“ Jim is teaching me to read,” she said, “such
an interesting story. Fancy, I’ve got to wait
till he comes home every evening to see what
happens next.”
Her childishness made us both laugh. It re-
lieved us to see that she could still be happy.
A little while later, Jim slipped from the room.
With an apology to my hostess I quickly followed
him. As I spoke the words of excuse I saw her
give a slight start. The room was now darkening,
and for a moment she must have fancied herself
quite alone. I found Jim in the hall, looking for
something that obviously was not there. He
lifted a flushed, happy face at my approach.
“ Well,” he said huskily ; “ well ? ”
I did not heed the implied question.
“Jim, my friend,” I said quietly ; “I’ve got a
plan to restore your wife’s memory. Just stay
here, and let me speak to her a few minutes.”
Without waiting for an answer, I re-entered.
Mrs. Hutchison was standing by the mantel-
piece. One black, slippered foot caressed the
fender. She was humming a tender little love-
song. Her face was radiant.
“ Well, Mrs. Hutchison,” I advanced towards
her. “ When is this farce to end ? ”
Her face changed : “ What—farce ? ”
“ This infamous deception of a good husband,
if you like it better.”
I expected some denial. She capitulated at
once.
“ Oh, don’t tell him,” she whispered. In her
agitation her voice seemed to desert her. “He
would never forgive me, never speak to me again.
Oh ! how—how did you ever find me out ? ”
“ Perhaps it was that hasty glance at the last
page of the novel when you thought no one wai
looking. Anyway ”
“ But you won’t tell him, will you, and break
his heart, dear doctor ? ” Her slim fingers touched
my arm. “I’m not deceitful, really, doctor. I
didn’t get lost in the bush on purpose,” she went
on in her pretty silly way. “ The idea came to
me when I found myself safe at home, and for a
little while really had forgotten. And,” here my
shoulder was deserted, the hands being wanted to
manipulate a delicate lace handkerchief ; “ and so
I thought I’d just pretend and keep it up, and—
and ”
“But what did you do it for?” I looked at
her in amazement —such a weak, hysterical little
creature, so evidently meant for pettings and
caressings, and for nothing deeper.
The handkerchief was rolled into a wet little
ball. She gave a final sob.
“ Jim was getting so cold, so silent,” she said ;
“and so I thought I would enjoy my courting
over again ! ”
M.L.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
A daring correspondent :
Among 1 the Lenten pastorals in Victoria, that of the
Bishop of Bendigo seizes most upon me, with divorce as
its burning question. Marriage a sacrament, and no
divorce, is a sheet-anchor of the Roman Church. It strikes
me there ought to be some kind of legal eoncul inage—
which would be entirely Scriptural. The Word only im-
poses its awful sanctions on marriage. Facility of divorce
impairs the value of every marriage, and people enter on
the sacred relationship with a determination that it shall
only be a weatherboard habitation.
According to Macdonald-Paterson, senior
M. for Brisbane North (Q.), who has repre-
sented that capital for nearly a year by touring in
Britain and elsewhere, the best meat on the
English market, superior even to Scotch or
English beef, is—American. He seems to say
nothing at all about Australian.
